│   │       │   │       ├── **That-clause:** "**sure** **that** **he** **will** **come**"
│   │       │   │       ├── **Gerund:** "**afraid** **of** **flying**" / "**good** **at** **teaching**"
│   │       │   │       └── **Noun phrase:** "**an** **hour** **long**" / "**worth** **a** **fortune**"
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **(3) Types of APs**
│   │       │   │   ├── **Gradable APs (can be intensified):**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**very** **beautiful**" / "**quite** **interesting**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**extremely** **difficult**" / "**rather** **cold**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── Allow comparative and superlative forms
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Non-gradable/Absolute APs:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**unique**" / "**perfect**" / "**dead**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "*very perfect" / "*quite dead" (unacceptable)
│   │       │   │   │   └── Cannot be intensified
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Comparative APs:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**more** **beautiful** **than** **before**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**taller** **than** **his** **brother**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── Compare two entities
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Superlative APs:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**the** **most** **beautiful**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**the** **tallest**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── Indicate highest degree
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   └── **Predicative APs (follow copula):**
│   │       │   │       ├── "**She** **is** **beautiful**." / "**They** **seem** **happy**."
│   │       │   │       ├── "**It** **became** **clear**." / "**The** **weather** **turned** **cold**."
│   │       │   │       └── Express state or quality
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   └── **(4) Functions of APs**
│   │       │       ├── **Predicative (with copula):** "She **is** [**very** **beautiful**]."
│   │       │       ├── **Attributive (modifying noun):** "a [**very** **beautiful**] **woman**"
│   │       │       ├── **Postpositive (after noun, uncommon):** "a woman [**beautiful**]" (poetic/archaic)
│   │       │       └── **Predicate adjective:** "**She** **became** **[happy]**."
│   │       │
│   │       ├── **D. d. Adverb Phrase (AdvP)**
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **(1) Basic Structure**
│   │       │   │   ├── **Minimum AdvP:** Adverb only
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**quickly**" / "**yesterday**" / "**here**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── "He spoke **quickly**."
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **AdvP with modifiers:** Intensifier + Adverb
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**very** **quickly**" / "**quite** **slowly**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**extremely** **carefully**" / "**rather** **softly**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── "**almost** **immediately**"
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   └── **Extended AdvP:** Intensifier + Adverb + Complement
│   │       │   │       ├── "**very** **quickly** **enough**"
│   │       │   │       ├── "**quite** **apart** **from** **the** **main** **issue**"
│   │       │   │       └── "**almost** **throughout** **the** **entire** **day**"
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **(2) Constituent Elements**
│   │       │   │   ├── **Degree modifier (optional)**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Intensifiers:** "**very**", "**extremely**", "**incredibly**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Downtoners:** "**quite**", "**rather**", "**somewhat**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Approximators:** "**almost**", "**practically**", "**nearly**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── "**much** **more** **quickly**" / "**far** **better**"
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Head Adverb (obligatory)**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Manner adverbs:** "quickly", "carefully", "beautifully"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Time adverbs:** "yesterday", "soon", "now"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Place adverbs:** "here", "there", "upstairs"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Frequency adverbs:** "often", "rarely", "never"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Degree adverbs:** "very", "quite", "extremely"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Sentence adverbs:** "Clearly", "Frankly", "Fortunately"
│   │       │   │   │   └── **Focuser adverbs:** "only", "even", "also"
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   └── **Complements/Modifiers**
│   │       │   │       ├── **Prepositional phrase:** "apart **from** **the** **main** **issue**"
│   │       │   │       ├── **Comparison:** "more **quickly** **than** **before**"
│   │       │   │       └── "enough **time**" / "**as** **quickly** **as** **possible**"
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **(3) Types of AdvPs**
│   │       │   │   ├── **Manner AdvPs:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**quickly**" / "**carefully**" / "**softly**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**very** **quickly**" / "**quite** **beautifully**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── Describe how action is performed
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Time AdvPs:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**yesterday**" / "**soon**" / "**today**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**in** **the** **morning**" / "**throughout** **the** **week**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── Indicate when action occurs
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Place AdvPs:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**here**" / "**there**" / "**upstairs**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**in** **the** **corner**" / "**near** **the** **station**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── Indicate where action occurs
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Frequency AdvPs:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**often**" / "**rarely**" / "**never**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**every** **day**" / "**once** **a** **week**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── Indicate how often action occurs
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Degree AdvPs:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**very**" / "**quite**" / "**extremely**"
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**almost**" / "**nearly**" / "**practically**"
│   │       │   │   │   └── Modify adjectives and adverbs
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Sentence AdvPs:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**Clearly**, she was upset."
│   │       │   │   │   ├── "**Frankly**, I don't agree." / "**Fortunately**, he arrived."
│   │       │   │   │   └── Modify entire clause
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   └── **Focuser AdvPs:**
│   │       │   │       ├── "**only** **five** **minutes**" / "**even** **she** **came**"
│   │       │   │       ├── "**also** **interested**" / "**as** **well** **as**"
│   │       │   │       └── Emphasize or focus particular element
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   └── **(4) Functions of AdvPs**
│   │       │       ├── **Modifying verbs:** "He **ran** [**very** **quickly**]."
│   │       │       ├── **Modifying adjectives:** "She **is** [**extremely** **beautiful**]."
│   │       │       ├── **Modifying adverbs:** "[**very** **quickly**] **indeed**"
│   │       │       ├── **Modifying clauses:** "[**Frankly**], I don't agree."
│   │       │       └── **Modifying prepositional phrases:** "almost **in** **the** **center**"
│   │       │
│   │       └── **E. e. Prepositional Phrase (PP)**
│   │           │
│   │           ├── **(1) Basic Structure**
│   │           │   ├── **Minimum PP:** Preposition + NP
│   │           │   │   ├── "**in** **the** **morning**" / "**on** **the** **table**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "**under** **the** **bridge**" / "**with** **care**"
│   │           │   │   └── "**at** **home**" / "**for** **you**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Extended PP:** Preposition + Modified NP
│   │           │   │   ├── "**in** **the** **[beautiful** **green**] **garden**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "**on** **the** **[large** **wooden**] **table** **[in** **the** **corner**]"
│   │           │   │   └── "**with** **[great** **care**] **and** **[complete** **attention**]"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   └── **Nested/Embedded PP:** PP within NP within PP
│   │           │       ├── "**in** **the** **[house** **on** **the** **hill**]**"
│   │           │       ├── "**with** **the** **[girl** **in** **the** **red** **dress**]**"
│   │           │       └── "**by** **the** **[man** **from** **the** **city**]**"
│   │           │
│   │           ├── **(2) Constituent Elements**
│   │           │   ├── **Preposition (obligatory)**
│   │           │   │   ├── **Simple prepositions (one word):**
│   │           │   │   │   ├── **Spatial:** "in", "on", "at", "by", "near", "beside"
│   │           │   │   │   ├── **Direction:** "to", "from", "toward", "away from"
│   │           │   │   │   ├── **Temporal:** "during", "after", "before", "since"
│   │           │   │   │   ├── **Causal:** "for", "because of", "due to"
│   │           │   │   │   ├── **Means/Manner:** "with", "by", "through"
│   │           │   │   │   ├── **Possession:** "of"
│   │           │   │   │   ├── **Agent (passive):** "by"
│   │           │   │   │   ├── **Accompaniment:** "with", "without"
│   │           │   │   │   └── **Comparison:** "like", "as"
│   │           │   │   │
│   │           │   │   └── **Complex prepositions (more than one word):**
│   │           │   │       ├── "in front of" / "in back of" / "beside"
│   │           │   │       ├── "because of" / "due to" / "owing to"
│   │           │   │       ├── "in addition to" / "in comparison with"
│   │           │   │       ├── "on top of" / "in the middle of"
│   │           │   │       └── "with regard to" / "instead of"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   └── **Object of preposition (obligatory NP/DP)**
│   │           │       ├── **Pronoun:** "**of** **me**" / "**to** **him**"
│   │           │       ├── **Noun:** "**on** **the** **table**" / "**in** **the** **morning**"
│   │           │       ├── **Modified noun:** "**in** **the** **beautiful** **garden**"
│   │           │       ├── **Gerund:** "**after** **leaving**" / "**before** **arriving**"
│   │           │       ├── **Noun clause:** "**in** **what** **manner**" / "**about** **whether** **he** **came**"
│   │           │       └── **Pro-form (some registers):** "**of** **which**" / "**to** **whom**"
│   │           │
│   │           ├── **(3) Types of PPs by Function**
│   │           │   ├── **Spatial/Locative PPs (where?):**
│   │           │   │   ├── **Location:** "**in** **the** **house**" / "**at** **school**"
│   │           │   │   ├── **Position:** "**on** **the** **table**" / "**under** **the** **bed**"
│   │           │   │   ├── **Distance:** "**near** **the** **station**" / "**close** **to** **me**"
│   │           │   │   └── **Spatial relation:** "**beside** **the** **river**" / "**in** **front** **of** **the** **building**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Temporal PPs (when? / how long?):**
│   │           │   │   ├── **Point in time:** "**at** **noon**" / "**on** **Monday**"
│   │           │   │   ├── **Duration:** "**for** **three** **hours**" / "**during** **the** **summer**"
│   │           │   │   ├── **Period:** "**in** **the** **morning**" / "**at** **night**"
│   │           │   │   ├── **Sequence:** "**after** **breakfast**" / "**before** **noon**"
│   │           │   │   └── **Starting point:** "**since** **yesterday**" / "**from** **Monday**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Manner/Instrumental PPs (how? / with what?):**
│   │           │   │   ├── "**with** **care**" / "**by** **bus**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "**through** **hard** **work**" / "**with** **great** **enthusiasm**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "**in** **a** **whisper**" / "**with** **a** **smile**"
│   │           │   │   └── "**by** **hand**" / "**without** **effort**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Causal/Reason PPs (why? / because of what?):**
│   │           │   │   ├── "**for** **this** **reason**" / "**out** **of** **fear**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "**because** **of** **the** **rain**" / "**due** **to** **circumstances**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "**from** **exhaustion**" / "**of** **necessity**"
│   │           │   │   └── "**on** **account** **of** **the** **delay**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Purpose PPs (for what? / for what purpose?):**
│   │           │   │   ├── "**for** **education**" / "**for** **fun**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "**for** **the** **purpose** **of** **learning**"
│   │           │   │   └── "**in** **order** **to** **help**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Possessive/Attributive PPs (whose? / of what?):**
│   │           │   │   ├── "**of** **John**" / "**of** **my** **friend**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "book **of** **the** **student**" / "house **of** **blue** **stones**"
│   │           │   │   └── "**of** **great** **importance**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Benefactive PPs (for whom? / for whose benefit?):**
│   │           │   │   ├── "**for** **her**" / "**for** **the** **children**"
│   │           │   │   └── "**for** **the** **good** **of** **all**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Accompaniment PPs (with whom? / with what else?):**
│   │           │   │   ├── "**with** **my** **friend**" / "**with** **her** **sister**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "**with** **great** **style**" / "**without** **hesitation**"
│   │           │   │   └── "**together** **with** **him**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   └── **Agent PPs (by whom? - in passive constructions):**
│   │           │       ├── "**by** **John**" / "**by** **the** **teacher**"
│   │           │       ├── "**by** **the** **wind**" / "**by** **scientists**"
│   │           │       └── "**by** **his** **parents**"
│   │           │
│   │           ├── **(4) Functions of PPs in Sentences**
│   │           │   ├── **Adjectival modifier (modifies noun):**
│   │           │   │   ├── "the book **on** **the** **shelf**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "the girl **in** **red**"
│   │           │   │   └── "ideas **of** **great** **importance**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Adverbial modifier (modifies verb/clause):**
│   │           │   │   ├── "He spoke **with** **care**."
│   │           │   │   ├── "She arrived **at** **dawn**."
│   │           │   │   └── "They met **near** **the** **station**."
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Verb complement (required by verb):**
│   │           │   │   ├── "depend **on** **luck**" / "listen **to** **music**"
│   │           │   │   ├── "look **for** **answers**" / "deal **with** **problems**"
│   │           │   │   └── "succeed **in** **the** **attempt**"
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   ├── **Predicate complement (after copula):**
│   │           │   │   ├── "He **is** **in** **the** **room**."
│   │           │   │   ├── "The book **is** **on** **the** **table**."
│   │           │   │   └── "She **remained** **in** **the** **background**."
│   │           │   │
│   │           │   └── **Modifier of adjective/adverb:**
│   │           │       ├── "independent **of** **others**" / "aware **of** **the** **danger**"
│   │           │       ├── "different **from** **usual**" / "similar **to** **the** **previous**"
│   │           │       └── "differently **from** **before**"
│   │           │
│   │           └── **(5) Constraints and Patterns**
│   │               ├── **Preposition stranding:**
│   │               │   ├── **Allowed in some varieties:** "What did she depend **on**?"
│   │               │   ├── **Preferred in formal register:** "**On** what did she depend?"
│   │               │   └── End-position preposition considered informal by prescriptivists
│   │               │
│   │               ├── **Preposition choice constraints:**
│   │               │   ├── **Semantic compatibility:** Some verbs require specific prepositions
│   │               │   ├── "depend **on**" / "*depend to**" / "*depend **for**"
│   │               │   ├── "listen **to**" / "*listen **for**" (less common with "for")
│   │               │   └── "look **at**" / "look **for**" (different meanings)
│   │               │
│   │               ├── **PP stacking (multiple modifying PPs):**
│   │               │   ├── "the book **on** **the** **table** **in** **the** **corner**"
│   │               │   ├── "he **spoke** **with** **care** **in** **the** **morning**"
│   │               │   └── Right-branching structure
│   │               │
│   │               └── **PP attachment ambiguity:**
│   │                   ├── "I saw the man **with** the telescope." (with = using / with = accompanying)
│   │                   ├── "Students **of** linguistics **from** America" (ambiguous scope)
│   │                   └── Context resolves ambiguity
│   │
│   ├── **B. Sentence Classification by Structure**
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **1. Simple Sentence**
│   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Contains ONE independent clause and NO dependent clauses
│   │   │   ├── **Structure:** [Subject] + [Predicate] (can have compound subjects or compound predicates, but still ONE clause)
│   │   │   ├── **Key Point:** "Simple" refers to clause structure, NOT sentence length
│   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   ├── "**The cat sat on the mat.**" (basic: S + V + complement)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**Birds sing in the morning.**" (S + V + adverbial)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**Alice and Bob played chess.**" (compound subject, but ONE clause)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**She walked quickly and disappeared.**" (compound predicate, but ONE clause)
│   │   │   │   └── "**The tall, elegant woman with red hair drove the expensive silver car very fast down the winding mountain road.**" (long but SIMPLE - ONE independent clause with modifiers)
│   │   │   └── **Analysis:** All are simple sentences because they each contain only ONE independent clause
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **2. Compound Sentence**
│   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Contains TWO or MORE independent clauses joined by coordinating conjunctions
│   │   │   ├── **Joining Methods:**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Coordinating Conjunctions (FANBOYS):** for, and, nor, but, or, yet, so
│   │   │   │   │   └── Pattern: [Independent Clause] **CONJ** [Independent Clause]
│   │   │   │   ├── **Semicolon (;):** Joins independent clauses without conjunction
│   │   │   │   │   └── Pattern: [Independent Clause] **;** [Independent Clause]
│   │   │   │   ├── **Conjunctive Adverb:** however, therefore, moreover, nevertheless, etc.
│   │   │   │   │   └── Pattern: [Independent Clause] **;** [Conjunctive Adverb] [Independent Clause]
│   │   │   │   └── **Colon (rare):** Can join independent clauses if second explains first
│   │   │   │       └── Pattern: [Independent Clause] **:** [Independent Clause]
│   │   │   ├── **Key Point:** All clauses are EQUAL in importance (coordinate, not subordinate)
│   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   ├── "**I studied hard, and I passed the exam.**" (two independent clauses + conjunction)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**The rain fell; the birds hid.**" (two independent clauses + semicolon)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**She wanted to leave, but he insisted on staying.**" (two independent clauses + 'but')
│   │   │   │   ├── "**He arrived late; however, she waited patiently.**" (two independent clauses + conjunctive adverb)
│   │   │   │   └── "**Alice prepared the presentation, Bob created the slides, and Charlie handled the projector.**" (three independent clauses)
│   │   │   └── **⚠️ Common Error:** Using comma alone to join independent clauses = COMMA SPLICE ✘
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **3. Complex Sentence**
│   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Contains ONE independent clause + ONE or MORE dependent clauses
│   │   │   ├── **Structure Patterns:**
│   │   │   │   ├── **[Dependent] [Independent]** - subordinate clause first
│   │   │   │   ├── **[Independent] [Dependent]** - subordinate clause last
│   │   │   │   ├── **[Independent] [Dependent] [Independent]** - embedded dependent clause
│   │   │   │   └── **[Dependent] [Independent] [Dependent]** - multiple subordinate clauses
│   │   │   ├── **Types of Dependent Clauses:**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Noun Clause:** functions as noun (subject, object, complement)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Adjective/Relative Clause:** modifies a noun or pronoun
│   │   │   │   └── **Adverbial Clause:** modifies verb, adjective, adverb, or entire clause
│   │   │   ├── **Key Point:** ONE main clause is most important; others support/modify it
│   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   ├── "**When she arrived, we started the meeting.**" (adverbial + independent)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**I believe **that he is honest**.**" (independent + noun clause)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**The student **who studied hard** passed the exam.**" (independent with embedded relative clause)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**Although it was raining, the children played outside because they were excited.**" (two dependent + one independent)
│   │   │   │   └── "**If you study diligently, you will succeed in whatever you attempt.**" (dependent + independent + noun clause)
│   │   │   └── **Analysis:** Each has one main idea with supporting/modifying clauses
│   │   │
│   │   └── **4. Compound-Complex Sentence**
│   │       ├── **Definition:** Contains TWO or MORE independent clauses AND ONE or MORE dependent clauses
│   │       ├── **Structure Pattern:** Combines characteristics of both compound and complex
│   │       │   └── [Independent + Dependent] **CONJ** [Independent + Dependent]
│   │       ├── **Identifying Feature:** Must have:
│   │       │   ├── ✔ At least 2 independent clauses (makes it COMPOUND)
│   │       │   ├── ✔ At least 1 dependent clause (makes it COMPLEX)
│   │       │   └── = COMPOUND-COMPLEX overall
│   │       ├── **Examples:**
│   │       │   ├── "**When she arrived, we started the meeting, and we finished early.**"
│   │       │   │   └── (dependent: "When she arrived" + independent: "we started" + independent: "we finished")
│   │       │   ├── "**Although the weather was terrible, the team won their game, and fans celebrated.**"
│   │       │   │   └── (dependent: "Although..." + independent: "team won" + independent: "fans celebrated")
│   │       │   ├── "**If you work hard, you will succeed; however, luck helps too.**"
│   │       │   │   └── (dependent: "If you work hard" + independent: "you will succeed" + independent: "luck helps")
│   │       │   └── "**Because inflation rose, prices increased, and consumers suffered.**"
│   │       │       └── (dependent: "Because inflation rose" + independent: "prices increased" + independent: "consumers suffered")
│   │       └── **⚠️ Note:** Compound-complex sentences are high-level structures; often seen in formal academic writing
│   │
│   ├── **C. Sentence Force (by Function)**
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **1. Declarative (Statement)**
│   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Makes a statement or assertion about a fact, belief, or opinion
│   │   │   ├── **Function:** To inform, explain, describe, or present information
│   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Standard word order: [Subject] [Verb] [Object/Complement]
│   │   │   ├── **Punctuation:** Ends with **period (.)** or sometimes **exclamation mark (!)** if emphatic
│   │   │   ├── **Intonation:** Falls at the end (in speech); relatively flat
│   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   ├── "**The sun rises in the east.**" (factual statement)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**Shakespeare was a brilliant playwright.**" (opinion)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**She has been studying for three hours.**" (present perfect)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**If you practice daily, you will improve.**" (conditional statement)
│   │   │   │   └── "**We should always be kind to others!**" (emphatic declarative)
│   │   │   └── **Most Common:** Declaratives make up the majority of English sentences
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **2. Interrogative (Question)**
│   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Seeks information or asks a question
│   │   │   ├── **Function:** To request information, clarification, or confirmation
│   │   │   ├── **Structure Types:**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Yes/No Questions (Polar Questions):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Word order: [Auxiliary] [Subject] [Main Verb]
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Do you like pizza?**"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Have they finished their work?**"
│   │   │   │   │   └── "**Will you come tomorrow?**"
│   │   │   │   ├── **Wh- Questions (Open Questions):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Word order: [Wh-word] [Auxiliary] [Subject] [Main Verb]
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**What time is it?**"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Where did you go?**"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Why have they left?**"
│   │   │   │   │   └── "**How many books have you read?**"
│   │   │   │   ├── **Tag Questions (Echo Questions):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Structure: [Statement] **[Tag with opposite polarity]**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**You like coffee, don't you?**" (positive statement → negative tag)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**She hasn't arrived, has she?**" (negative statement → positive tag)
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Function:** Seeks confirmation; expects agreement
│   │   │   │   └── **Rhetorical Questions:**
│   │   │   │       ├── "**Don't we all want to be happy?**" (not really asking; making a point)
│   │   │   │       ├── "**Who doesn't love chocolate?**" (implied answer: everyone does)
│   │   │   │       └── **Function:** Makes a statement in question form; expects no answer
│   │   │   ├── **Punctuation:** Ends with **question mark (?)**
│   │   │   ├── **Intonation:** Rises at the end for yes/no questions; varied for wh- questions
│   │   │   └── **Answer Options:** Can be answered with yes/no, specific information, or understanding
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **3. Imperative (Command/Request)**
│   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Gives a command, makes a request, or provides instructions
│   │   │   ├── **Function:** To direct action, issue orders, give advice, or make requests
│   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Usually **omits subject** (implied "you"); [Verb] [Object/Complement]
│   │   │   ├── **Types:**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Direct Command (formal/authoritative):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Close the door!**" (strong command)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Stop immediately!**" (urgent order)
│   │   │   │   │   └── "**Get out of here!**" (forceful)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Polite Request:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Please pass the salt.**" (polite request)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Would you mind closing the window?**" (very polite question-form request)
│   │   │   │   │   └── "**Could you help me, please?**" (indirect polite request)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Instructions/Directions:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Mix the ingredients together.**"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Turn left at the traffic light.**"
│   │   │   │   │   └── "**Apply the cream evenly.**"
│   │   │   │   └── **Advice/Suggestions:**
│   │   │   │       ├── "**Take care of yourself.**" (advice)
│   │   │   │       ├── "**Try calling him again.**" (suggestion)
│   │   │   │       └── "**Don't forget to submit the assignment.**" (negative advice)
│   │   │   ├── **Punctuation:** Ends with **period (.) or exclamation mark (!)**; exclamation for strong commands
│   │   │   ├── **Intonation:** Varies from calm (requests) to urgent (commands)
│   │   │   ├── **Special Features:**
│   │   │   │   ├── Can have explicit subject: "**You be quiet!**" (for emphasis)
│   │   │   │   ├── Can address specific person: "**John, come here.**"
│   │   │   │   └── First-person plural: "**Let's go!**" / "**Let us leave.**"
│   │   │   └── **No Answer Expected:** Imperatives do not require a response (unlike questions)
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **4. Optative (Wish/Desire)**
│   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Expresses a wish, hope, or desire; may be unattainable or formal
│   │   │   ├── **Function:** To express longing, blessing, curse, or polite wishes
│   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Often uses subjunctive mood or special formulas
│   │   │   ├── **Common Patterns:**
│   │   │   │   ├── "**May you have good health!**" (formal blessing)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**Would that I could travel!**" (archaic/formal wish; unattainable)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**If only she would listen!**" (regret/wish)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**I wish I had more time.**" (contrary-to-fact wish)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**God bless you!**" (traditional blessing)
│   │   │   │   ├── "**Let's hope for the best!**" (hopeful expression)
│   │   │   │   └── "**Hopefully, we'll meet again.**" (modern expression of hope)
│   │   │   ├── **Punctuation:** Ends with **period (.) or exclamation mark (!)**
│   │   │   ├── **Frequency:** Relatively rare in everyday modern English; more formal or literary
│   │   │   └── **Note:** Some grammarians classify optatives as a type of imperative or separate mood
│   │   │
│   │   └── **5. Exclamative (Exclamation)**
│   │       ├── **Definition:** Expresses strong emotion, surprise, wonder, or emphasis
│   │       ├── **Function:** To convey intense feeling, amazement, strong approval/disapproval
│   │       ├── **Structure Types:**
│   │       │   ├── **What-Exclamatives:**
│   │       │   │   ├── "**What a beautiful sunset!**" (noun phrase emphasis)
│   │       │   │   ├── "**What an amazing performance!**"
│   │       │   │   └── "**What nonsense!**"
│   │       │   ├── **How-Exclamatives:**
│   │       │   │   ├── "**How clever you are!**" (adjective emphasis)
│   │       │   │   ├── "**How quickly time passes!**"
│   │       │   │   └── "**How absolutely wonderful!**"
│   │       │   ├── **Standard Sentences with Exclamation:**
│   │       │   │   ├── "**I can't believe it!**" (exclamatory declarative)
│   │       │   │   ├── "**That's incredible!**"
│   │       │   │   ├── "**She's so talented!**"
│   │       │   │   └── "**You're absolutely right!**"
│   │       │   └── **Single Words/Phrases (Interjections):**
│   │       │       ├── "**Wow!**" / "**Amazing!**" / "**Excellent!**"
│   │       │       ├── "**Oh no!**" / "**Help!**" / "**Ouch!**"
│   │       │       └── "**Fantastic!**" / "**Dreadful!**"
│   │       ├── **Punctuation:** Ends with **exclamation mark (!)**
│   │       ├── **Intonation:** Emphatic, with rising or falling stress depending on emotion
│   │       ├── **Emotion Types:**
│   │       │   ├── **Positive:** Joy, wonder, admiration, enthusiasm
│   │       │   ├── **Negative:** Shock, disgust, anger, dismay
│   │       │   └── **Neutral:** Strong emphasis without specific emotion
│   │       └── **Note:** Exclamatives overlap with emphatic declaratives; distinction lies in **primary function** (emotion vs. information)
│   │
│   └── **D. Aspect & Voice**
│       │
│       ├── **1. Aspect**
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **Overview:** Aspect shows how an action is distributed over TIME (continuous, repeated, or completed)
│       │   ├── **NOT the same as Tense:** Tense locates action in time (past/present/future); Aspect shows nature/duration
│       │   ├── **Formed by:** Auxiliary verbs (be, have) + participles (-ing, -ed)
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **a. Simple Aspect**
│       │   │   ├── **Definition:** Presents action as a completed whole or habitual/general truth
│       │   │   ├── **Formation:** Simple verb alone (no auxiliary for aspectual meaning)
│       │   │   ├── **Time Frame:** Ongoing action treated as single unit; habitual/iterative; or general truth
│       │   │   ├── **Examples by Tense:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Present Simple:** "She **works** as a doctor." (habitual/general truth)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Past Simple:** "He **arrived** at 5 o'clock." (completed action, single moment)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Future Simple:** "They **will leave** tomorrow." (future event as whole)
│       │   │   │   ├── "**She works** there now." (current state/habitual)
│       │   │   │   ├── "**I eat breakfast every morning.**" (regular routine)
│       │   │   │   └── "**The earth orbits the sun.**" (scientific truth)
│       │   │   ├── **Usage:** Neutral, objective presentation; no emphasis on duration or completion
│       │   │   └── **Note:** Default aspect; used when no other aspect is indicated
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **b. Perfect Aspect**
│       │   │   ├── **Definition:** Shows action COMPLETED BEFORE a reference point; emphasizes RESULT/RELEVANCE
│       │   │   ├── **Formation:** have + past participle (-ed)
│       │   │   ├── **Time Frame:** Looks backward from a reference point; emphasizes connection to present/past moment
│       │   │   ├── **Subtypes by Reference Point:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Present Perfect:** have + past participle (reference = NOW)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**She has finished her work.**" (completed before now; result relevant now)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**I have lived here for ten years.**" (started in past, continues to present)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**They have never seen this movie.**" (ever in their life)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Emphasizes: relevance to present, recent past, or unfinished period
│       │   │   │   ├── **Past Perfect:** had + past participle (reference = PAST moment)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**She had finished before he arrived.**" (completed before another past action)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**They had been waiting for hours.**" (completed by a past point)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Emphasizes: sequence of past events; one completed before another
│       │   │   │   └── **Future Perfect:** will have + past participle (reference = FUTURE moment)
│       │   │   │       ├── "**By tomorrow, I will have completed the project.**" (will be completed by future point)
│       │   │   │       ├── "**When you arrive, they will have finished dinner.**"
│       │   │   │       └── Emphasizes: completion before a future point
│       │   │   ├── **Key Feature:** Emphasizes COMPLETED ACTION with present/past/future relevance
│       │   │   └── **Compare:** Simple past: "She finished" (just states it happened); Perfect: "She has finished" (and it's relevant now)
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **c. Progressive Aspect**
│       │   │   ├── **Definition:** Shows action IN PROGRESS; emphasizes DURATION/PROCESS (not completion)
│       │   │   ├── **Formation:** be + present participle (-ing)
│       │   │   ├── **Time Frame:** Focuses on action happening DURING a specific time period; highlights the ongoing nature
│       │   │   ├── **Subtypes by Tense:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Present Progressive:** am/is/are + -ing
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**She is studying right now.**" (happening at this moment)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**They are building a new house.**" (ongoing process; not yet completed)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**What are you doing?**" (current action)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Emphasizes: current action, temporary activity, action in progress NOW
│       │   │   │   ├── **Past Progressive:** was/were + -ing
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**She was studying when he called.**" (ongoing action interrupted)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**They were playing football yesterday.**" (action during a past time)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Emphasizes: action happening over a past time period; background action
│       │   │   │   └── **Future Progressive:** will be + -ing
│       │   │   │       ├── "**She will be studying tomorrow at 5 PM.**" (ongoing at a future point)
│       │   │   │       ├── "**They will be flying to Paris next week.**" (action happening during future time)
│       │   │   │       └── Emphasizes: duration of future action; action spanning a future period
│       │   │   ├── **Key Feature:** Emphasizes PROCESS, DURATION, and IN-PROGRESS nature
│       │   │   └── **Compare:** Simple: "She studies" (habitual) vs. Progressive: "She is studying" (right now)
│       │   │
│       │   └── **d. Perfect Progressive Aspect**
│       │       ├── **Definition:** Combines PERFECT (completed) + PROGRESSIVE (ongoing); shows continuous action UP TO a point
│       │       ├── **Formation:** have/has/had been + present participle (-ing)
│       │       ├── **Time Frame:** Emphasizes duration of action leading up to a reference point; focuses on continuous process
│       │       ├── **Subtypes by Reference Point:**
│       │       │   ├── **Present Perfect Progressive:** have/has been + -ing
│       │       │   │   ├── "**She has been studying for three hours.**" (started in past, continuing now; may continue)
│       │       │   │   ├── "**They have been building the house for six months.**" (ongoing since past; not yet finished)
│       │       │   │   ├── "**How long have you been waiting?**" (duration from past to now)
│       │       │   │   └── Emphasizes: duration of continuous action from past to now; process continuing
│       │       │   ├── **Past Perfect Progressive:** had been + -ing
│       │       │   │   ├── "**She had been studying for hours before he interrupted.**" (continuous up to past moment)
│       │       │   │   ├── "**They had been waiting for days when the news came.**" (duration up to past point)
│       │       │   │   └── Emphasizes: how long the action had been going on by a past point
│       │       │   └── **Future Perfect Progressive:** will have been + -ing
│       │       │       ├── "**By next year, she will have been working here for ten years.**" (duration up to future point)
│       │       │       ├── "**When you arrive, they will have been waiting for hours.**"
│       │       │       └── Emphasizes: how long the action will have continued by a future point
│       │       ├── **Key Feature:** Emphasizes CONTINUOUS DURATION with reference to a specific moment
│       │       └── **Compare:** Perfect: "She has studied" (completion); Perfect Progressive: "She has been studying" (continuous up to now)
│       │
│       └── **2. Voice**
│           │
│           ├── **Overview:** Voice shows the relationship between the subject and the action (agent vs. patient)
│           ├── **Active vs. Passive:** Shows who PERFORMS the action vs. who RECEIVES the action
│           │
│           ├── **a. Active Voice**
│           │   ├── **Definition:** Subject PERFORMS the action; subject is the AGENT
│           │   ├── **Structure:** [Subject (agent)] + [Verb] + [Object (patient)]
│           │   ├── **Focus:** What the subject IS DOING; the doer of the action
│           │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   ├── "**The cat chased the mouse.**" (cat = agent; performs action)
│           │   │   ├── "**She wrote an excellent essay.**" (she = agent; writer)
│           │   │   ├── "**The company produces quality products.**" (company = agent; producer)
│           │   │   ├── "**They have completed the project.**" (they = agents; completers)
│           │   │   └── "**The wind knocked over the fence.**" (wind = agent; knocker)
│           │   ├── **Emphasis:** Energy, action, directness; WHO is doing WHAT
│           │   ├── **Frequency:** More common in active speech/writing; more dynamic
│           │   └── **Verb Types:** All verbs can appear in active voice; requires a direct object (for transitive verbs)
│           │
│           └── **b. Passive Voice**
│               ├── **Definition:** Subject RECEIVES the action; subject is the PATIENT (not the agent)
│               ├── **Structure:** [Subject (patient)] + [be + past participle] + [by + agent (optional)]
│               ├── **Formation:** be (in appropriate tense) + past participle (-ed)
│               ├── **Key Feature:** Agent may be omitted; focus is on the ACTION, not the doer
│               ├── **Examples:**
│               │   ├── "**The mouse was chased by the cat.**" (mouse = patient; receiver of action)
│               │   ├── "**An excellent essay was written by her.**" (essay = patient; what was written)
│               │   ├── "**Quality products are produced by the company.**" (products = patient)
│               │   ├── "**The project has been completed.**" (project = patient; agent omitted as obvious/unimportant)
│               │   ├── "**The fence was knocked over by the wind.**" (fence = patient; wind = agent)
│               │   └── "**The letter was delivered this morning.**" (letter = patient; agent omitted)
│               ├── **When Agent is Omitted:**
│               │   ├── "**The letter was mailed yesterday.**" (who mailed it? unknown or unimportant)
│               │   ├── "**The house was built in 1990.**" (builders are not relevant to the point)
│               │   ├── "**The window was broken.**" (focus on damage, not the breaker)
│               │   └── **Note:** Agent often omitted to protect identity, shift focus, or when agent is obvious/irrelevant
│               ├── **Passive in Different Tenses:**
│               │   ├── **Present Simple Passive:** am/is/are + past participle
│               │   │   └── "**The report is written carefully.**"
│               │   ├── **Past Simple Passive:** was/were + past participle
│               │   │   └── "**The bridge was destroyed in the earthquake.**"
│               │   ├── **Future Simple Passive:** will be + past participle
│               │   │   └── "**The building will be completed next year.**"
│               │   ├── **Present Perfect Passive:** have/has been + past participle
│               │   │   └── "**The work has been finished.**"
│               │   └── **Progressive Passive:** be being + past participle
│               │       └── "**The house is being renovated.**" (ongoing passive action)
│               ├── **Focus & Emphasis:** The ACTION and its RESULT; not WHO did it
│               ├── **Usage Contexts:**
│               │   ├── **Scientific/Academic Writing:** "The experiment was conducted under controlled conditions."
│               │   ├── **Instructions:** "The mixture should be stirred slowly."
│               │   ├── **News Reports:** "The suspect was arrested yesterday."
│               │   ├── **Formal Communication:** "Your application has been received."
│               │   ├── **When agent is unknown:** "The vase was broken." (don't know who broke it)
│               │   └── **For politeness/tact:** "Mistakes were made." (avoids direct blame)
│               ├── **Limitations:** 
│               │   ├── **Intransitive verbs cannot be passivized** (no object to become subject)
│               │   │   └── ✘ "*The child was slept."* vs. ✔ "The child slept." (simple active only)
│               │   ├── **Some verbs resist passivization:** 
│               │   │   └── "He resembles his father." → ✘ Not typically passivized (seems awkward)
│               │   └── **Passive generally less direct than active:** Can sound awkward if overused
│               └── **Active vs. Passive Comparison:**
│                   ├── **Active (Direct, Dynamic):** "The manager approved the request." (clear agent)
│                   ├── **Passive (Indirect, Static):** "The request was approved." (focus on action/result)
│                   ├── **Choose based on:** What is more important to emphasize—the doer or the action/result?
│                   └── **General Rule:** Prefer active voice for clarity; use passive when necessary for focus/context
│
├── **II. Constituents & Structure**
│   │
│   ├── **1. Concept**
│   │   │
│   │   ├──  **a. Head**
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **What is a Head?**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** The central, essential element of a phrase/clause that determines the phrase's grammatical category
│   │   │   │   ├── **Core Function:** Determines what KIND of phrase it is (noun phrase, verb phrase, adjective phrase, etc.)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Principle:** A phrase inherits its name and category FROM its head
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Example:** Noun = N-head → Noun Phrase (NP); Verb = V-head → Verb Phrase (VP)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Head-Dependency Rule:** Cannot be omitted without changing the phrase's fundamental nature
│   │   │   │   │   └── "The **big** book" can drop "big" but NOT "book" (would no longer be a noun phrase)
│   │   │   │   └── **Nucleus:** Every phrase has exactly ONE head (minimum requirement for phrase identity)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **How Heads Behave: Core Characteristics**-
│   │   │   │   ├── **1. Head Selection (Subcategorization):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Heads DETERMINE what complements can appear:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Some verbs REQUIRE objects: "eat (something)"; "give (something to someone)"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Some verbs FORBID objects: "sleep (✔)" vs. "*sleep the child (✘)"
│   │   │   │   ├── **2. Theta Role Assignment (Semantic Roles):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Heads assign SEMANTIC ROLES to their arguments:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Verb "eat" assigns: AGENT (to subject), PATIENT (to object)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "**She ate the apple**" = She (AGENT) + apple (PATIENT)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Verb "give" assigns: AGENT, GOAL, THEME
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "**He gave the book to her**" = He (AGENT), book (THEME), her (GOAL)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Preposition "in" assigns LOCATION: "in the house" = house (LOCATION)
│   │   │   │   ├── **3. Agreement Control (Morphological Features):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Heads impose agreement requirements on dependents:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Subject agrees with verb's tense/number: "She **works**" (3rd sing); "They **work**" (3rd pl)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Noun determines article/determiner: "a **book**" (singular); "**books**" (plural)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Gender (in languages with it): Spanish "un libro" (masc), "una mesa" (fem)
│   │   │   │   ├── **4. Structural Positioning (X-bar Hierarchy):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Head determines phrase structure:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Head combines with complements FIRST: [V [Complement]]
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "give the book" = V + Complement
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Then adjuncts attach: [[V Complement] Adjunct]
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "give the book **carefully**" = [V + Comp] + Adjunct
│   │   │   │   │   └── Finally specifier attaches: [Specifier [[V Complement] Adjunct]]
│   │   │   │   │       └── "She **gives the book carefully**" = Specifier + [V + Comp + Adjunct]
│   │   │   │   ├── **5. Case Assignment (Syntactic Feature):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Transitive verbs assign ACCUSATIVE case to objects:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "I **saw** **him**" (not "he"); "I gave **them** (not "they") the book"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Prepositions assign OBLIQUE case:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "in **her** house" (not "she"); "with **me**" (not "I")
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Exception - Nominative:** Subject gets nominative from Tense head, not verb
│   │   │   │   ├── **6. Lexical Content vs. Function:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Lexical Heads carry semantic meaning:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Noun: "table" (physical object)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Verb: "run" (action)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Adjective: "blue" (property)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Adverb: "quickly" (manner)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Functional Heads carry grammatical meaning:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Determiner "the" (indicates specificity)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Tense "will" (indicates future time)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Complementizer "that" (introduces clause)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Preposition "in" (indicates spatial relationship)
│   │   │   │   └── **7. Headedness Direction (Head-Initial vs. Head-Final):**
│   │   │   │       ├── **English is HEAD-INITIAL in most contexts:**
│   │   │   │       ├── Verb comes BEFORE its complements: "eat **the apple**" (V then Obj)
│   │   │   │       ├── Preposition comes BEFORE its object: "**in** the house" (P then NP)
│   │   │   │       └── BUT: Nouns come BEFORE their modifiers: "**book** on the shelf" (N then PP modifier)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **How Heads Work: Functional Operations**
│   │   │   │   ├── **1. Establishing Phrase Category:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Head's part of speech = Phrase's name
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples:
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Noun head** → Noun Phrase: "**the beautiful old house**"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Remove modifiers: still "house" = NP
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Verb head** → Verb Phrase: "**run quickly through the park**"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Remove modifiers: still "run" = VP
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Adjective head** → Adjective Phrase: "**very tall and dark**"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Remove modifiers: still "tall" = AdjP
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **Preposition head** → Prepositional Phrase: "**deep in the forest**"
│   │   │   │   │   │       └── Remove modifiers: still "in" = PP
│   │   │   │   ├── **2. Controlling Dependencies (Binding and Structure):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Heads control what other elements can appear:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Verb "persuade" requires: [V NP to-infinitive]
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "persuade John **to go**" (✔); "*persuade John that go" (✘)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Verb "know" requires: [V NP/Clause]
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "know the answer" or "know **that it's true**" (✔); "*know to go" (✘)
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Pronoun binding:** Head determines who can be referred to
│   │   │   │   │       └── "**John** saw himself" (✔); "*himself saw John" (✘)
│   │   │   │   ├── **3. Semantic Composition (Meaning Creation):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Head provides core semantic content; modifiers refine it:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**red** book" = a book that IS red (property added)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**big** explosion" = an explosion that IS big (intensity added)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "book **on the table**" = a book that IS located on the table (location added)
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Remove head = lose core meaning:**
│   │   │   │   │       └── "the red" (incomplete); "on the table" (incomplete without head noun)
│   │   │   │   ├── **4. Satisfying Grammatical Requirements:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Tense Head satisfies "clause must have tense":**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "**will** go" = Tense head "will" makes it a complete TP
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "**go**" (bare) = incomplete (no tense marking)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Determiner Head satisfies "DP structure":**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "**the** book" = has D head (determiner)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "book" (bare) = sometimes acceptable as bare NP
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Complementizer Head satisfies "embedded clause requirement":**
│   │   │   │   │       └── "I know **that** he left" = that-complementizer marks CP boundary
│   │   │   │   └── **5. Projection (Creating Larger Structures):**
│   │   │   │       ├── **Heads "project" up to create larger phrases:**
│   │   │   │       ├── **N projects:** N → N' → NP (with specifiers/complements/adjuncts)
│   │   │   │       ├── **V projects:** V → V' → VP → TP (with specifiers/complements/adjuncts)
│   │   │   │       └── **Each projection level adds structure but keeps head at core**
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **What Makes Something a Head? (Tests & Criteria)**
│   │   │   │   ├── **1. Omission Test:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── If omitting an element breaks the phrase = that element is the HEAD
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**red** big book" → "red **big** book" (still noun phrase, element not head)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "red big **book**" → "red big [MISSING]" (not a noun phrase anymore, "book" is head)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Only the head is irreplaceable in maintaining phrase identity
│   │   │   │   ├── **2. Category Determination Test:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── If removing an element changes the phrase's grammatical category = that element is head
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**quickly** run" → "run" is still a verb phrase (VP); "quickly" is not head
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "run **quickly**" → "run" is still a verb phrase; "quickly" is not head
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "**run** quickly" → MISSING = no longer a verb phrase; "run" is head
│   │   │   │   │   └── What determines phrase TYPE must be the head
│   │   │   │   ├── **3. Distribution Test:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── If an element can be replaced by a single word of the same category = not head
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "the **red book**" → "the **book**" (can drop "red")
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "the **book on the shelf**" → "the **book**" (can drop "on the shelf")
│   │   │   │   │   └── But "the book" → "the" **??** (can't replace with single modifier = "book" is head)
│   │   │   │   ├── **4. Selection/Subcategorization Test:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── If an element selects what can come with it = that element is head
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Verb "put" selects: NP + PP
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "**put** the book on the shelf" (put = head; it determines structure)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Noun "book" selects: not much (modifiers are optional)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Heads REQUIRE/SELECT; dependents MODIFY
│   │   │   │   └── **5. Morphological Feature Test:**
│   │   │   │       ├── If an element carries the morphological features = that element is head
│   │   │   │       ├── In "the books," "book" carries NUMBER (plural -s) = head
│   │   │   │       ├── In "slowly running," "running" carries ASPECT (-ing) = head
│   │   │   │       ├── In "will go," "will" carries TENSE = head
│   │   │   │       └── Morphological features mark the head position
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Lexical Heads (Content Words)**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Noun (N) - Entity Head:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Names persons, places, things, ideas, qualities
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** child, hospital, freedom, justice, Peter, London
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Behavior:** 
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Takes determiners: "**the** child"; "**a** hospital"; "**this** freedom"
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Takes modifiers: "**beautiful** child"; "**new** hospital"; "**absolute** freedom"
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Takes prepositional complements: "**the book on the shelf**"; "**the city of London**"
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Shows number agreement: "child/children"; "hospital/hospitals"
│   │   │   │   │   └── **In Phrases:** Always head of NP/DP; central to nominal meanings
│   │   │   │   ├── **Verb (V) - Action/State Head:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Expresses actions, processes, states, or events
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** run, believe, exist, become, seem, give, eat
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Behavior:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Takes tense/aspect markers: "**will** run"; "**is running**"; "**has run**"
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Takes complements selectively: "give" requires two; "sleep" requires none
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Takes adverbial modifiers: "run **quickly**"; "**suddenly** appeared"
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Governs subject: "**She** runs"; "**They** run" (agreement)
│   │   │   │   │   └── **In Phrases:** Always head of VP; controls clause structure (transitivity)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Adjective (A) - Property Head:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Describes properties or qualities of entities
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** red, beautiful, tall, intelligent, happy, dangerous
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Behavior:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Takes degree modifiers: "**very** red"; "**somewhat** happy"; "**quite** beautiful"
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Takes prepositional complements: "proud **of her work**"; "afraid **of spiders**"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Shows degree inflection: tall, taller, tallest
│   │   │   │   │   └── **In Phrases:** Always head of AdjP; modifies nouns or functions as complement
│   │   │   │   ├── **Adverb (Adv) - Circumstance Head:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Modifies verbs, adjectives, other adverbs, or entire clauses
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** quickly, very, here, soon, certainly, unfortunately
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Behavior:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Takes degree modifiers: "**very** quickly"; "**extremely** carefully"
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── No inflection (except in some languages)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Attaches freely as adjuncts (optional)
│   │   │   │   │   └── **In Phrases:** Always head of AdvP; modifies various elements
│   │   │   │   └── **Preposition (P) - Relation Head:**
│   │   │   │       ├── **Function:** Expresses spatial, temporal, causal, or other relationships
│   │   │   │       ├── **Examples:** in, on, at, under, before, after, because, despite
│   │   │   │       ├── **Behavior:**
│   │   │   │       │   ├── Takes NP objects: "in **the house**"; "after **the party**"
│   │   │   │       │   ├── Assigns oblique case to object (in case-marking languages)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── Creates locative/temporal/causal meanings
│   │   │   │       │   └── Some take clausal objects: "because **she left**"; "after **he finished**"
│   │   │   │       └── **In Phrases:** Always head of PP; provides relational semantics
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   └── **Functional Heads (Structure Words)**
│   │   │       ├── **Determiner (D) - Reference Marker:**
│   │   │       │   ├── **Function:** Marks definiteness, demonstrative reference, possession, quantity
│   │   │       │   ├── **Examples:** the, a, this, that, my, his, her, some, many, each, both
│   │   │       │   ├── **Behavior:**
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Appears sentence-initially relative to noun: "**the** book"; "**my** car"; "**this** dog"
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Controls/restricts NP interpretation: "the book" (specific); "a book" (general)
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Creates DP structure (Determiner Phrase), not bare NP
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Determiners are mutually exclusive: "*the my book*" (✘); "*this that dog*" (✘)
│   │   │       │   │   └── Shows agreement with noun: "**this** cat" (sing); "**these** cats" (pl)
│   │   │       │   └── **In Phrases:** Head of DP (Determiner Phrase); houses the noun
│   │   │       ├── **Tense/Inflection (T/I) - Time/Mood Marker:**
│   │   │       │   ├── **Function:** Marks tense (past/present/future), mood (indicative/subjunctive), modality
│   │   │       │   ├── **Examples:** -ed (past); -s (3rd sg); will, would, can, could, should, must
│   │   │       │   ├── **Behavior:**
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Attaches to verb: "walk→walked"; "walk→walks"; "will walk"
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Creates finite vs. non-finite distinctions
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Controls subject agreement: "She **walks**" (3rd sg); "They **walk**" (plural)
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Marks clause as independent (finite) or dependent (non-finite)
│   │   │       │   │   └── Modals (will, can, must) cannot co-occur: "*will can go*" (✘)
│   │   │       │   └── **In Phrases:** Head of TP (Tense Phrase); dominates VP
│   │   │       ├── **Complementizer (C) - Clause Introducer:**
│   │   │       │   ├── **Function:** Introduces embedded clauses; marks clause type
│   │   │       │   ├── **Examples:** that, if, whether, for, because (also subordinating conjunctions)
│   │   │       │   ├── **Behavior:**
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Appears sentence-initially in embedded context: "I know **that** he left"
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Marks clause as subordinate (dependent)
│   │   │       │   │   ├── For wh-clauses, interacts with wh-phrases: "I wonder **[C what]** he saw"
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Can be deleted in some contexts: "I know (that) he left" (optional in some dialects)
│   │   │       │   │   └── Marks different clause types: "that" (declaration); "if/whether" (polar Q); "wh-" (open Q)
│   │   │       │   └── **In Phrases:** Head of CP (Complementizer Phrase); highest clause layer
│   │   │       ├── **Degree (Deg) - Intensity Modifier:**
│   │   │       │   ├── **Function:** Modifies/intensifies adjectives and adverbs for degree/intensity
│   │   │       │   ├── **Examples:** very, quite, somewhat, rather, too, so, extremely, incredibly
│   │   │       │   ├── **Behavior:**
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Takes only adjectives/adverbs as complements: "**very** happy" (✔); "*very run*" (✘)
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Gradable only: modifies words with degree scales: "**very** tall" (✔); "*very bury*" (✘)
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Some degree heads are exclusive: "*very quite happy*" (✘)
│   │   │       │   │   └── Combines with comparatives/superlatives: "**so** much; **very** best"
│   │   │       │   └── **In Phrases:** Head of DegP (Degree Phrase); modifies AdjP/AdvP
│   │   │       └── **Aspect/Negation (Asp/Neg) - Tense Modifiers:**
│   │   │           ├── **Function:** Marks aspect (progressive, perfect) and negation
│   │   │           ├── **Examples:** 
│   │   │           │   ├── **Aspect:** be (progressive); have (perfect)
│   │   │           │   └── **Negation:** not, n't (negative marker)
│   │   │           ├── **Behavior:**
│   │   │           │   ├── Progressive: "**is** running" = be-head + V-ing
│   │   │           │   ├── Perfect: "**has** run" = have-head + past participle
│   │   │           │   ├── Negation: "**do not** go" = Neg head negates entire VP
│   │   │           │   ├── Must follow Tense but precede main verb: "has **been** running"
│   │   │           │   └── Forms complex verbal structures: "will have been running"
│   │   │           └── **In Phrases:** Head of AspP (Aspect Phrase); intermediate between T and V
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **b. Modifier (Adjunct)**
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Definition & Core Function**
│   │   │   │   ├── **What is a Modifier/Adjunct?**
│   │   │   │   │   └── An optional constituent that provides supplementary information about a head or clause
│   │   │   │   │       without being required by the head's subcategorization frame. Modifiers are
│   │   │   │   │       semantically restrictive (narrow scope) or descriptive (add properties/circumstances)
│   │   │   │   │       but grammatically NON-OBLIGATORY.
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **Key Distinction: Modifier vs. Complement**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Modifiers (Adjuncts):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── OPTIONAL - can be omitted without ungrammaticality
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── REPETITIVE - multiple modifiers can attach to same head
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── MOVABLE - can shift position (within constraints)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── NON-SUBCATEGORIZED - head does not select for them
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── EXTERNALLY ATTACHED - added to phrase after head selects complements
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Complements (covered in section II.1.c):**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── OBLIGATORY - often required by head's subcategorization
│   │   │   │   │       ├── UNIQUE - typically one complement per type per head
│   │   │   │   │       ├── ORDERED - fixed position adjacent to head
│   │   │   │   │       ├── SUBCATEGORIZED - head explicitly selects for them
│   │   │   │   │       └── INTERNALLY ATTACHED - integrated into core structure
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   └── **Examples Showing Distinction:**
│   │   │   │       ├── "Maria read the book quickly." 
│   │   │   │       │   ├── Complement: "the book" (REQUIRED - object of read)
│   │   │   │       │   └── Modifier: "quickly" (OPTIONAL - adds manner information)
│   │   │   │       ├── "The students carefully discussed the problem yesterday."
│   │   │   │       │   ├── Complement: "the problem" (REQUIRED - object of discussed)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── Modifier: "carefully" (OPTIONAL - manner)
│   │   │   │       │   └── Modifier: "yesterday" (OPTIONAL - temporal)
│   │   │   │       └── "She enjoys reading novels in the library." 
│   │   │   │           ├── Complement: "reading novels" (REQUIRED - object of enjoys)
│   │   │   │           └── Modifier: "in the library" (OPTIONAL - location)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Core Behavioral Characteristics**
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **1. Optionality & Repeatability**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Modifiers are purely optional; deletion yields grammatical sentences
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Multiple modifiers of the same type can modify a single head (recursion)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── No maximum limit on modifier accumulation (though readability suffers)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Examples:
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "The book" → "The new book" → "The new, expensive, hardcover book"
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "She ran" → "She ran quickly" → "She ran quickly yesterday" → "She ran quickly yesterday at dawn"
│   │   │   │   │       └── Infinitely recursive: "I saw the tall, dark, handsome, intelligent, charming gentleman."
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **2. Position Constraints & Selectivity**
│   │   │   │   ├── Not all modifiers can appear in all positions
│   │   │   │   ├── Pre-modifiers have strict ordering rules (Adjective Order Convention)
│   │   │   │   ├── Post-modifiers have limited positions (after head, often with restrictions)
│   │   │   │   ├── Different head types license different modifier positions
│   │   │   │   └── Examples:
│   │   │   │       ├── Noun Pre-modifiers: Opinion (beautiful) → Size (big) → Shape (round) → Color (red) → Origin (Italian) → Material (wooden) → Purpose (dining) NOUN
│   │   │   │       │   ├── ✓ "a beautiful big round red Italian wooden dining table"
│   │   │   │       │   └── ✗ "a wooden red round big beautiful Italian dining table" (violates adjective order)
│   │   │   │       ├── Adjective modifiers: Only degree words pre-modify; most post-modifiers rare
│   │   │   │       │   ├── ✓ "very beautiful" / ✓ "proud of her accomplishments" (post-modifier)
│   │   │   │       │   └── ✗ "beautiful very" (impossible post-position)
│   │   │   │       └── Adverbial modifiers: Can appear in sentence-initial, mid, or final position
│   │   │   │           ├── ✓ "Yesterday, they left." / ✓ "They yesterday left." / ✓ "They left yesterday."
│   │   │   │           └── But: Manner adverbs prefer post-verbal position: ✓ "He ran quickly." / ✗ "He quickly ran." (less acceptable)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **3. Scope & Semantic Composition**
│   │   │   │   ├── Modifiers RESTRICT, NARROW, or REFINE the meaning of their head
│   │   │   │   ├── Intersective modifiers: [H + MOD] = [things that are both H AND MOD]
│   │   │   │   ├── Non-intersective modifiers: Meaning shifts contextually or comparatively
│   │   │   │   ├── Scope determines modifier interpretation (same word → different meanings by position)
│   │   │   │   └── Examples:
│   │   │   │       ├── Intersective: "red apple" = {things: red AND apple}
│   │   │   │       │   ├── Can be test-compared: ✓ "Is it red? Is it an apple?" (both yes = intersective)
│   │   │   │       │   └── Order-independent: "red apple" ≈ "apple red" (in meaning, though not grammar)
│   │   │   │       ├── Non-intersective: "good doctor" ≠ {things: good AND doctor}
│   │   │   │       │   ├── Cannot test separately: "Is he good?" depends on "good at what?" (contextual)
│   │   │   │       │   └── "good doctor" ≠ "doctor who is good in general"
│   │   │   │       ├── Scope-dependent: 
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "potentially dangerous animals" (potentially: modifier scope over "dangerous animals")
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "dangerous potentially animals" would shift scope (but ungrammatical)
│   │   │   │       └── "She was definitely going to London." vs. "She definitely was going to London."
│   │   │   │           (Scope of "definitely" shifts interpretation)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **4. Stacking & Ordering Dependencies**
│   │   │   │   ├── Multiple modifiers of different types interact via hierarchy rules
│   │   │   │   ├── Pre-nominal adjectives follow rigid Adjective Order Convention (English universal)
│   │   │   │   ├── Post-nominal modifiers have scope-dependent ordering (often non-commutative)
│   │   │   │   ├── Adverbial modifiers have scope-based ordering (verb > subject > clause modifiers)
│   │   │   │   └── Examples:
│   │   │   │       ├── Pre-nominal stacking (adjective order):
│   │   │   │       │   ├── ✓ "a beautiful tall German wooden house"
│   │   │   │       │   │   └── Order: OPINION (beautiful) > SIZE (tall) > ORIGIN (German) > MATERIAL (wooden)
│   │   │   │       │   └── ✗ "a German tall beautiful wooden house" (violates OPINION before ORIGIN)
│   │   │   │       ├── Post-nominal stacking:
│   │   │   │       │   ├── ✓ "a book on shelves from last year" (post-modifier sequence)
│   │   │   │       │   └── ✓ "a book from last year on shelves" (can reorder with scope change)
│   │   │   │       └── Adverbial stacking:
│   │   │   │           ├── ✓ "He speaks English fluently in class." 
│   │   │   │           │   └── Manner (fluently) before Place (in class)
│   │   │   │           ├── ✓ "He speaks in class English fluently." (unusual but grammatical with different scope)
│   │   │   │           └── Time > Place > Manner (general adverbial ordering)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **5. Compatibility & Semantic Constraints**
│   │   │   │   ├── Not all modifiers can combine with all heads (semantic/pragmatic constraints)
│   │   │   │   ├── Modifiers must be semantically compatible with head's features
│   │   │   │   ├── Sortal/selectional restrictions may apply
│   │   │   │   ├── Pragmatic plausibility can reject "grammatical" combinations
│   │   │   │   └── Examples:
│   │   │   │       ├── Incompatible combinations (grammatical but anomalous):
│   │   │   │       │   ├── ✓ "an invisible chair" (logically anomalous - objects are visible/invisible by nature)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── ✓ "a liquid rock" (contradictory property combination)
│   │   │   │       │   └── ✓ "a silent scream" (oxymoronic - pragmatically marked)
│   │   │   │       ├── Semantic incompatibility:
│   │   │   │       │   ├── ✗ "the redness of running" (property modifier + action: fails)
│   │   │   │       │   └── ✗ "*five truthfully" (cardinal + manner adverb: incompatible)
│   │   │   │       └── Selectional constraints:
│   │   │   │           ├── Temporal modifiers select for dynamic predicates: ✓ "ran yesterday" ✗ "knew yesterday" (in certain contexts)
│   │   │   │           └── Degree modifiers select for gradable adjectives: ✓ "very beautiful" ✗ "very married"
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **How Modifiers Attach & Operate**
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **Mechanism 1: Adjunction (Standard Attachment)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Modifiers attach via ADJUNCTION operation (duplicating phrasal node)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Creates recursive phrase structure: NP → (Modifier) NP → (Modifier) NP...
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Allows unlimited modifier attachment and repeatability
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Preserves original phrase category while adding information
│   │   │   │   │   └── Structure Example:
│   │   │   │   │       ├── Base: [NP the book]
│   │   │   │   │       ├── Add modifier 1: [NP [AP new] [NP the book]]
│   │   │   │   │       ├── Add modifier 2: [NP [PP in the library] [NP [AP new] [NP the book]]]
│   │   │   │   │       └── Tree structure allows stacking via recursion
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Mechanism 2: Position-Determined Attachment**
│   │   │   │   ├── Pre-modifiers attach to left of head (adjoin to higher node)
│   │   │   │   ├── Post-modifiers attach to right of head (adjoin to lower node or sister to head)
│   │   │   │   ├── Sentence-level adverbials adjoin to TP or VP (scope-dependent)
│   │   │   │   └── Different attachment sites create scope ambiguities
│   │   │   │       ├── "I probably will leave." (probably modifies modal/future)
│   │   │   │       ├── "Probably, I will leave." (probably modifies whole clause)
│   │   │   │       └── Same adverb → different structural positions → different interpretations
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Mechanism 3: Semantic Modification**
│   │   │   │   ├── Modifiers contribute semantic predicates that apply to head referent
│   │   │   │   ├── For NP modifiers: Create property sets that filter NP extension
│   │   │   │   ├── For predicate modifiers: Add circumstantial information to event/state
│   │   │   │   └── Examples:
│   │   │   │       ├── "The red book" = {x: book(x) ∧ red(x)} (semantic conjunction)
│   │   │   │       ├── "She left quickly" = left'(she) ∧ quickly(leaving-event) (manner adds to event)
│   │   │   │       └── "Yesterday she left." = left'(she) ∧ yesterday(leaving-event-time) (temporal adds location)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Position Categories & Distribution Rules**
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **A. PRE-MODIFICATION (Modifiers BEFORE Head)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Where:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Noun phrases: "beautiful house" / "three books" / "my new car"
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Some adjectival phrases: "very beautiful" (degree pre-modifies adjective)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Limited adverbial: "almost left" (degree pre-modifies adverb)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Characteristics:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── STRICTLY ORDERED (Adjective Order Convention governs sequences)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── DIRECT ADJACENCY (pre-modifiers attach immediately left of head)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── TIGHT SEMANTICS (pre-modifiers have narrowing/restrictive scope)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── NOT SEPARATED BY PAUSE (in speech, pre-modifiers lack intonational break)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Adjective Order Convention (English Pre-nominal Order):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **1. QUANTITY:** Many, few, numerous, several, multiple
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **2. OPINION/EVALUATION:** Beautiful, ugly, good, bad, wonderful, terrible, lovely
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **3. SIZE:** Big, small, large, tiny, huge, enormous, little
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **4. SHAPE:** Round, square, long, short, wide, narrow, straight, curved
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **5. AGE/RECENCY:** Old, new, young, ancient, modern, recent, stale
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **6. COLOR:** Red, blue, green, yellow, multicolored, pale, dark
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **7. ORIGIN/NATIONALITY:** Italian, French, American, Egyptian, Eastern, Western
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **8. MATERIAL:** Wooden, stone, metal, plastic, glass, leather, silk
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **9. PURPOSE/PARTICIPLE:** Dancing, dining, sleeping, reading (table, shoes, bag)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Pre-nominal Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "a beautiful big round red Italian wooden dining table"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Following: OPINION > SIZE > SHAPE > COLOR > ORIGIN > MATERIAL > PURPOSE
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the tall dark handsome stranger"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── OPINION (tall=positive appraisal) > SHAPE/SIZE (dark=somber tone) > QUALITY (handsome)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "several important German philosophical concepts"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── QUANTITY > OPINION > ORIGIN > PURPOSE NOUN
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "my new expensive leather jacket"
│   │   │   │   │   │       └── OPINION (new, expensive) > MATERIAL > NOUN
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Violations of Pre-nominal Order (Unnatural/Ungrammatical):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✗ "a red round beautiful table" (COLOR before SHAPE/OPINION)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✗ "a wooden Italian big table" (MATERIAL before ORIGIN/SIZE)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── ✗ "a size big beautiful table" (redundant + wrong order)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **B. POST-MODIFICATION (Modifiers AFTER Head)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Where:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Noun phrases: "the book on the shelf" / "students who worked hard"
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Adjective phrases: "proud of her success" / "afraid of spiders"
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Adverbial phrases (sentence-level): "She left yesterday" / "They met in Paris"
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Prepositional phrases (predicate modification): "He walked slowly through the park"
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Characteristics:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── LESS STRICTLY ORDERED (some reordering possible; scope-dependent)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ADJACENCY FLEXIBLE (post-modifiers can be separated by other elements)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── OPEN SEMANTICS (post-modifiers often have broader/non-restrictive scope)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── SEPARATED BY PAUSE (intonational break typical, especially relative clauses)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Post-nominal (Noun) Modifier Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Relative clauses (most common):
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the book that I read" (restrictive - specifies which book)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "Shakespeare's plays, which are timeless, remain popular." (non-restrictive - adds info)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "the person you mentioned" (no relative pronoun possible)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Prepositional phrases (location/possession/characteristics):
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the book on the shelf" (location)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the president of the company" (possession/relation)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "a woman with red hair" (property/characteristic)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "the fear of failure" (abstract relation)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Participle phrases:
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "students working late" (-ing participle; active sense)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "problems solved yesterday" (-ed participle; passive sense)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Appositive noun phrases:
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "My friend, Sarah, teaches chemistry." (appositive noun)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "The fact that she left early surprised us." (can follow certain nouns)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Adjectival phrases (less common):
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "people [afraid of spiders]" (adjective + PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │       └── "someone [interesting to talk to]" (adjective + infinitive)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Post-predicate (Adverbial) Modifier Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Manner adverbs/phrases (how action occurs):
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She spoke softly." / "She spoke with confidence."
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "He ran quickly through the park." (manner + location)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Temporal adverbs/phrases (when action occurs):
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "They met yesterday." / "They met in 2020." / "They met during the conference."
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "She will return next week."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Locative/directional phrases (where action occurs):
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "He lives in Paris." / "She walked to the market." / "They ran from the building."
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "I'll wait outside the theater."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Frequency adverbs (how often):
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "She exercises regularly." / "He visits often." / "They meet daily."
│   │   │   │   │   │       └── Note: Sentence-medial positions also possible for frequency ("often visits")
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **C. MID-POSITION (For Adverbial Modifiers)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Where:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Between subject and verb (pre-verbal position)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Between auxiliary and main verb
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Between subject and auxiliary (rare, emphatic)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── In embedded clauses
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She frequently visits." / "She often visits." (frequency in mid-position)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She has often visited." / "She is probably leaving." (between auxiliary + main verb)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She probably will leave." / "She definitely has gone." (emphasis via position)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "I think he probably left." (embedded mid-position)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **D. SENTENCE-INITIAL (For Adverbial Modifiers)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Where:**
