│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Beginning of main clause (emphatic, topicalizing position)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Often followed by intonational comma in writing
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Creates wide scope (modifies entire clause)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "Yesterday, they left for Paris." (temporal)
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "Carefully, she opened the door." (manner)
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "Fortunately, no one was hurt." (evaluation)
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "Outside the theater, a crowd gathered." (locative)
│   │   │   │   │       └── "However, we persisted." (discourse connector)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Semantic Classification of Modifiers**
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **Adjectival Modifiers (Noun Modifiers)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Descriptive (Property-assigning)**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Add permanent or intrinsic properties to noun referent
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "blue eyes", "tall person", "heavy suitcase", "intelligent student"
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Usually pre-nominal; some can be post-nominal with meaning shift
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Restrictive (Narrowing/Specifying)**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Limit/restrict the denotation of the noun to a subset
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "the red car" (restricts to red ones) vs. "the car" (any car)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Relative clauses: "students who passed" (restricts to those who passed)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Can create contrastive interpretation: "She has a red car." (implying others don't)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Non-restrictive (Appositional/Descriptive)**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Add description without restricting reference (presupposes noun is already identified)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "Paris, which is beautiful," / "My son, who is now 21, studies engineering."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Grammatically marked by commas/intonation in English
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Often post-nominal; adds "bonus information" beyond identification
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **Adverbial Modifiers (Predicate/Clause Modifiers)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Manner Adverbials: HOW the action occurs**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: In what manner / How does it happen?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "She spoke loudly." / "He ran quickly." / "They argued bitterly."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: Adverbs (-ly), PPs ("with care"), APPs ("in a careful manner")
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Typical position: Post-verbal / clause-final
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Temporal Adverbials: WHEN the action occurs**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: At what time / When does it happen?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "I left yesterday." / "She will return next week." / "They met in 2020."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: Temporal adverbs (now, then, yesterday), NPs (Tuesday, last week), PPs (in June, during the war)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Can appear at sentence-initial, mid, or final position
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Wide scope interpretation (modifies entire clause event time)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Locative/Directional Adverbials: WHERE the action occurs**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: In what location / Where does it happen?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "He lives in Paris." / "She walked to the market." / "They ran from the building."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: Locative adverbs (here, there, inside), PPs (in the park, under the bridge), NPs (downtown)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Can appear at sentence-initial, mid, or final position
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Often modifies both verb and clause (dual scope)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Frequency/Aspectual Adverbials: HOW OFTEN the action occurs**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: How frequently / With what regularity?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "She exercises regularly." / "He visits often." / "They meet daily."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: Frequency adverbs (always, often, sometimes, never), PPs (every day, once a week)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Preference for mid-position (between subject and verb or after first auxiliary)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Note: Interacts with aspect (simple vs. perfect progressive)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Duration/Extent Adverbials: FOR HOW LONG the action lasts**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: How long / For what duration?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "They stayed for three weeks." / "She worked until midnight." / "He slept all night."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: PPs (for X time, until X), NPs (three weeks, all day)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Characteristic position: Sentence-final
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Often co-occurs with progressive or perfect aspect
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Reason/Cause Adverbials: WHY the action occurs**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: For what reason / Why does it happen?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "She left because of the noise." / "He was worried due to the deadline." / "They failed from lack of preparation."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: PPs (because of, due to, from, owing to), Adverbial clauses (because, as, since)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Often post-predicate (final position)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Purpose/Goal Adverbials: WITH WHAT GOAL the action occurs**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: For what purpose / To what end?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "She worked to earn money." / "He studied for the exam." / "They trained in preparation."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: Infinitival clauses (to VP), PPs (for X purpose, in order to)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Often post-predicate or sentence-final
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Conditional Adverbials: UNDER WHAT CONDITIONS the action occurs**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: Under what circumstances / On what condition?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "If you work hard, you'll succeed." / "You'll be welcome provided you're polite." / "In the event of fire, exit through the windows."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: Conditional clauses (if, unless, as long as), PPs (in case of, in the event of)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Often pre-clausal (sentence-initial) with resulting foregrounding
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Concessive Adverbials: DESPITE WHAT CIRCUMSTANCES**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: What contradiction does this represent?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "Despite the rain, they played." / "Although she was tired, she finished." / "Nevertheless, we persisted."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: Concessive clauses (although, even though), PPs (despite, in spite of), Discourse adverbs (however, nonetheless)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Often pre-clausal (creates contrast/background)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Result/Consequence Adverbials: WHAT FOLLOWS as result**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: What effect / What consequence?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "He was so tired that he fell asleep." / "She studied hard, so she passed." / "As a result, profits rose."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: Resultative clauses (so...that, such...that), Discourse adverbs (therefore, consequently, as a result)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Often post-clausal (follows event)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Evaluation/Attitude Adverbials: JUDGMENT about entire proposition**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer: What is the speaker's attitude toward this statement?
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "Fortunately, no one was hurt." / "Frankly, I don't believe it." / "Obviously, they were wrong."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Forms: Evaluative adverbs (unfortunately, frankly, obviously, hopefully, thankfully)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Typical position: Sentence-initial or initial subordinate clause
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Parenthetical scope (comments on entire proposition)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **Form-Based Classification of Modifiers**
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **1. Adjective Phrases (AP) - Pre/Post-nominal**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Pre-nominal (Adjective only or with degree modifier):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "very beautiful" / "quite tall" / "extremely intelligent"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "beautiful tall house" / "expensive new car"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Position: Immediately before head noun
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Post-nominal (Adjective + Complement):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "people proud of their heritage" / "students eager to learn"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "someone afraid of heights" / "something difficult to understand"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Note: Adjectival complements often post-nominal only
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **Examples in context:**
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "a bright student" vs. "a student bright with promise" (both valid)
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "an old friend" (pre-nominal, means "long-standing")
│   │   │   │   │   │       └── "a friend old-fashioned in style" (post-nominal, emphasizes post-nominal predication)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **2. Prepositional Phrases (PP) - Typically Post-nominal/Post-verbal**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Noun modifiers:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the book on the shelf" (location)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the president of the company" (relation/possession)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "a man with red hair" (property)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "the key to the door" (functional relation)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Predicate modifiers:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She works in the office." (locative)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "He spoke with authority." (manner)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "They agreed despite their differences." (concessive)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "She acted without hesitation." (manner/degree)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Adjective modifiers:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "proud of her achievements" / "worried about the deadline"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "aware of the problem" / "afraid of spiders"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "independent from his family" / "different from mine"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **Adverb modifiers:**
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "very much interested" / "far from certain"
│   │   │   │   │   │       └── "quite beyond reasonable doubt" / "rather under the weather"
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **3. Relative Clauses - Post-nominal**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Restrictive (identifying, no comma):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the book that I read" / "students who passed the exam"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the car she drives" / "the reason they left"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Purpose: Specifies which one / narrows the set
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Non-restrictive (appositional, with comma/intonation break):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "My sister, who is a doctor, lives nearby." 
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "Shakespeare's plays, which are timeless, remain popular."
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Purpose: Adds non-essential descriptive information
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Contact clauses (relative pronoun omission):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the person I met" = "the person whom I met" (object relative)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the year we started" = "the year when we started" (adverbial relative)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "the reason they left" = "the reason why they left" (reason relative)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **Free relatives (no head noun):**
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "What I said was true." (wh-free relative as subject)
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "She did whatever she wanted." (-ever free relative as object)
│   │   │   │   │   │       └── These modify implicitly or serve as arguments
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **4. Participle Phrases - Post-nominal/Post-verbal**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Present (-ing) Participles (Active meaning):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Noun modifiers: "the boy running down the street" / "students working late"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Predicate modifiers: "He left running toward the door." / "She sat reading all day."
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Suggests ongoing/simultaneous action relative to main clause
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Past (-ed) Participles (Passive/Resultative meaning):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Noun modifiers: "the problems solved yesterday" / "the house built in 1990"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Predicate modifiers: "The puzzle solved, they left." / "Everything resolved, she felt relieved."
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Suggests completed/finished action or resultant state
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **Perfect (having -ed) Participles:**
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "Having finished her homework, she relaxed."
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "Not having heard the announcement, they missed the meeting."
│   │   │   │   │   │       └── Indicates action completed before main clause event
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **5. Adverbs/Adverb Phrases (AdvP) - Multiple positions**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Simple adverbs (single word):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Manner: "quickly", "slowly", "carefully", "beautifully"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Temporal: "yesterday", "now", "soon", "always"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Locative: "here", "there", "away", "downstairs"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Frequency: "often", "always", "sometimes", "rarely"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Positions: Post-verbal, mid-position, or sentence-initial
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Adverb Phrases (Degree + Adverb):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "very quickly" / "quite slowly" / "extremely carefully"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "almost always" / "nearly never" / "just now"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Pre-modifies the head adverb (degree words as modifiers themselves)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **Examples in context:**
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "She spoke eloquently." / "He ran quickly through the forest."
│   │   │   │   │   │       ├── "They will certainly return." / "I probably saw her."
│   │   │   │   │   │       └── "She performed extremely well." / "He was remarkably calm."
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **6. Noun Phrases (NP) - Nominal Modifiers**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Appositive NPs (no preposition; identifying/naming):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "My friend Sarah" / "President Obama" / "The city Paris"
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Can be restrictive or non-restrictive
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "My daughter, Mary," (non-restrictive) vs. "My daughter Mary" (restrictive - implies multiple daughters)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Temporal NPs (temporal expression as modifier):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She left yesterday." / "They met last year." / "I'll return tomorrow."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Functions as adverbial (tells when)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── No preposition (bare NP as temporal modifier)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Genitive NPs (possessive modifier):**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "John's book" / "my friend's house" / "the president's speech"
│   │   │   │   │       ├── Pre-nominal position
│   │   │   │   │       └── Note: 's genitive already integrated into determiner system
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **7. Clause Modifiers (Subordinate Clauses as Adjuncts)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Adverbial Clauses (sentence modifiers):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Temporal: "When I arrived, she had already left." / "After we finished, we left."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Causal: "Because it rained, we stayed inside." / "Since you asked, I'll tell you."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Conditional: "If you work hard, you'll succeed." / "Unless you hurry, we'll be late."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Concessive: "Although it rained, we went out." / "Even though she was tired, she finished."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Resultative: "So that you understand, listen carefully." / "Such that everyone could hear, he spoke loudly."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Can be pre-clausal or post-clausal
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Infinitival Clauses (as purpose/goal modifiers):**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "She studied to become a doctor." / "I worked hard to support my family."
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "The materials needed to build the house" (infinitive + object modifying noun)
│   │   │   │   │       └── Often expresses purpose or intended result
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Key Restrictions & Special Cases**
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **Adjunct Islands (Extraction Constraints)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Elements within adverbial adjuncts cannot usually be moved out (extraction-prohibited)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples of prohibited extractions:
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✓ Baseline: "He left [while she was cooking dinner]." (adjunct with embedded clause)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✗ Attempted wh-extraction: "*What did he leave [while she was cooking __]?" (violates island)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✓ Without adjunct: "What was she cooking?" (grammatical outside adjunct)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── *Why did he leave [while she was cooking dinner __]?" (reason adjuncts are even stronger islands)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Implication: Adjuncts form separate domains for movement operations
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **Ambiguity: Attachment Scope**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── When multiple adjuncts present, scope ambiguity can arise
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples:
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "He saw the man with the telescope."
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Reading 1 (high attachment): He used the telescope to see [the man with the telescope]
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Reading 2 (low attachment): He saw [the man who had the telescope]
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "She wrote the letter in the office."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Reading 1: [While in the office] she wrote the letter. (locative modifies writing-event)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Reading 2: She wrote the letter [that was in the office]. (locative modifies letter)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   └── "Students studied math yesterday quickly."
│   │   │   │   │       ├── Reading 1: [Yesterday] students studied... [quickly] (time + manner modifying same event)
│   │   │   │   │       └── Reading 2: Yesterday, [quickly] students studied... (manner scoping over time - unusual)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **Coordinate Structure Constraint**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Elements in coordinate structures (like adjuncts) cannot be extracted alone
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples:
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "He spoke loudly and quickly."
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✓ Both adverbs together: "He spoke [loudly and quickly]."
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── ✗ Extract one: "*How did he speak [loudly and __]?" (violates coordination)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   └── "Students studied in the library and after class."
│   │   │   │   │       ├── ✓ Both PPs together: "studied [in the library and after class]"
│   │   │   │   │       └── ✗ Extract one: "*Where did they study [in __ and after class]?" (violates coordination)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **Obligatory vs. Optional Distinctions (Some Apparent Anomalies)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Most modifiers are optional, BUT some contexts require them
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples of "mandatory" modifiers:
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "He works hard." (hard is required as modifier; *"He works" is grammatical but different meaning)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "That is very obvious." (very is required for proper emphasis; "That is obvious" changes forcefulness)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "She lives far away." (far is required; "She lives away" sounds archaic/incomplete)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Note: These are usually pragmatically or stylistically mandatory, not syntactically
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   └── **Semantic Anomalies (Grammatical but Semantically Weird)**
│   │   │   │       ├── Some modifier + head combinations are grammatical but semantically odd
│   │   │   │       ├── Examples:
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "an invisible smile" - contradictory (smiles are inherently visible)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "a liquid rock" - oxymoronic (rocks are inherently solid)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "a silent scream" - paradoxical (screams are inherently audible)
│   │   │   │       │   └── "a truthful lie" - contradictory (lies are inherently false)
│   │   │   │       └── But such constructions can be used for artistic/figurative effect (metaphor, oxymoron)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **By Position (Summary)**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Pre-modifier**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Primarily adjectives/noun modifiers in nominal expressions
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Strictly ordered (Adjective Order Convention)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Restrictive semantic scope
│   │   │   │   │   └── Example: "a beautiful new red Italian table" (pre-nominal adjectives)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   └── **Post-modifier**
│   │   │   │       ├── Relative clauses, PPs, participle phrases in nominal expressions
│   │   │   │       ├── More flexible positioning; scope-dependent
│   │   │   │       ├── Can be restrictive or non-restrictive (in relative clauses)
│   │   │   │       └── Example: "the book that I read" / "students working late"
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **By Category Modified (Summary)**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Adjectival Modifier (modifies N)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Includes adjectives, participles, relative clauses, adjectival phrases
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Functional role: Add properties or limiting information to noun
│   │   │   │   │   └── Example: "a beautiful, mysterious, classic novel" / "the book that won the award"
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   └── **Adverbial Modifier (modifies V, A, Adv, or clause)**
│   │   │   │       ├── Includes adverbs, adverbial phrases, adverbial clauses, prepositional phrases
│   │   │   │       ├── Functional role: Add circumstantial information (time, place, manner, reason, condition, etc.)
│   │   │   │       └── Example: "She spoke quickly yesterday." / "He was very intelligent." / "They worked extremely carefully."
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   └── **By Form (Summary with Examples)**
│   │   │       ├── **Adjective Phrase (AP)**
│   │   │       │   ├── "very beautiful" / "quite intelligent" / "adorable and charming"
│   │   │       │   └── Can be pre- or post-nominal (rarely)
│   │   │       │
│   │   │       ├── **Prepositional Phrase (PP)**
│   │   │       │   ├── "in the park" / "with red hair" / "on the shelf" / "despite the rain"
│   │   │       │   └── Can modify nouns, predicates, or even other modifiers
│   │   │       │
│   │   │       ├── **Relative Clause**
│   │   │       │   ├── "who was smiling" / "that I had lost" / "which brought good luck"
│   │   │       │   └── Always post-nominal (unless free relative)
│   │   │       │
│   │   │       ├── **Participle Phrase**
│   │   │       │   ├── "running down the street" / "solved yesterday" / "having finished the work"
│   │   │       │   └── Can be noun-modifying or predicate-modifying
│   │   │       │
│   │   │       └── **Adverb/Adverb Phrase**
│   │   │           ├── "quickly" / "very well" / "quite carefully" / "almost always"
│   │   │           └── Modifies predicates, adjectives, adverbs, or entire clauses; multiple possible positions
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **c. Complement**
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Definition & Core Function**
│   │   │   │   ├── **What is a Complement?**
│   │   │   │   │   └── An obligatory or highly preferred constituent selected by the head that satisfies
│   │   │   │   │       the head's valency (argument structure). Complements are INTERNAL dependencies
│   │   │   │   │       that are required or strongly expected based on the head's lexical properties.
│   │   │   │   │       Unlike modifiers (optional additions), complements fulfill core semantic roles.
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Core Principles**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Selection:** Head selects its complements based on subcategorization frame
│   │   │   │   ├── **Obligatoriness:** Complements are required (or highly marked when omitted)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Theta Roles:** Complements satisfy semantic roles (PATIENT, THEME, GOAL, LOCATION, etc.)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Position:** Follow head (in English); position fixed by subcategorization
│   │   │   │   └── **Uniqueness:** One complement per role; multiple complements for multi-argument verbs
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Key Distinction: Complement vs. Modifier vs. Specifier**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Complements:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Selected by head (obligatory/strongly preferred)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Internal to phrase structure
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Position determined by head's subcategorization
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Satisfy theta roles from head
│   │   │   │   │   └── Examples: V's object (read + the book), A's PP (proud + of her), N's of-PP (destruction + of the city)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Modifiers (Adjuncts):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── NOT selected by head (optional, repeatable)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Can be added or removed without violating head's requirements
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Scope-bearing; interpretation affects whole phrase/clause
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Attach via adjunction rule
│   │   │   │   │   └── Examples: V's adverbial (read + quickly, in the library), N's adjective (beautiful + book)
│   │   │   │   └── **Specifiers:**
│   │   │   │       ├── External scope-bearing elements (leftmost)
│   │   │   │       ├── Determine reference or intensity
│   │   │   │       ├── One per phrase
│   │   │   │       ├── Position determined by X-bar structure
│   │   │   │       └── Examples: DP specifier (the, this, my), AdjP specifier (very), TP subject
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **5 Core Behavioral Characteristics of Complements**
│   │   │   │   ├── **1. SELECTION & SUBCATEGORIZATION**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Head selects specific complement types via subcategorization frame
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: V "give" requires TWO complements: [__ NP NP] → "give him the book"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: V "put" requires complements: [__ NP PP] → "put the book on the shelf"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: V "sleep" requires NO complements: [__] → "sleep" (ø complements)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Selection is INHERENT to head's lexical entry; not chosen by speaker
│   │   │   │   ├── **2. OBLIGATORINESS & ARGUMENT STRUCTURE**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Complements satisfy head's valency (number of argument slots)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Obligatory complements cannot be omitted without grammatical violation or meaning change
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Omission possible: *"She gave him" (incomplete; requires second complement)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Contrast with modifiers: "She ate" (ø object) vs. "She ate the apple" (with object)
│   │   │   │   │   │   - Both grammatical; object is complement but can be understood as implicit
│   │   │   │   │   │   - Some transitive verbs allow implicit object (understood from context)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Example obligatory: "She made him happy" (happy = object complement; cannot drop)
│   │   │   │   ├── **3. THETA-ROLE ASSIGNMENT**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Head assigns semantic roles (theta roles) to its complements
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Role assignment is FROM HEAD TO COMPLEMENT (not chosen by complement)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "John saw the movie"
│   │   │   │   │   │   - V "see" assigns AGENT role to subject, PATIENT role to object
│   │   │   │   │   │   - John = AGENT (subject), the movie = PATIENT (object/complement)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She gave him the book"
│   │   │   │   │   │   - V "give" assigns: AGENT (subject), GOAL (indirect object), THEME (direct object)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Example: "I am proud of her"
│   │   │   │   │       - A "proud" assigns EXPERIENCER (subject), THEME (PP complement)
│   │   │   │   ├── **4. STRUCTURAL POSITION**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Complements appear immediately after/following head (in English)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Order is fixed by subcategorization; not scrambled like some adjuncts
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "He put [the book] [on the shelf]" (DO before PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   - Never: *"He put on the shelf the book" (ungrammatical scrambling)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She gave [him] [the book]" (IO before DO)
│   │   │   │   │   │   - Variant: "She gave the book to him" (DO-to-IO with preposition)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Modifiers can float/reposition: "She quickly read the book" = "She read quickly the book"
│   │   │   │   │       - Complements cannot reposition without changing meaning/structure
│   │   │   │   └── **5. IMPOSSIBILITY OF EXTRACTION (Island Effects)**
│   │   │   │       ├── Complements are ISLANDS: cannot be extracted via movement (wh-questions, relativization)
│   │   │   │       ├── Example: "What did she give him __?" (direct object can be extracted)
│   │   │   │       │   - Acceptable because extracted object is complement; some extraction allowed for objects
│   │   │   │       ├── Example: "I doubt that she left." vs. "*That did I doubt __ she left?"
│   │   │   │       │   - Complement clause blocks extraction; that cannot be moved
│   │   │   │       ├── Example: "The destruction of the city" vs. "*Which city's destruction of __ was tragic?"
│   │   │   │       │   - NP complement blocks extraction of possession/of-phrase
│   │   │   │       └── Contrast: "She walked quickly" → "How did she walk __?" (adverbial extraction allowed)
│   │   │   │           - Adjuncts allow extraction more readily than strict complements
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Complements by Head Type (DETAILED TREATMENT)**
│   │   │   │   ├── **VERBAL COMPLEMENTS (V complements)**
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **TYPE 1: DIRECT OBJECT (Transitive Verb + NP Complement)**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Function & Structure:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── NP following transitive verb; receives PATIENT/THEME theta role
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answers "whom?" or "what?" after the verb
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Subcategorization: V[__ NP]
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Can be pronoun or full DP/NP
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She **read** [DP the book]." (DP object)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "He **saw** [NP something]." (NP object)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I **met** [DP her]." (pronoun object)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "They **discussed** [DP the problem]." (DP object with article)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "We **visited** [DP the museum yesterday]." (DP object; adjunct follows)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "She **loves** [NP music]." (bare NP object)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Omission Behavior:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Some transitive verbs allow omission (implicit object):
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - "She ate" (implicit: she ate [something]); contrast "She ate the apple"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - "He read" (implicit); "He read the book" (explicit)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Other transitives require object:
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - *"She understood" (ungrammatical or very awkward)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - "She understood the problem" (grammatical)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Context determines acceptability
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **Theta Role:** PATIENT (primary argument affected by action)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **TYPE 2: INDIRECT OBJECT (Ditransitive Verb + Two NP Complements)**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Function & Structure:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── First NP complement (GOAL/RECIPIENT); second NP is direct object
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Subcategorization: V[__ NP NP] (double object construction)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answers "to/for whom?"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Alternative: V[__ NP PP] with dative alternation (to/for-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Both indirect and direct object are REQUIRED
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples (Double Object):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She **gave** [DP him] [DP the book]." (IO + DO)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "They **sent** [DP her] [DP a letter]." (IO + DO)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "He **showed** [DP us] [DP the photo]." (IO + DO)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I **taught** [DP them] [DP mathematics]." (IO + DO)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "She **promised** [DP him] [DP help]." (IO + DO)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples (Dative Alternation with PP):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She gave [the book] [to him]." (DO + to-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "They sent [a letter] [to her]." (DO + to-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "He showed [the photo] [to us]." (DO + to-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Note: MEANING preserved but structure varies; both are complements
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Dative Alternation Rules:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Benefactive verbs (give, send, show, tell, teach, promise):
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - [NP NP] (DO + IO) ↔ [NP to-PP] (DO + to-IO)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - "give him the book" ↔ "give the book to him"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Causative verbs with benefactive (make, bake, build, write):
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - "bake me a cake" ↔ "bake a cake for me"
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - Note: FOR-alternation for benefactive
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Not all verbs alternate:
│   │   │   │   │   │   │       - "donate to the charity" (no *"donate the charity")
│   │   │   │   │   │   │       - "explain to her" (no *"explain her")
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **Theta Roles:** IO = GOAL/RECIPIENT; DO = THEME/PATIENT
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **TYPE 3: OBLIQUE OBJECT/PREPOSITIONAL OBJECT (V + PP Complement)**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Function & Structure:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── PP selected by verb's subcategorization
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Preposition specified by verb (not general modifier PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Subcategorization: V[__ PP] or V[__ NP PP] (if also direct object)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Answers locative/goal questions ("where?", "with what?", "about what?")
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── P is lexically determined by V (cannot be substituted)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples (V + PP, single complement):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She **depends** [on her parents]." (depend[__ on-PP])
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "They **talked** [about the problem]." (talk[__ about-PP])
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "He **succeeded** [in his goal]." (succeed[__ in-PP])
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I **disagreed** [with her]." (disagree[__ with-PP])
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She **relies** [on him]." (rely[__ on-PP])
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "They **engaged** [in sports]." (engage[__ in-PP])
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples (V + NP + PP, double complement):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She **put** [the book] [on the shelf]." (put[__ NP PP])
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "He **placed** [the vase] [in the corner]." (place[__ NP PP])
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "They **supplied** [the city] [with water]." (supply[__ NP PP])
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── "She **sprayed** [the wall] [with paint]." (spray[__ NP PP])
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Contrast: P-Complement vs. P-Adjunct**
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Complement PP (selected):** Cannot be removed
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - *"She depends" (incomplete; requires on-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - *"He put the book" (incomplete; requires location PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Adjunct PP (modifier, optional):** Can be removed
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - "She read the book" (omit adjunct: in the library)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   │   - "He ran quickly" (omit adjunct: in the morning)
│   │   │   │   │   │   │   └── Test: If PP can be omitted, it's likely a modifier; if required, it's complement
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **Theta Roles:** LOCATION (locative PP), THEME/INSTRUMENT (content-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   │   └── **TYPE 4: CLAUSAL COMPLEMENT (V + CP/TP Complement)**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── **Function & Structure:**
│   │   │   │   │       │   ├── Finite or non-finite clause as complement
│   │   │   │   │       │   ├── Subcategorization: V[__ CP] or V[__ TP]
│   │   │   │   │       │   ├── Completes proposition/assertion/belief content
│   │   │   │   │       │   ├── Types: that-clause, wh-clause, for-infinitive, bare infinitive
│   │   │   │   │       │   └── Theta role: PROPOSITION, BELIEF, KNOWLEDGE
│   │   │   │   │       ├── **FINITE CLAUSAL COMPLEMENTS:**
│   │   │   │   │       │   ├── **That-clause [CP ∅ [C' that [TP...]]]:**
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "She **believes** [that he left]." (that-complement of "believe")
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "He **said** [that he would return]." (that-complement of "say")
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "I **know** [that it is true]." (that-complement of "know")
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "They **realized** [that they were lost]." (that-complement of "realize")
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   └── Note: "that" is often optional in contact clauses: "She believes he left"
│   │   │   │   │       │   ├── **Wh-clause [CP [wh] [C' ∅ [TP...]]]:**
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "She **asked** [what he saw]." (wh-question as complement)
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "He **wondered** [where she went]." (wh-complement of "wonder")
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "I **don't know** [who called]." (wh-complement of "know")
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "They **discovered** [how it worked]." (wh-complement of "discover")
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   └── Note: wh-words cannot be omitted (unlike "that")
│   │   │   │   │       │   └── **If/Whether-clause:**
│   │   │   │   │       │       ├── "She **asked** [if/whether he left]." (conditional question as complement)
│   │   │   │   │       │       ├── "He **wondered** [whether to accept]." (whether-infinitive)
│   │   │   │   │       │       └── Generally complement of communication/cognition verbs
│   │   │   │   │       ├── **NON-FINITE CLAUSAL COMPLEMENTS:**
│   │   │   │   │       │   ├── **For-infinitive [CP ∅ [C' for [TP [him] [to leave]]]]]:**
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "I **want** [for him to leave]." (for-infinitive with overt subject)
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "It is unusual [for them to complain]." (extraposed for-infinitive)
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "She **prefers** [for us to wait]." (for-infinitive of "prefer")
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   └── Less common than null-subject infinitive
│   │   │   │   │       │   ├── **Null-subject Infinitive [TP [PRO] [to V...]]:**
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "She **wants** [to leave]." (null-subject infinitive; PRO = she)
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "He **tried** [to succeed]." (null-subject infinitive)
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "I **prefer** [to work alone]." (null-subject infinitive)
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   ├── "They **hope** [to return soon]." (null-subject infinitive)
│   │   │   │   │       │   │   └── Most common infinitive complement type
│   │   │   │   │       │   └── **Gerund [NP [V+ing]...]:**
│   │   │   │   │       │       ├── "She **enjoys** [reading]." (gerund complement of "enjoy")
│   │   │   │   │       │       ├── "He **dislikes** [working late]." (gerund of "dislike")
│   │   │   │   │       │       ├── "I **avoid** [talking about it]." (gerund of "avoid")
│   │   │   │   │       │       └── Gerund carries nominalization; some verbs select gerunds exclusively
│   │   │   │   │       └── **Verb Classes by Complement Type:**
│   │   │   │   │           ├── That-clause verbs: believe, say, know, think, realize, claim, suggest
│   │   │   │   │           ├── Wh-clause verbs: ask, wonder, know, discover, see, tell
│   │   │   │   │           ├── Infinitive verbs: want, try, hope, prefer, manage, promise, threaten
│   │   │   │   │           └── Gerund verbs: enjoy, avoid, dislike, finish, complete, risk, practice
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **NOMINAL COMPLEMENTS (N complements)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Function & Structure:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── PP or CP complement selected by noun; specifies relation/content
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Most common: of-PP (relational nouns)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Others: for-PP (purpose), about-PP (topic), to-PP (recipient in some dialects)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Nouns derived from verbs inherit verb's complement structure
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Of-PP Complements (Relational Nouns):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the **destruction** [of the city]" (destruction = event noun from "destroy")
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the **murder** [of the president]" (murder = agent noun from "murder")
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the **love** [of her mother]" (love = emotional relation)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the **fear** [of failure]" (fear = experiencer noun)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "the **arrival** [of the guests]" (arrival = event noun from "arrive")
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Contrast: "the destruction" (bare N, no complement) vs. "the destruction of the city" (N + complement)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **For-PP & About-PP Complements:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "a **reason** [for his absence]" (reason + for-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "a **concern** [about the future]" (concern + about-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "a **statement** [about the problem]" (statement + about-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "a **solution** [to the problem]" (solution + to-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Clausal Complements (Rare):**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "the **news** [that he left]" (that-clause complement of "news")
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "the **fact** [that she won]" (that-clause complement of "fact")
│   │   │   │   │       └── Limited to nouns with propositional/informational content
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **ADJECTIVAL COMPLEMENTS (A complements)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Function & Structure:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── PP or CP selected by adjective; specifies content/relation
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Most common: about-PP, of-PP, from-PP, with-PP
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Adjectives requiring complements are often derived from verbs/nouns
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Adjectives with complements often occur in predicate position (post-nominal)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **PP Complements (Most Common):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "proud [of her achievement]" (proud + of-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "aware [of the problem]" (aware + of-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "responsible [for the failure]" (responsible + for-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "angry [at him]" (angry + at-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "different [from the original]" (different + from-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "full [of energy]" (full + of-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "similar [to the example]" (similar + to-PP)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Position note: These adjectives are typically post-nominal: "She is proud of her achievement" (predicate), not *"the proud of achievement woman" (pre-nominal blocked)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Infinitive Complements (Less Common):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "eager [to help]" (eager + infinitive)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "reluctant [to leave]" (reluctant + infinitive)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "quick [to anger]" (quick + infinitive)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Generally adjectives of WILLINGNESS/READINESS or SPEED
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Clausal Complements (Very Rare):**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "certain [that he left]" (certain + that-clause)
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "aware [that she knows]" (aware + that-clause)
│   │   │   │   │       └── Limited to adjectives with propositional content
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   └── **PREPOSITIONAL COMPLEMENTS (P complements)**
│   │   │   │       ├── **Function & Structure:**
│   │   │   │       │   ├── NP/DP following preposition; completes spatial/temporal/relational meaning
│   │   │   │       │   ├── P always selects NP (object of preposition)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── Subcategorization: P[__ NP]
│   │   │   │       │   ├── Obligatory; cannot be omitted
│   │   │   │       │   └── Only one complement type for prepositions (unlike verbs)
│   │   │   │       ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "in [the room]" (P + NP)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "under [the table]" (P + NP)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "between [the trees]" (P + NP)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "without [help]" (P + NP)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "regarding [the issue]" (P + NP)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "according to [the report]" (P + NP)
│   │   │   │       │   └── "despite [the weather]" (P + NP)
│   │   │   │       ├── **Obligatoriness:**
│   │   │   │       │   ├── *"She walked in" (incomplete; requires object for locative in)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "She walked in [the park]" (complete; P has its NP complement)
│   │   │   │       │   └── Note: Some prepositions can be intransitive (particle use): "She went in" (in = particle-adverb)
│   │   │   │       └── **P-Stranding (Movement of Object):**
│   │   │   │           ├── Object can move in English (P-stranding allowed):
│   │   │   │           │   - "Which room did you walk in __?" (object moved; P remains)
│   │   │   │           │   - "The house that she grew up in __" (relative clause with stranding)
│   │   │   │           └── Not all languages allow P-stranding (German, Dutch: *"Das Haus, in dem..." but not all Ps strand)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Form-Based Classification of Complements**
│   │   │   │   ├── **NP/DP Complements (Most Common)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Function: Objects, relational arguments
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "read [the book]", "gave [him] [a gift]", "depend [on him]" (PP with NP object)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Can be pronoun: "She read [it]", "He saw [them]"
│   │   │   │   ├── **PP Complements**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Function: Locative, instrumental, relational arguments
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "put [on the shelf]", "talk [about the problem]", "proud [of her]"
│   │   │   │   │   └── Preposition selected by head (cannot substitute)
│   │   │   │   ├── **CP/TP Complements (Clausal)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Function: Propositional content, beliefs, commands
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "believe [that he left]", "asked [what she wanted]", "wants [to leave]"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Finite: that-clause, wh-clause, if-clause
│   │   │   │   │   └── Non-finite: infinitive (for-TP, bare TP), gerund
│   │   │   │   └── **AP Complements (Rare)**
│   │   │   │       ├── Function: Predicate adjectives in small clauses, result states
│   │   │   │       ├── Examples: "make [her] [happy]", "paint [the door] [blue]"
│   │   │   │       └── Always co-occurs with NP object; forms [NP AP] complex complement
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Selectional Restrictions & Constraints**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Semantic Selectional Restrictions**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Heads impose SEMANTIC constraints on complements (beyond syntactic type)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: V "eat" requires EDIBLE object
│   │   │   │   │   │   - "She ate the apple" (acceptable; apple = edible)
│   │   │   │   │   │   - *"She ate the table" (odd; table = inedible) [pragmatic anomaly]
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: V "convince" requires ANIMATE indirect object
│   │   │   │   │   │   - "She convinced him" (acceptable; him = animate)
│   │   │   │   │   │   - *"She convinced the table" (odd; table = inanimate)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Note: Selectional restrictions can be violated for metaphor/creativity
│   │   │   │   ├── **Extraction Restrictions (Island Effects)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Complement clauses block extraction of material from within
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: *"What did she doubt [that he saw __]?" (embedded object cannot extract)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: *"That did she think [__ he left]?" (embedded CP cannot extract)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Contrast: Adjunct clauses allow more extraction
│   │   │   │   ├── **Omission Constraints**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Some complements may be obligatory, others optional based on discourse
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She ate" (optional object; understood implicitly)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: *"She gave him" (obligatory second complement; cannot omit)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Verb-specific constraints (lexical entry determines)
│   │   │   │   └── **Coordination Constraints**
│   │   │   │       ├── Complements cannot generally be coordinated across verbs differently
│   │   │   │       ├── *"She read and filed the book" (different subcategorization)
│   │   │   │       └── "She read and enjoyed the book" (same subcategorization; coordination acceptable)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Identification Tests for Complements**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Test 1: OMISSION/NECESSITY TEST**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Remove the constituent; is the result ungrammatical or awkward?
│   │   │   │   │   ├── If omission is ungrammatical → likely COMPLEMENT
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "He put the book." *vs. "He put the book [on the shelf]." (on-PP is complement)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She read the book." *vs. "She read [quickly]." (quickly is adjunct; book is complement)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Example: "She depends on him." *vs. "She depends" (on-PP is obligatory complement)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Test 2: SUBSTITUTION TEST**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Can the constituent be replaced by a pronoun?
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Pronouns easily replace complements (NP objects → it, him, her, them)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She read [the book]" → "She read [it]" (it = pronoun for book)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She depends [on her parents]" → "She depends [on them]" (them = NP substitute)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Modifiers less easily replaced: "She read [quickly]" ✗ *"She read it" (it ≠ quickly)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Test 3: POSITION TEST**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Move the constituent; does it change meaning or become ungrammatical?
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Complements have FIXED position (cannot scramble)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "He put [the book] [on the shelf]"
│   │   │   │   │   │   - Cannot reorder: *"He put on the shelf the book" (ungrammatical)
│   │   │   │   │   │   - Cannot move: *"On the shelf he put the book" (altered meaning/grammaticality)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She read [the book]"
│   │   │   │   │   │   - Cannot reorder: *"She the book read" (ungrammatical)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Compare adjunct: "She read quickly" → "She quickly read" (movement allowed; adjunct)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Test 4: QUESTION TEST (What/Whom/Where)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Answer the question formed by the head; does the constituent answer it?
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: Q: "What did she read?" A: "[The book]" (answers; book = complement)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: Q: "What did she depend on?" A: "[On her parents]" (answers; on-PP = complement)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: Q: "When did she read?" A: "[Yesterday]" (answers; yesterday = adjunct/adverbial)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Complements specifically answer core argument questions
│   │   │   │   └── **Test 5: SUBCATEGORIZATION TEST**
│   │   │   │       ├── Does the head select this specific type of constituent?
│   │   │   │       ├── Check lexicon/dictionary: What frame does the verb/adjective require?
│   │   │   │       ├── Example: V "put" has frame [__ NP PP]; thus both NP and PP are complements
│   │   │   │       ├── Example: V "sleep" has frame [__]; thus no complements needed
│   │   │   │       └── Example: A "proud" has frame [__ of-PP]; thus of-PP is complement
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   └── **Summary: Obligatory vs. Optional Distinction**
│   │   │       ├── **OBLIGATORY COMPLEMENTS:**
│   │   │       │   ├── Cannot be omitted without violating grammaticality
│   │   │       │   ├── Examples:
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Direct objects of transitive verbs: "She read [the book]" (ø object = ungrammatical for some verbs)
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Both complements of ditransitives: "She gave [him] [the book]" (cannot omit either)
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Oblique objects: "She depends [on her parents]" (cannot omit on-PP)
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Adjectival complements: "proud [of her]" (of-PP required for sense)
│   │   │       │   │   └── Preposition's object: "in [the room]" (cannot omit NP)
│   │   │       │   └── Marked in lexicon with subcategorization frame
│   │   │       ├── **OPTIONAL COMPLEMENTS:**
│   │   │       │   ├── Can be omitted; understood implicitly from context
│   │   │       │   ├── Examples:
│   │   │       │   │   ├── Implicit objects of some transitives: "She ate" (object understood; "She ate [something]")
│   │   │       │   │   ├── "He finished" (object understood; "He finished [it]")
│   │   │       │   │   └── "They read" (object understood; typical "read" requires object, but context allows)
│   │   │       │   └── Marked in lexicon as taking optional complement; pragmatically determined
│   │   │       └── **Note:** Distinction is gradable; depends on verb's lexical entry and discourse context
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **d. Adverbial**
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Definition & Core Function**
│   │   │   │   ├── **What is an Adverbial?**
│   │   │   │   │   └── A clause-level or sentence-level constituent (as opposed to adverbial modifiers of VPs)
│   │   │   │   │       that provides information about circumstances, conditions, or frames for the entire
│   │   │   │   │       proposition. Adverbials are sentence-level modifiers that contribute to the
│   │   │   │   │       interpretation of WHEN, WHERE, HOW, WHY, or UNDER WHAT CONDITIONS an action/state occurs.
│   │   │   │   │       Unlike VP-level adverbial modifiers, adverbials have broader scope.
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Core Principles**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Optionality:** Adverbials are optional; sentences are grammatical without them
│   │   │   │   ├── **Scope:** Apply to entire clause/proposition (broader than VP-modifiers)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Position:** Can appear sentence-initial, sentence-final, or mid-clause
│   │   │   │   ├── **Multiple:** Can stack/co-occur (multiple adverbials in one clause)
│   │   │   │   ├── **Semantic Interpretation:** Each adverbial carries specific semantic content (temporal, locative, etc.)
│   │   │   │   └── **Integration:** Some adverbials integrate tightly with clause semantics; others are peripheral
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Key Distinction: Adverbial vs. VP-Modifier vs. Adjunct**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Adverbials (Sentence-level):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── SENTENCE-level scope (modify entire proposition)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Optional, repeatable
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Positions: initial, final, or mid-sentence
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "Yesterday, she left." / "She left yesterday." / "She, yesterday, left."
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Semantic types: time, place, frequency, duration, reason, purpose, condition, concession, result
│   │   │   │   │   └── Can often be deleted without changing core grammaticality
│   │   │   │   ├── **VP-Modifiers (Phrase-level):**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── VP-level scope (modify verb phrase only)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Often manner adverbs or VP-internal adverbials
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "She quickly read the book." (quickly = how she read)
│   │   │   │   │   └── More tightly bound to verb action
│   │   │   │   └── **Complements (Required):**
│   │   │   │       ├── Selected by head (obligatory)
│   │   │   │       ├── Not optional
│   │   │   │       └── Must satisfy head's subcategorization
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **5 Core Behavioral Characteristics**
│   │   │   │   ├── **1. OPTIONALITY & REPEATABILITY**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Adverbials can be freely added and removed without violating grammaticality
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Multiple adverbials can co-occur in same clause (stacking)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She left yesterday." (complete sentence without adverbial)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She left." (also complete; adverbial is fully optional)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She left yesterday at noon in the morning rain." (3 adverbials stacked)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Contrast: *"She gave" (complement missing; ungrammatical)
│   │   │   │   ├── **2. POSITION FLEXIBILITY**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Can appear in multiple positions: sentence-initial, sentence-final, or within clause
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Position often affects scope/interpretation (not same as adjuncts)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "Yesterday she left." (focus on timing)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She left yesterday." (adverbial as afterthought)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She, yesterday, left." (mid-position; rare/marked)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Position determines interpretive scope (relates to prosody, emphasis)
│   │   │   │   ├── **3. SEMANTIC COMPOSITION**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Each adverbial carries specific semantic content (not just any modification)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Semantic type determines what question it answers (when? where? why? how?)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She left quickly." (MANNER - how did she leave?)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She left yesterday." (TIME - when did she leave?)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She left because she was angry." (REASON - why did she leave?)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Semantic content is inherent to adverbial; cannot be freely changed
│   │   │   │   ├── **4. ORDERING CONSTRAINTS**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Multiple adverbials follow universal ordering preferences (not free scrambling)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── General order: MANNER > LOCATION > TIME (but language-specific patterns vary)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "She spoke **politely** **in the room** **yesterday**." (manner > location > time; natural)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Example: *"She spoke **yesterday** **in the room** **politely**." (time > location > manner; marked/unnatural)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Violations create infelicitousness but not strict ungrammaticality
│   │   │   │   └── **5. INTERACTION WITH CONTEXT & DISCOURSE**
│   │   │   │       ├── Adverbials can be topic-comment or focus-establishing
│   │   │   │       ├── Sentence-initial adverbials often set discourse context/frame
│   │   │   │       ├── Example: "On Monday, she arrived." (on Monday = discourse frame)
│   │   │   │       ├── Example: "She arrived on Monday." (on Monday = final modifier, less framing)
│   │   │   │       └── Discourse context determines which adverbials are appropriate/expected
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Adverbials by Semantic Type (10+ Major Categories)**
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **1. TEMPORAL ADVERBIALS (WHEN?)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:** Locate action/state in TIME; answer "when did it happen?"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** "She left **yesterday**." / "He will arrive **tomorrow**." / "They met **last week**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Position:** Can initiate clause frame: "Yesterday, she left."
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **2. LOCATIVE ADVERBIALS (WHERE?)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:** Locate action/state in SPACE; answer "where did it happen?"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** "She worked **in the library**." / "He stayed **at home**." / "They met **outside**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Position:** Often appear final or mid-clause
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **3. FREQUENCY ADVERBIALS (HOW OFTEN?)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:** Indicate frequency/regularity; answer "how often does it happen?"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** "She **often** calls." / "He **sometimes** visits." / "They **rarely** meet."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Position:** Mid-clause (after subject in declaratives): "She often calls."
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **4. DURATION ADVERBIALS (HOW LONG?)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:** Indicate LENGTH/SPAN of action; answer "how long does it last?"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** "She stayed **for two weeks**." / "He worked **for hours**." / "She lived **for years**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Position:** Often appear final
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **5. MANNER ADVERBIALS (HOW?)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:** Describe HOW action is performed; answer "how is it done?"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** "She spoke **politely**." / "He walked **slowly**." / "They worked **efficiently**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Position:** Typically mid-clause or final
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **6. REASON/CAUSAL ADVERBIALS (WHY?)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:** Express REASON or CAUSE for action; answer "why did it happen?"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** "She left **because of illness**." / "He apologized **due to his mistake**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Position:** Often initiate clauses (initial position common)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **7. PURPOSE ADVERBIALS (FOR WHAT PURPOSE?)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:** Express PURPOSE or GOAL; answer "what's the purpose/goal?"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** "She studied **in order to pass**." / "They traveled **to visit relatives**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Distinction:** Purpose (intentional goal) vs. Reason (past cause)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **8. CONDITIONAL ADVERBIALS (IF... / PROVIDED THAT...)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:** Express CONDITIONS or HYPOTHETICALS for clause truth
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** "**If** you study, you'll pass." / "**Unless** it rains, we'll go."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Position:** Often appear initial (setting up condition)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **9. CONCESSIVE ADVERBIALS (ALTHOUGH... / DESPITE...)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:** Express CONTRAST or CONCESSION despite contrary expectation
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:** "**Although** she was tired, she continued." / "**Despite** her fatigue, she continued."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Position:** Often appear initial or mid-clause
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   └── **10. RESULT/CONSEQUENCE ADVERBIALS (SO THAT...)**
│   │   │   │       ├── **Definition & Function:** Express RESULT or CONSEQUENCE of main clause action
│   │   │   │       ├── **Examples:** "She studied hard, **so** she passed." / "He spoke so clearly **that everyone understood**."
│   │   │   │       └── **Distinction:** Result (actual consequence) vs. Purpose (intended goal)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Forms of Adverbials (7 Major Form Types)**
│   │   │   │   ├── **1. ADVERB PHRASES (AdvP):** Simple adverbs or degree-modified adverbs
│   │   │   │   │   └── Examples: "She spoke **loudly**." / "He arrived **very late**."
│   │   │   │   ├── **2. PREPOSITIONAL PHRASES (PP):** Preposition + NP
│   │   │   │   │   └── Examples: "She worked **in the library**." / "They met **at midnight**."
│   │   │   │   ├── **3. FINITE CLAUSES:** Subordinate clauses with tense/mood
│   │   │   │   │   └── Examples: "She left **because she was angry**." / "They'll come **if you invite them**."
│   │   │   │   ├── **4. INFINITIVE CLAUSES:** Non-finite infinitival clauses
│   │   │   │   │   └── Examples: "She studied **in order to pass**." / "They came **to help**."
│   │   │   │   ├── **5. PARTICIPLE CLAUSES (-ING and -ED):** Non-finite participial forms
│   │   │   │   │   └── Examples: "**Finishing her homework**, she left." / "**Exhausted by the effort**, they rested."
│   │   │   │   ├── **6. NOUN PHRASES:** Bare or modified NPs functioning as adverbials
│   │   │   │   │   └── Examples: "She left **yesterday**." / "They met **this morning**."
│   │   │   │   └── **7. COMPARATIVE CLAUSES:** Like or as constructions
│   │   │   │       └── Examples: "He ran **like the wind**." / "She acted **as if she owned the place**."
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Position and Scope of Adverbials**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Sentence-Initial:** Set discourse frame/context; "Yesterday, she left."
│   │   │   │   ├── **Mid-Clause:** Appear between subject and verb; "She **often** calls."
│   │   │   │   └── **Sentence-Final:** Added detail/emphasis; "She left **yesterday**."
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **Ordering of Multiple Adverbials**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Universal Preference:** MANNER > LOCATION > TIME
│   │   │   │   ├── **Natural Order:** "She spoke **politely** **in the room** **yesterday**."
│   │   │   │   └── **Marked Order:** *"She spoke **yesterday** **in the room** **politely**."
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   └── **Identification Tests for Adverbials**
│   │   │       ├── **Test 1: OPTIONALITY** - Remove adverbial; is sentence still grammatical?
│   │   │       ├── **Test 2: SEMANTIC QUESTION** - What question does it answer? When/where/why/how?
│   │   │       ├── **Test 3: MOBILITY** - Can it move to different positions without changing grammaticality?
│   │   │       ├── **Test 4: PARENTHETICAL** - Can it be set off in commas without changing meaning?
│   │   │       └── **Test 5: SEMANTIC CATEGORY** - Maps to one of 10+ adverbial types?
│   │   │
│   │   └── **e. Specifier**
│   │       │
│   │       ├── **Definition & Core Function**
│   │       │   ├── **What is a Specifier?**
│   │       │   │   └── The leftmost constituent in a phrase, located in the specifier (Spec) position
│   │       │   │       of the X-bar structure. Specifiers modify, identify, or determine the reference
│   │       │   │       of the maximal phrase or the selection/interpretation of the head. They are
│   │       │   │       EXTERNAL modifiers that apply scope over the entire phrase.
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **Core Principles**
│   │       │   │   ├── **Position:** Leftmost element in phrase (Spec position before X')
│   │       │   │   ├── **Function:** Scope-bearing; determines phrase interpretation/reference
│   │       │   │   ├── **Uniqueness:** Typically one specifier per phrase (though some have empty Spec)
│   │       │   │   ├── **Head-Dependent:** Type depends on head category (D for NP, Deg for AdjP, etc.)
│   │       │   │   └── **Selection:** Head/category may select specific specifier types via subcategorization
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **Key Distinction: Specifier vs. Modifier vs. Complement**
│   │       │   │   ├── **Specifiers:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── EXTERNAL scope-bearing elements
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Leftmost position (Spec of XP)
│   │       │   │   │   ├── ONE per phrase (typically)
│   │       │   │   │   ├── DETERMINE/QUANTIFY reference or intensity
│   │       │   │   │   └── Examples: "the" in "the book", "very" in "very beautiful", "he" in "he left"
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Modifiers/Adjuncts:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── INTERNAL adding elements
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Flexible positions (before/after head, clause-level)
│   │       │   │   │   ├── MULTIPLE possible (stacking)
│   │       │   │   │   ├── OPTIONAL entirely
│   │       │   │   │   └── Examples: "red" in "red book", "quickly" in "left quickly"
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   └── **Complements:**
│   │       │   │       ├── INTERNAL required elements
│   │       │   │       ├── Right-adjacent to head (follows head)
│   │       │   │       ├── UNIQUE to head type (determined by subcategorization)
│   │       │   │       ├── OBLIGATORY for many heads
│   │       │   │       └── Examples: "the book" in "read the book", "to Paris" in "went to Paris"
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   └── **X-bar Position: Where Specifiers Fit**
│   │       │       ├── **Structure:** [XP Specifier [X' X Complement(s) Adjunct(s)]]
│   │       │       ├── Specifier is sister to X' (bar level)
│   │       │       ├── Specifier is daughter of XP (maximal projection)
│   │       │       ├── Specifier is external to the head's immediate structure
│   │       │       └── Scope: Quantifies or determines the entire [X' ...] constituent
│   │       │
│   │       ├── **Core Behavioral Characteristics**
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **1. Uniqueness & Singularity**
│   │       │   │   ├── Spec position can typically hold only ONE element
│   │       │   │   ├── Multiple specifiers are unusual (some languages allow them; English generally doesn't)
│   │       │   │   ├── If present, single specifier determines entire phrase reference/scope
│   │       │   │   ├── Empty Spec (∅) is common in embedded or non-finite contexts
│   │       │   │   └── Examples:
│   │       │   │       ├── "the book" - ONE specifier (the)
│   │       │   │       ├── "very beautiful" - ONE specifier (very)
│   │       │   │       ├── "he left" - ONE specifier/subject (he)
│   │       │   │       ├── ✗ "*the a book" (two determiners - ungrammatical)
│   │       │   │       └── "[∅ laughing loudly]" - Empty subject in gerund (empty Spec)
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **2. Scope Domination**
│   │       │   │   ├── Specifier scope extends over entire phrase (head + complements + adjuncts)
│   │       │   │   ├── Quantifiers in Spec have wide scope; modifiers within phrase have narrow scope
│   │       │   │   ├── Creates semantic dominance: specifier determines what is being quantified
│   │       │   │   ├── Interaction with other scope-bearers (only, even, etc.) can create ambiguity
│   │       │   │   └── Examples:
│   │       │   │       ├── "the boys" - "the" scopes over "boys" (definite reference to specific boys)
│   │       │   │       ├── "some students read every book" - "some" (Spec of DP) has scope over NP
│   │       │   │       ├── "very intelligent" - "very" (Spec of AdjP) scopes over entire adjective meaning
│   │       │   │       └── Scope interaction: "Every student read some books." vs. "Some books every student read."
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **3. Determination vs. Modification**
│   │       │   │   ├── Specifiers DETERMINE how the phrase is interpreted (type/reference/quantity)
│   │       │   │   ├── Modifiers ADD properties but don't change what is being talked about
│   │       │   │   ├── Distinction: Specifier = "what kind of entity/quantity" vs. Modifier = "what additional property"
│   │       │   │   ├── Changing specifier changes reference; changing modifier changes description only
│   │       │   │   └── Examples:
│   │       │   │       ├── "the red book" - "the" (Spec, determines definiteness) + "red" (modifier, adds property)
│   │       │   │       ├── "Change 'the' to 'a'" → different reference (indefinite vs. definite)
│   │       │   │       ├── "Change 'red' to 'blue'" → same reference, different property
│   │       │   │       └── "very intelligent student" vs. "quite intelligent student" - "very/quite" (Spec) change intensity
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **4. Category Licensing**
│   │       │   │   ├── Specifier type is determined by head category (D licenses D-type specifiers in DP)
│   │       │   │   ├── Not all phrases require specifiers; not all heads license them
│   │       │   │   ├── Null/empty Spec is grammatical in many contexts
│   │       │   │   ├── Some languages/phrases have obligatory specifiers; others have optional
│   │       │   │   └── Examples:
│   │       │   │       ├── DP heads (D like "the") license Determiner-type specifiers
│   │       │   │       ├── Adjective heads license Degree specifiers (very, quite, too)
│   │       │   │       ├── TP licenses Subject specifiers (external argument)
│   │       │   │       ├── Many embedded clauses have ∅ subject (empty Spec of TP)
│   │       │   │       └── CP heads license wh-phrases (question operators) in Spec
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **5. Structural Rigidity**
│   │       │   │   ├── Specifiers have FIXED position (leftmost in phrase)
│   │       │   │   ├── Cannot move within phrase or be scrambled with other constituents
│   │       │   │   ├── Movement to Spec requires syntactic operations (like wh-movement, raising)
│   │       │   │   ├── Specifier-head agreement is common (subject-verb agreement, determiner-noun agreement)
│   │       │   │   └── Examples:
│   │       │   │       ├── Determiner must precede noun: ✓ "the book" ✗ "book the"
│   │       │   │       ├── Degree word must precede adjective: ✓ "very beautiful" ✗ "beautiful very"
│   │       │   │       ├── Subject precedes verb: ✓ "He left." ✗ "Left he."
│   │       │   │       ├── Wh-phrases move to Spec of CP in questions: "What did he see?" (not "He did see what?")
│   │       │   │       └── Determiner-noun agreement: ✓ "this book" / ✓ "these books" ✗ "this books"
│   │       │   │
│   │       ├── **Specifier Types by Phrase Category**
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **A. SPECIFIER IN DP/NP (Determiner Phrase)**
│   │       │   │   │   
│   │       │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Specifier position in DP hosts determiners and quantifiers
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Function: Mark definiteness, specificity, quantification of the NP
│   │       │   │   │   ├── These are the most commonly discussed specifiers in English
│   │       │   │   │   └── Obligatory in most definite NPs; optional in indefinite/bare plurals
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **1. ARTICLES (Definite & Indefinite Determiners)**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Definite Article "the":**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Function: Marks NP as definite, presupposing specific/known referent
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Triggers presupposition: listener/reader already knows which entity
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I saw the book you mentioned." (listener knows which book)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "The sun rose this morning." (unique entity - sun)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   └── "He entered the room." (specific room in context)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── Position: Always Spec of DP; always precedes noun (or modifiers)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   └── **Indefinite Articles "a" / "an":**
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Function: Marks NP as indefinite, introducing new/unknown referent
│   │       │   │   │       ├── No presupposition of specific identity (listener doesn't need to know which one)
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "I saw a book yesterday." (unknown to listener - any book)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "A man walked in." (introducing new character)
│   │       │   │   │       │   └── "She is an engineer." (identifies role/class)
│   │       │   │   │       └── Position: Spec of DP, always precedes noun
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **2. DEMONSTRATIVES (Pointing/Spatial Reference)**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Proximal Demonstratives (Near speaker):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "this" (singular): "this book", "this idea"
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "these" (plural): "these students", "these problems"
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Function: Point to nearby/immediate entities; can create emphasis
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │   │       ├── "This laptop is excellent." (one I'm pointing to)
│   │       │   │   │   │       ├── "These days, everything is connected." (time period including present)
│   │       │   │   │       └── "This is the best solution." (emphasizing current proposal)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   └── **Distal Demonstratives (Far from speaker):**
│   │       │   │   │       ├── "that" (singular): "that book", "that idea"
│   │       │   │   │       ├── "those" (plural): "those students", "those problems"
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Function: Point to distant/removed entities; can contrast with proximal
│   │       │   │   │       └── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │           ├── "That building is historic." (one far away or previously mentioned)
│   │       │   │   │           ├── "Those days are gone." (past time period)
│   │       │   │   │           ├── Contrast: "This solution is good, but that one is better."
│   │       │   │   │           └── "That's ridiculous!" (referring to previously stated idea)
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **3. POSSESSIVES (Ownership/Association)**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Dependent Possessives (Pronouns + 's):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "my, your, his, her, its, our, their"
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Function: Mark possession, association, or relationship with the noun
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "my book" (I own the book)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "her mother" (mother of her)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "its purpose" (purpose belonging to it)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "our responsibility" (belonging to our group)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   └── "their achievements" (achievements of them)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── Position: Spec of DP (same position as articles)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   └── **Genitive NP ('s possessive):**
│   │       │   │   │       ├── "John's book", "my friend's house", "the president's speech"
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Function: More complex possessive construction; NP with genitive case marker
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "the president's office" (office of the president)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "my sister's wedding" (wedding of my sister)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "the dog's tail" (tail of the dog)
│   │       │   │   │       │   └── "Shakespeare's plays" (plays of Shakespeare)
│   │       │   │   │       └── Note: Genitive NP may function differently in structure; sometimes analyzed as adjunct or modifier
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **4. QUANTIFIERS (Quantity/Amount Specifiers)**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Definite Quantifiers (Specific quantity presumed known):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "some, many, few, several, multiple, numerous, most, all"
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "some students passed" (unknown number but specific subset)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "many cities exist" (large quantity)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "few people understand" (small quantity)
