│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "several options remain" (multiple but discrete count)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "most countries agreed" (majority/bulk)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   └── "all students participated" (complete set)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── Function: Introduce quantity information; scope-bearing for logical interpretation
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   └── **Universal Quantifiers (All-inclusive):**
│   │       │   │   │       ├── "all, every, each, either, neither"
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "every student must attend" (each individual in complete set)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "each book costs $20" (one by one, individually)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "either option works" (one or the other, choice)
│   │       │   │   │       │   └── "neither solution is perfect" (not one, not the other)
│   │       │   │   │       └── Note: Universal quantifiers are particularly scope-important for logical readings
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **5. CARDINALS & ORDINALS (Number & Order)**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Cardinal Numbers (Count/Quantity):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "one, two, three, ... hundred, thousand, etc."
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Function: Specify exact quantity; often function as specifiers (though sometimes modifiers)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "three books" (exactly three)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "twenty students" (exactly twenty)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "one hundred fifty pages" (exact count)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   └── "I have five apples" (specific number)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── Position: Usually Spec of DP or sometimes modifier position (adjective)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   └── **Ordinal Numbers (Order/Position):**
│   │       │   │   │       ├── "first, second, third, ... last, next"
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Function: Indicate position/sequence rather than quantity
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "the first book" (ordinal + definite article)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "his third marriage" (ordinal possessive)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "the next meeting" (ordinal temporal reference)
│   │       │   │   │       │   └── "her second chance" (ordinal counting events)
│   │       │   │   │       └── Note: Ordinals typically appear as modifiers, not specifiers (but "the first", "the third" exist)
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   └── **Summary of DP Specifiers:**
│   │       │   │       ├── Determiner position (Spec of DP) hosts articles, demonstratives, possessives, quantifiers
│   │       │   │       ├── Exactly one from this set typically appears (✗ "*the this book", ✗ "*the some students")
│   │       │   │       ├── Absence allowed in some contexts (bare plurals: "Books are important.", generics: "Cats are felines.")
│   │       │   │       └── Critical for definiteness, specificity, and quantification interpretation
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **B. SPECIFIER IN TP/CLAUSE (Subject Specifier)**
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── The subject is the specifier of TP (Tense Phrase / IP - Inflection Phrase)
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Structurally: [TP Spec(Subject) [T' T [VP V-complements-adjuncts]]]
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Function: External argument of the predicate; binds variables in the proposition
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Controls subject-verb agreement and verb inflection
│   │       │   │   │   └── Required in finite clauses (except imperatives); often empty (∅) in non-finite contexts
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Properties:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Position:** Leftmost in TP; before tense marking and verb
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Form:** Usually DP (noun phrase) or other constituent that can be argument
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Agreement:** Triggers verb agreement (3rd person singular -s in present)
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Case:** Nominative case in languages with case; English shows in pronouns (he, she, we vs. him, her, us)
│   │       │   │   │   └── **Distribution:** Obligatory in finite clauses, optional/null in infinitival/gerund contexts
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Overt Subjects (filled Spec of TP):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "He left early." (subject = "he")
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "The students studied hard." (subject = "the students")
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "She will return next week." (subject = "she", future tense)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "It is raining." (subject = "it", even if non-referential)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── "This book is interesting." (subject = "this book")
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **Null/Empty Subjects (∅ in Spec of TP):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "[∅ to leave early] is impossible" (infinitive - null subject)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "[∅ reading quickly] improved my comprehension" (gerund - null subject)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "[∅ Having finished, we left]" (absolute participle - null subject)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "He said [∅ to call me later]" (embedded infinitive - subject controlled by object)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── "Do [∅ eat your vegetables]!" (imperative - null subject, understood as "you")
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   └── **Subject-Verb Agreement:**
│   │       │   │       ├── Subject determines verb inflection:
│   │       │   │       │   ├── "I am", "you are", "he/she/it is" (present of be)
│   │       │   │       │   ├── "I walk", "she walks" (present tense -s)
│   │       │   │       │   ├── "They were", "it was" (past of be)
│   │       │   │       │   └── "The students participate" vs. "The student participates" (number agreement)
│   │       │   │       └── Demonstrates subject's control over TP inflection
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **C. SPECIFIER IN ADJP/ADVP (Degree Specifiers)**
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Degree words (intensifiers) are specifiers of adjective and adverb phrases
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Function: Modify the intensity/degree of adjective or adverb meaning
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Must appear immediately before the head (A or Adv)
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Scalar: Apply to gradable (measurable/comparative) predicates
│   │       │   │   │   └── Examples: very, quite, rather, so, too, enough, extremely, incredibly, remarkably
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Degree Words by Type:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **INTENSIFIERS (Increase degree):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "very, extremely, incredibly, remarkably, especially, particularly"
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "very beautiful" (high intensity)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "extremely intelligent" (very high degree)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "remarkably fast" (notably fast)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "especially talented" (particularly so)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   └── "incredibly expensive" (very high cost)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── Function: Scale up the degree/intensity; make predicate stronger
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **DIMINISHERS (Decrease degree):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "quite, rather, somewhat, fairly, relatively, almost, nearly, somewhat"
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "quite beautiful" (moderately beautiful)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "rather tired" (somewhat tired; British: more positive; American: more negative)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "fairly good" (moderately good)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "relatively expensive" (expensive compared to something)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   └── "almost finished" (nearly at limit of scale)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── Function: Scale down the degree; soften predicate strength
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **EXTREMITY MARKERS (Emphasize extremes):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "so, too, enough, just, simply"
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "so beautiful!" (emphatic degree; often triggers consequence clause)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "too expensive" (exceeds acceptable limit; negative evaluation)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "enough time" (sufficient amount)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   ├── "just right" (exactly appropriate)
│   │       │   │   │   │   │   └── "simply wonderful" (emphatic; absolute)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── Function: Mark boundary/limit conditions on scale
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   └── **COMPARATIVE MARKERS (For comparison contexts):**
│   │       │   │   │       ├── "more, less, as, much, far, a little, a bit"
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "more beautiful" (comparative form)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "less expensive" (lower degree)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "as intelligent" (equal degree)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "much faster" (significantly greater)
│   │       │   │   │       │   ├── "far more difficult" (much greater degree)
│   │       │   │   │       │   └── "a little better" (slightly greater)
│   │       │   │   │       └── Function: Modify degree in comparative/superlative contexts
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Position and Selection:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **In Adjective Phrases (AdjP):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "very beautiful" / "incredibly smart" / "too expensive" / "quite tall"
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Degree word immediately precedes adjective
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── Can be followed by adjective complement: "proud of her work" → "very proud of her work"
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **In Adverb Phrases (AdvP):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "very quickly" / "incredibly slowly" / "too hastily" / "quite well"
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Degree word immediately precedes adverb
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── Can modify how-adverbs, not usually time/place adverbs
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── Exception: "quite yesterday" is impossible (temporal adverbs aren't gradable)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   └── **Semantic Constraints:**
│   │       │   │       ├── Can only modify GRADABLE predicates (those with measurable scale)
│   │       │   │       ├── ✓ "very tall", "quite beautiful", "extremely fast" (gradable)
│   │       │   │       ├── ✗ "very married", "quite dead", "extremely simultaneous" (non-gradable; absolute properties)
│   │       │   │       ├── ✗ "very yesterday", "extremely often" (temporal/frequency adverbs not gradable)
│   │       │   │       └── Reason: Non-gradable predicates don't have a scale to be modified
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   ├── **D. SPECIFIER IN CP (Complementizer Phrase - Question Operators)**
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Definition & Function:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Wh-phrases (who, what, where, when, why, how, which) appear in Spec of CP
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Function: Mark question formation; move to front (via wh-movement operation)
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Create interrogative scope; determine type of question
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Interact with C (complementizer): related/unrelated elements (C and Spec must be compatible)
│   │       │   │   │   └── Create the surface question word-order in English (wh-fronting)
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Wh-Specifiers (Question Words):**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **WHO/WHOM (Personal):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "Who called?" (subject question)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "Whom did you see?" (object question; formal/archaic)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "Who did you give the book to?" (object of preposition; preposition stranding)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── "Whose book is this?" (possessive wh-question)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **WHAT (Non-personal):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "What happened?" (subject question)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "What did you see?" (object question)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "What are you doing?" (object of progressive)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── "What book did you read?" (wh-determiner modifying noun)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **WHERE (Location):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "Where is the book?" (location of object)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "Where did you go?" (destination)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "Where does he work?" (place of work)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── "Whereabouts is she?" (more specific location)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **WHEN (Temporal):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "When did it happen?" (time specification)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "When will you return?" (future time)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "When is the party?" (temporal reference)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── "When did you last see him?" (past time)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **WHY (Reason/Purpose):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "Why did you leave?" (reason/motivation)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "Why are you crying?" (cause question)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "Why not?" (short form questioning purpose)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── "Why in the world?" (emphatic reason question)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   ├── **HOW (Manner/Degree):**
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "How did you do it?" (manner question)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "How are you?" (status/feeling)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "How many students?" (quantity question)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "How much money?" (amount question)
│   │       │   │   │   │   ├── "How often?" (frequency question)
│   │       │   │   │   │   └── "How tall?" (degree question)
│   │       │   │   │   │
│   │       │   │   │   └── **WHICH (Selection from set):**
│   │       │   │   │       ├── "Which book did you read?" (selection question)
│   │       │   │   │       ├── "Which students passed?" (selection among students)
│   │       │   │   │       ├── "Which one?" (selection from implied set)
│   │       │   │   │       └── Often implies closed set; contrast with "What" (open set)
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   ├── **Movement to Spec of CP:**
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Wh-questions require wh-movement: wh-phrase moves to Spec of CP
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Base position (within clause): "You saw what?" (echo question - base order)
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Moved position (Spec of CP): "What did you see?" (standard question)
│   │       │   │   │   ├── Creates interrogative C' that agrees with wh-specifier
│   │       │   │   │   └── Examples:
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Base: "He left [why]?" → Moved: "[Why] did he leave?"
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Base: "She visited [which place]?" → Moved: "[Which place] did she visit?"
│   │       │   │   │       ├── Base: "He likes [what]?" → Moved: "[What] does he like?"
│   │       │   │   │       └── Wh-movement is obligatory in English (ungrammatical to leave at base in standard register)
│   │       │   │   │
│   │       │   │   └── **Embedded Questions (Spec of CP still active):**
│   │       │   │       ├── "I asked [who left]." (wh-specifier in embedded CP)
│   │       │   │       ├── "She wonders [where he went]." (wh-specifier in embedded CP)
│   │       │   │       ├── "Tell me [what you want]." (wh-specifier in embedded CP)
│   │       │   │       └── Wh-phrases don't move as far (stay in embedded Spec of CP)
│   │       │   │
│   │       │   └── **E. SPECIFIER IN PP (Rare)**
│   │       │       ├── **Definition & Context:**
│   │       │       │   ├── PP (Prepositional Phrase) rarely takes specifiers
│   │       │       │   ├── P is a unique head that typically governs its complement NP tightly
│   │       │       │   ├── Some quantifier-like elements can appear before preposition (debated analysis)
│   │       │       │   └── When they do appear, they are often considered intensifiers or degree modifiers
│   │       │       │
│   │       │       ├── **Rare Specifiers in PP:**
│   │       │       │   ├── "Right, directly, exactly, just" (position-intensifying particles):
│   │       │       │   │   ├── "right in the center" (exactly at the center)
│   │       │       │   │   ├── "directly into the room" (straight into)
│   │       │       │   │   ├── "just at the moment" (right at that moment)
│   │       │       │   │   └── "exactly near the door" (right near)
│   │       │       │   │
│   │       │       │   ├── **Quantifier intensifiers:**
│   │       │       │   │   ├── "far ahead" (much ahead)
│   │       │       │   │   ├── "well within" (solidly within)
│   │       │       │   │   ├── "deep inside" (far inside)
│   │       │       │   │   └── "right through" (directly through)
│   │       │       │   │
│   │       │       └── **Uncertain Status:**
│   │       │           ├── These may be better analyzed as pre-modifiers to PP (adverbial modifiers)
│   │       │           ├── Rather than true specifiers in formal X-bar structure
│   │       │           ├── English PPs have minimal specifier licensing (unlike other categories)
│   │       │           └── Structural analysis here is debated in generative grammar
│   │       │
│   │       ├── **Core Functions of Specifiers (Summary)**
│   │       │   ├── **DETERMINATION:** Mark definiteness, specificity, identifiability of phrase
│   │       │   ├── **QUANTIFICATION:** Introduce quantity, number, distribution (all, some, three)
│   │       │   ├── **DEGREE MODIFICATION:** Indicate intensity level for gradable predicates
│   │       │   ├── **INTERROGATION:** Create questions by marking wh-operators
│   │       │   ├── **EXTERNAL ARGUMENT:** Bind external argument (subject in clauses)
│   │       │   ├── **SCOPE DETERMINATION:** Control scope interpretation of entire phrase
│   │       │   └── **REFERENCE:** Determine how listener/reader interprets NP reference
│   │       │
│   │       └── **Key Restrictions on Specifiers**
│   │           ├── **Spec can hold only ONE element** (major difference from modifiers which can stack)
│   │           ├── **Position is FIXED leftmost** (cannot move within phrase; requires syntactic operation to relocate)
│   │           ├── **Type is HEAD-DETERMINED** (head category licenses which specifier types)
│   │           ├── **Agreement often required** (subject-verb agreement, determiner-noun number agreement)
│   │           ├── **Empty Spec is grammatical** (in embedded, non-finite, or bare contexts)
│   │           └── **Specifiers REPLACE, don't combine** (you choose ONE determiner, not stack them)
│   │
│   └── **2. Tools**
│       │
│       ├── **a. Clause Structure Rules**
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **Overview: The Clause Hierarchy**
│       │   │   ├── English clauses follow a nested hierarchical structure with three major layers:
│       │   │   │   ├── **CP (Complementizer Phrase):** Outermost; marks clause type (declarative, interrogative, etc.)
│       │   │   │   ├── **TP (Tense Phrase):** Middle; marks tense, mood, modality; subject position
│       │   │   │   └── **VP (Verb Phrase):** Innermost; contains verb and its complements/adjuncts
│       │   │   ├── Not all clauses have all three layers (main clauses may lack CP; non-finite lack TP)
│       │   │   └── This reflects the principle that heads project; each head creates its own phrase level
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **LAYER 1: CP (Complementizer Phrase) - Clause Typing**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Function & Structure:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Marks clause type, embedding status, and operator scope (questions)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Head:** C (Complementizer) - functional head marking clause properties
│       │   │   │   ├── **Complements:** Embedded clause is C's complement (TP or full CP)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Specifier:** Wh-phrases in questions, operators, or empty (∅) in declaratives
│       │   │   │   └── **Rule:** CP → [Spec(CP) Wh-phrase/∅] [C' C[+tense,-tense,+that,-that] TP]
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Rule Breakdown:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **CP → Spec(CP) C'**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── CP node dominates entire clause structure
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Specifier position (leftmost) hosts wh-phrases or remains empty
│       │   │   │   │   └── C' is the bar-level projection (intermediary node)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **C' → C TP**
│       │   │   │       ├── C' node dominates complementizer and its complement
│       │   │   │       ├── C (Complementizer) is the head element
│       │   │   │       ├── TP (Tense Phrase) is C's complement
│       │   │   │       └── C is optional in many contexts (especially in main clauses)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Complementizer (C) Types & Features:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Declarative C (∅ - Null/Empty in main clauses):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Used in: Main declarative clauses (no overt C needed)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "He left." / "She studies hard." / "They will arrive tomorrow."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Feature: [+tense] - embedded in finite TP
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [CP ∅ [C' ∅ [TP S T' [VP V O]]]]
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Declarative C (that) - in embedded contexts:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Used in: Embedded that-clauses (optional but marking subordination)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "I think [that he left]." / "She said [that it was raining]."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Feature: [+tense, +that] - marks subordination explicitly
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Optionality: Can often be omitted in English (contact clause)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [CP ∅ [C' that [TP S T' [VP V O]]]]
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Interrogative C (∅ - empty, marked by Spec position):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Used in: Yes/no questions where Spec is empty (marked by subject-aux inversion)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "Did he leave?" / "Will she return?" / "Can you help?"
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Feature: [+tense, -that] - interrogative, no explicit marker
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Marked by: Auxiliary movement to C position; subject follows
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [CP ∅ [C' Did [TP he T' [VP leave]]]]
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Wh-interrogative C (marked by Spec position):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Used in: Wh-questions where Spec contains wh-phrase
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "What did he see?" / "Where will she go?" / "Who called?"
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Feature: [+tense, -that, +wh] - interrogative with wh-operator
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Marked by: Wh-phrase in Spec; C may contain auxiliary or be empty
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [CP [What] [C' did [TP he T' [VP see t]]]] (t = trace of moved object)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **For-infinitive C (for):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Used in: Non-finite embedded clauses with overt subject
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "I want [for him to leave]." / "It is unusual [for them to complain]."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Feature: [-tense] - non-finite; allows subject assignment
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Function: Allows non-finite clauses to have overt subjects (normally banned)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [CP ∅ [C' for [TP him T[infinitive] [VP leave]]]]
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **Relative C (that / wh-relative words / ∅):**
│       │   │   │       ├── Used in: Relative clauses modifying NP antecedents
│       │   │   │       ├── Examples: "the book [that I read]" / "the person [who came]" / "the time [when we met]"
│       │   │   │       ├── Feature: [+tense, +relative] - marks clause as modifying NP
│       │   │   │       ├── Types: that-relatives (most restrictive), wh-relatives (identifies role), null (contact)
│       │   │   │       └── Structure varies: [CP that [C' ∅ [TP [VP I read t]]]] or [CP [who] [C' ∅ [TP [VP came]]]]
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **CP Summary Examples:**
│       │   │       ├── Main clause (no CP): "He left."
│       │   │       ├── Embedded declarative: "[CP ∅ [C' that [TP he left]]]" 
│       │   │       ├── Yes/no question: "[CP ∅ [C' Did [TP he leave]]]"
│       │   │       ├── Wh-question: "[CP [What] [C' did [TP he see]]]"
│       │   │       └── Relative clause: "[CP [that] [C' ∅ [TP [VP I read]]]]"
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **LAYER 2: TP (Tense Phrase) - Subject & Tense**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Function & Structure:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Hosts subject; marks tense, mood, modality of the clause
│       │   │   │   ├── **Head:** T (Tense/Inflection) - functional head carrying inflectional features
│       │   │   │   ├── **Complements:** VP (Verb Phrase) is T's complement
│       │   │   │   ├── **Specifier:** Subject position (external argument of predicate)
│       │   │   │   └── **Rule:** TP → [Spec(TP) Subject NP/DP] [T' T[±past, ±perf, ±prog, ±modal] VP]
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Rule Breakdown:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **TP → Spec(Subject) T'**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── TP node dominates subject and tense marking
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Specifier position holds subject (required in finite clauses)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Can be null (∅) in non-finite contexts (infinitives, gerunds)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Subject controls agreement with T head
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **T' → T VP**
│       │   │   │       ├── T' node dominates tense marker and verb phrase
│       │   │   │       ├── T (Tense) is the head element
│       │   │   │       ├── VP (Verb Phrase) is T's complement
│       │   │   │       └── T carries features: ±past, ±perfect, ±progressive, ±modal
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Tense (T) Types & Features:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Simple Present Tense (T: [-past]):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Form: Base verb or verb+s (3rd person singular)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "He leaves." / "I leave." / "They leave."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Features: [-past, -perfect, -progressive, -modal]
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [TP [He] [T' [T: -past] [VP leave]]] → "He leaves"
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Simple Past Tense (T: [+past]):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Form: Verb+ed (regular) or irregular forms (went, saw, etc.)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "He left." / "She walked." / "They understood."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Features: [+past, -perfect, -progressive, -modal]
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [TP [He] [T' [T: +past] [VP left]]] → "He left"
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Future Tense (T: with modal will):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Form: will + base verb
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "He will leave." / "She will understand." / "They will return."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Features: [+modal(will), -past, -perfect, -progressive]
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [TP [He] [T' [T: will] [VP leave]]] → "He will leave"
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Perfect Aspect (T with have):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Form: have + past participle
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "He has left." / "She had understood." / "They will have returned."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Features: [+perfect, ±past] - combines with tense
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [TP [He] [T' [T: have] [AspP [Asp: perf] [VP left]]]]
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Progressive Aspect (T with be):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Form: be + present participle (-ing)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "He is leaving." / "She was working." / "They will be running."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Features: [+progressive, ±past] - combines with tense
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [TP [He] [T' [T: is] [AspP [Asp: prog] [VP leaving]]]]
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **Modals (T with modal auxiliaries):**
│       │   │   │       ├── Form: can, could, may, might, must, should, would, shall
│       │   │   │       ├── Examples: "He can leave." / "She might understand." / "They must return."
│       │   │   │       ├── Features: [+modal, ±past] - modality is separate from tense
│       │   │   │       └── Structure: [TP [He] [T' [T: can/could/may/etc.] [VP leave]]]
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Non-Finite TP (Reduced Tense Phrase):**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Infinitival TP (T: [infinitive, -tense]):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Form: to + base verb (or bare infinitive in certain contexts)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "[to leave]" / "[to understand]" / "[to return]"
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Features: [-tense, -agreement] - no subject inflection
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Subject: Usually null (∅) in infinitives; controlled by matrix verb
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [TP [∅] [T' [T: to-infinitive] [VP leave]]]
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Gerundial TP (T: [gerund, -tense]):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Form: -ing form (verbal noun)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: "[reading]" / "[studying]" / "[playing]"
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Features: [-tense, +nominalized] - acts as noun-like
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Subject: Usually null (∅) in gerunds; can be possessive determiner
│       │   │   │   │   └── Structure: [TP [∅] [T' [T: -ing] [VP read]]] → "reading"
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **Participial TP (T: [participle, -tense]):**
│       │   │   │       ├── Form: -ing or -ed forms (in non-finite clauses)
│       │   │   │       ├── Examples: "[running down the street]" / "[having finished]" / "[beaten by the storm]"
│       │   │   │       ├── Features: [-tense, -agreement] - reduced inflection
│       │   │   │       ├── Subject: Usually null (∅); understood from matrix clause
│       │   │   │       └── Structure: [TP [∅] [T' [T: participle] [VP run]]] → "running"
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **TP Summary Examples:**
│       │   │       ├── Finite: "[TP [He] [T' leaves]]" (simple present)
│       │   │       ├── Finite: "[TP [She] [T' had left]]" (past perfect)
│       │   │       ├── Infinitival: "[TP [∅] [T' to leave]]"
│       │   │       ├── Gerundial: "[TP [∅] [T' reading]]"
│       │   │       └── Participial: "[TP [∅] [T' having finished]]"
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **LAYER 3: VP (Verb Phrase) - Action/State & Complements**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Function & Structure:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Core predication; contains verb and its arguments (complements, adjuncts)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Head:** V (Verb) - lexical head carrying semantic content
│       │   │   │   ├── **Complements:** Objects, oblique arguments (determined by subcategorization)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Specifier:** Often empty (∅); can host internal theme arguments in some analyses
│       │   │   │   ├── **Adjuncts:** Adverbials modifying the verb or event
│       │   │   │   └── **Rule:** VP → [V Complement(s) Adjunct(s)]* or [Spec [V' V Complements Adjuncts]]
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Rule Breakdown:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **VP → (Specifier) V'** [if VP has specifier]
│       │   │   │   │   ├── VP node dominates all verb-related elements
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Specifier position (rarely filled in English; debated)
│       │   │   │   │   └── V' is the bar-level projection
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **V' → V Complements Adjuncts**
│       │   │   │       ├── V' node dominates verb, complements, and adjuncts
│       │   │   │       ├── V (Verb) is the head element
│       │   │   │       ├── Complements appear immediately after V (objects, oblique phrases)
│       │   │   │       └── Adjuncts appear at V', VP, or higher levels (modifying scope varies)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Verb Types & Subcategorization:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Intransitive (V: no object):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: laugh, sneeze, arrive, sleep, die, exist
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Structure: [VP [V' laugh]]
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Sentence: "[TP [He] [T' [T laughed]]]" → "He laughed."
│       │   │   │   │   └── Complement: None required (optional adverbials only)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Transitive (V: requires direct object):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: see, eat, hit, read, give, build
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Structure: [VP [V' eat [NP the apple]]]
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Sentence: "[TP [She] [T' [T ate] [VP [NP the apple]]]]" → "She ate the apple."
│       │   │   │   │   └── Complement: Direct object (NP/DP) is obligatory
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Ditransitive (V: requires indirect + direct object):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: give, send, show, teach, tell, ask
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Structure: [VP [V' give [NP him] [NP the book]]]
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Sentence: "[TP [She] [T' gave] [VP [NP him] [NP the book]]]" → "She gave him the book."
│       │   │   │   │   └── Complements: Two objects (IO and DO) required
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Obligatory Object Complement (V: requires NP + AdjP/NP):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: make, paint, dye, render, leave, consider
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Structure: [VP [V' paint [NP the door] [AP blue]]]
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Sentence: "[TP [She] [T' painted] [VP [NP the door] [AP blue]]]" → "She painted the door blue."
│       │   │   │   │   └── Complements: Object + Complement required
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Prepositional Object (V: requires prepositional phrase):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Examples: put, place, depend, rely, care
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Structure: [VP [V' put [NP the book] [PP on the shelf]]]
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Sentence: "[TP [He] [T' put] [VP [NP the book] [PP on the shelf]]]" → "He put the book on the shelf."
│       │   │   │   │   └── Complements: NP + PP required
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **Clausal Complement (V: requires TP/CP complement):**
│       │   │   │       ├── Examples: know, believe, say, think, doubt, realize
│       │   │   │       ├── Structure: [VP [V' believe [CP that [TP he left]]]]
│       │   │   │       ├── Sentence: "[TP [She] [T' believes] [VP [CP that [he left]]]]" → "She believes that he left."
│       │   │   │       └── Complements: CP/TP complement required
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **VP Summary Examples:**
│       │   │       ├── Intransitive VP: "[VP laugh loudly]"
│       │   │       ├── Transitive VP: "[VP read the book]"
│       │   │       ├── Ditransitive VP: "[VP give him the book]"
│       │   │       ├── ObjComp VP: "[VP paint the door blue]"
│       │   │       └── Clausal VP: "[VP believe that he left]"
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **Embedded Clauses (Subordinate Structures)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **That-Clause (Finite embedded clause with complementizer that):**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Form:** [CP ∅ [C' that [TP Subject T' VP]]]
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Embed declarative propositions as complements
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "I think [that he left]."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She said [that it was raining]."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "They knew [that we would arrive]."
│       │   │   │   │   └── "The fact [that she succeeded] surprised everyone."
│       │   │   │   ├── **Optionality:** "that" can often be omitted in English (contact clause)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "I think [∅ he left]." (omitted "that")
│       │   │   │   │   └── "She said [∅ it was raining]." (omitted "that")
│       │   │   │   └── **Constraints on omission:**
│       │   │   │       ├── ✓ Omit after: believe, think, say, know, hope, realize, etc. (most verbs)
│       │   │   │       └── ✗ Cannot omit when: that-clause is in subject position, or after some fixed expressions
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Wh-Clause (Embedded question):**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Form:** [CP [Wh-phrase] [C' C [TP Subject T' VP]]]
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Embed questions as embedded questions (not polar questions)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "I asked [what he saw]."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She wondered [where he went]."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "They knew [when she would return]."
│       │   │   │   │   └── "The question [why he left] remains unanswered."
│       │   │   │   ├── **Wh-Phrase Position:** Moves to Spec of embedded CP (doesn't move as far as in main questions)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Word Order:** Embedded wh-questions maintain subject-verb order (unlike main wh-questions)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Main: "What did he see?" (aux-inversion)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Embedded: "I asked [what he saw]." (no inversion; normal order)
│       │   │   │   └── **Yes/No embedded questions (if/whether):**
│       │   │   │       ├── Form: [CP [if/whether] [C' C [TP Subject T' VP]]]
│       │   │   │       ├── Examples: "I asked [if he was coming]." / "She wondered [whether they succeeded]."
│       │   │   │       └── Note: "if" and "whether" are not wh-phrases; they mark polar questions
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **For-Infinitive Clause (Non-finite embedded clause):**
│       │   │       ├── **Form:** [CP ∅ [C' for [TP Subject T[infinitive] VP]]]
│       │   │       ├── **Function:** Allows non-finite clauses to have overt subjects (normally impossible)
│       │   │       ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │       │   ├── "I want [for him to leave]."
│       │   │       │   ├── "It is unusual [for them to complain]."
│       │   │       │   ├── "The best option [for her to do] is study harder."
│       │   │       │   └── "What would you like [for me to do]?"
│       │   │       ├── **Comparison with null-subject infinitive:**
│       │   │       │   ├── With "for": "I want [for him to leave]." (overt subject)
│       │   │       │   └── Without "for": "I want [to leave]." (null subject = I)
│       │   │       └── **Constraint:** "For" is optional in some contexts but required when explicit subject needed
│       │   │
│       │   └── **Complete Clause Structure Examples:**
│       │       ├── **Simple Main Clause:**
│       │       │   └── [CP ∅ [C' ∅ [TP [He] [T' left] [VP quickly]]]]
│       │       ├── **Interrogative Main Clause:**
│       │       │   └── [CP ∅ [C' Did [TP [he] [T' leave] [VP]]]]
│       │       ├── **Wh-Main Clause:**
│       │       │   └── [CP [What] [C' did [TP [he] [T' see] [VP t]]]]
│       │       ├── **Embedded That-Clause:**
│       │       │   └── [CP ∅ [C' that [TP [he] [T' left] [VP]]]]
│       │       ├── **Embedded Wh-Clause:**
│       │       │   └── [CP [what] [C' ∅ [TP [he] [T' saw] [VP t]]]]
│       │       └── **For-Infinitive Clause:**
│       │           └── [CP ∅ [C' for [TP [him] [T' to] [VP leave]]]]
│       │
│       └── **b. Phrase Structure Rules (X-bar Template)**
│           │
│           ├── **Overview: Universal X-bar Principle**
│           │   ├── All phrases follow the same hierarchical structure regardless of category
│           │   ├── X-bar notation: X (head), X' (bar level), XP (maximal projection)
│           │   ├── Standard order: Specifier > Head > Complements > Adjuncts (with language-specific variations)
│           │   ├── English is predominantly head-initial for verbs/prepositions; head-final for nouns
│           │   └── This principle explains structural parallelism across all phrase types
│           │
│           ├── **Universal X-bar Schema (The Template)**
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **Rule 1: Maximal Projection (XP) - Specifier Position**
│           │   │   ├── **Rule:** XP → (Specifier) X'
│           │   │   ├── **Components:**
│           │   │   │   ├── **XP:** Maximal Projection (largest phrase unit of category X)
│           │   │   │   ├── **Specifier:** Optional external modifier (determines/quantifies X')
│           │   │   │   └── **X':** Bar-level (intermediary between Specifier and head)
│           │   │   ├── **Examples across categories:**
│           │   │   │   ├── NP: [NP the [N' book]] - "the" is Spec of NP
│           │   │   │   ├── VP: [VP quickly [V' run]] - "quickly" is Spec (some analyses)
│           │   │   │   ├── AdjP: [AdjP very [Adj' beautiful]] - "very" is Spec of AdjP
│           │   │   │   ├── TP: [TP he [T' left]] - "he" is Spec of TP (subject)
│           │   │   │   └── CP: [CP what [C' did you see]] - "what" is Spec of CP
│           │   │   └── **Optionality:** Specifier can be empty (∅) in many phrases
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **Rule 2: Bar Level (X') - Adjunction**
│           │   │   ├── **Rule:** X' → (Adjunct) X'
│           │   │   ├── **Function:** Allows multiple adjuncts to attach recursively
│           │   │   ├── **Components:**
│           │   │   │   ├── **X':** Bar-level node on left
│           │   │   │   ├── **Adjunct:** Optional modifier attaching to X'
│           │   │   │   └── **X':** Bar-level node on right (creates recursive structure)
│           │   │   ├── **Recursion:** Allows unlimited adjuncts by repeating rule
│           │   │   │   ├── [N' [N'[red book] in the library] yesterday]
│           │   │   │   ├── [VP [VP [VP ran] quickly] yesterday] [in the park]
│           │   │   │   └── Multiple adjuncts can stack via recursion
│           │   │   └── **Examples:**
│           │   │       ├── N': [N' [red] [N' book]] (adjective modifies N')
│           │   │       ├── N': [N' [from France] [N' [large] [N' table]]] (PP adjunct modifies N')
│           │   │       ├── V': [V' [quickly] [V' run]] (adverb modifies V')
│           │   │       └── V': [V' [in the park] [V' [rapidly] [V' run]]] (PP + Adv modify V')
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **Rule 3: Head Level (X') - Complements**
│           │   │   ├── **Rule:** X' → X (Complement*)
│           │   │   ├── **Function:** Attaches complements required by head's subcategorization
│           │   │   ├── **Components:**
│           │   │   │   ├── **X':** Bar-level node on left
│           │   │   │   ├── **X:** Head (lexical or functional)
│           │   │   │   └── **Complement(s):** Objects, obliques required by X's subcategorization frame
│           │   │   ├── **Directionality:** Head-initial (English mostly); complements follow head
│           │   │   │   ├── English SVO: V comes before objects
│           │   │   │   ├── English NON (mostly): N comes before of-PP complements
│           │   │   │   └── English is predominantly head-initial
│           │   │   └── **Examples:**
│           │   │       ├── V: [V' [V read] [NP the book]] (transitive verb + direct object)
│           │   │       ├── V: [V' [V give] [NP him] [NP the book]] (ditransitive verb + two objects)
│           │   │       ├── V: [V' [V put] [NP the book] [PP on the shelf]] (verb + object + PP)
│           │   │       ├── N: [N' [N destruction] [PP of the city]] (noun + PP complement)
│           │   │       ├── P: [P' [P in] [NP the room]] (preposition + NP object)
│           │   │       └── A: [A' [A proud] [PP of her achievement]] (adjective + PP complement)
│           │   │
│           │   └── **Rule 4: Head (X) - Lexical Item**
│           │       ├── **Rule:** X → Head
│           │       ├── **Function:** Single lexical or functional item (terminal/lowest level)
│           │       ├── **Examples:**
│           │       │   ├── **Lexical heads:** book, read, beautiful, quickly, in
│           │       │   ├── **Functional heads:** the, he, very, did, that
│           │       │   └── Base form (no inflection applies below this level)
│           │       └── **Note:** This is the lowest level of X-bar; consists of single items
│           │
│           ├── **Category-Specific Rules & Instantiations**
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **DP/NP (Determiner Phrase / Noun Phrase)**
│           │   │   ├── **Structure:**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 1:** DP → (Det) D'
│           │   │   │   │   ├── DP = Maximal nominal projection
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Specifier = Determiner (the, a, this, my, some, etc.)
│           │   │   │   │   └── D' = Bar-level
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 2:** D' → (Adjunct-AP/PP) D'
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Allows modifiers (adjectives, PPs) to attach
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Creates recursive structure for multiple adjuncts
│           │   │   │   │   └── Adjuncts = pre-modifiers (adjectives, numerals) or post-modifiers (PPs, relatives)
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 3:** D' → D NP
│           │   │   │   │   ├── D = Determiner head
│           │   │   │   │   └── NP = Noun Phrase (or N' with noun and modifiers)
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   └── **Rule 4:** N → {noun: book, person, idea, etc.}
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Simple DP: [DP [D the] [NP [N book]]] → "the book"
│           │   │   │   ├── With modifiers: [DP [D the] [N' [AP red] [N' [N book] [PP in the library]]]] → "the red book in the library"
│           │   │   │   ├── With relative clause: [DP [D the] [N' [N book] [CP that I read]]] → "the book that I read"
│           │   │   │   └── Plural: [DP [D these] [N' [AP beautiful] [NP [N books]]]] → "these beautiful books"
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   └── **Notes:**
│           │   │       ├── Modern analyses treat D (Determiner) as head, creating DP above NP
│           │   │       ├── Traditional approach: NP was maximal; modern: DP is maximal
│           │   │       ├── D' = D-bar level intermediate structure
│           │   │       └── Adjectives and other pre-modifiers typically adjoin to N'
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **VP (Verb Phrase)**
│           │   │   ├── **Structure:**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 1:** VP → (Spec?) V'
│           │   │   │   │   ├── VP = Maximal verbal projection
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Specifier = Often empty in English; internal theme in some analyses
│           │   │   │   │   └── V' = Bar-level
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 2:** V' → (Adjunct-AdvP/PP) V'
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Allows adverbials to attach recursively
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Manner, locative, temporal adverbials modify V'
│           │   │   │   │   └── Creates structure for multiple verb modifiers
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 3:** V' → V (Complement)*
│           │   │   │   │   ├── V = Verb head
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Complement(s) = Direct object, indirect object, obliques
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Order: V then Complements (head-initial)
│           │   │   │   │   └── Number varies by subcategorization (0, 1, 2, or more)
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   └── **Rule 4:** V → {verb: read, run, give, etc.}
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Intransitive: [VP [V' laugh]] → "laugh"
│           │   │   │   ├── Transitive: [VP [V' [V read] [NP the book]]] → "read the book"
│           │   │   │   ├── Ditransitive: [VP [V' [V give] [NP him] [NP the book]]] → "give him the book"
│           │   │   │   ├── With oblique: [VP [V' [V put] [NP the book] [PP on the shelf]]] → "put the book on the shelf"
│           │   │   │   └── With modifiers: [VP [PP quickly] [V' [V [V run] [PP in the park]]]] → "run in the park quickly"
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   └── **Notes:**
│           │   │       ├── English is strictly head-initial for verbs (V precedes all complements)
│           │   │       ├── Adverbial adjuncts can attach at V' or VP level (creates scope ambiguities)
│           │   │       ├── Aspectual auxiliaries (be, have) may create separate AspP above VP
│           │   │       └── Complex predicates with multiple verbs show nested VP structures
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **AdjP (Adjective Phrase)**
│           │   │   ├── **Structure:**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 1:** AdjP → (Deg) Adj'
│           │   │   │   │   ├── AdjP = Maximal adjectival projection
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Specifier = Degree word (very, quite, too, extremely)
│           │   │   │   │   └── Adj' = Bar-level
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 2:** Adj' → (Adjunct?) Adj'
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Limited adjunct attachment for adjectives (not common)
│           │   │   │   │   └── Degree modifiers might recursively attach
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 3:** Adj' → Adj (Complement?)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Adj = Adjective head
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Complement = PP or CP (for adjectives like "proud", "aware")
│           │   │   │   │   └── Many adjectives don't require complements
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   └── **Rule 4:** Adj → {adjective: beautiful, tall, red, etc.}
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Simple: [AdjP [Adj tall]] → "tall"
│           │   │   │   ├── With degree: [AdjP [Deg very] [Adj' [Adj beautiful]]] → "very beautiful"
│           │   │   │   ├── With complement: [AdjP [Deg very] [Adj' [Adj proud] [PP of her]]] → "very proud of her"
│           │   │   │   ├── With CP complement: [AdjP [Adj aware] [CP that he left]] → "aware that he left"
│           │   │   │   └── Comparative: [AdjP [Deg more] [Adj' [Adj beautiful] [PP than ever]]] → "more beautiful than ever"
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   └── **Notes:**
│           │   │       ├── Degree specifiers are obligatory for intensity modification
│           │   │       ├── Complementation is optional; many adjectives lack complements
│           │   │       ├── Post-nominal positioning of adjective phrases is common in English
│           │   │       └── Comparative/superlative forms involve degree marker
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **AdvP (Adverb Phrase)**
│           │   │   ├── **Structure:**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 1:** AdvP → (Deg) Adv'
│           │   │   │   │   ├── AdvP = Maximal adverbial projection
│           │   │   │   ├── Specifier = Degree word (very, quite, too, extremely)
│           │   │   │   ├── Adv' = Bar-level
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 2:** Adv' → (Adjunct?) Adv'
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Limited adjunct attachment (occasional)
│           │   │   │   │   └── Very few adverbials modify other adverbs
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 3:** Adv' → Adv (Complement?)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Adv = Adverb head
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Complement = Rarely required for adverbs
│           │   │   │   │   └── Some manner adverbs can take PPs
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   └── **Rule 4:** Adv → {adverb: quickly, slowly, yesterday, etc.}
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Simple: [AdvP [Adv quickly]] → "quickly"
│           │   │   │   ├── With degree: [AdvP [Deg very] [Adv' [Adv quickly]]] → "very quickly"
│           │   │   │   ├── Comparative: [AdvP [Deg more] [Adv' [Adv quickly] [PP than before]]] → "more quickly than before"
│           │   │   │   └── With complement: [AdvP [Adv independently] [PP of his parents]] → "independently of his parents"
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   └── **Notes:**
│           │   │       ├── Many adverbs don't take degree modification (especially temporal/locative)
│           │   │       ├── Degree specifiers mainly apply to manner adverbs
│           │   │       ├── Complements are rare for adverbs
│           │   │       └── Comparative adverbs use "more" rather than inflection (more quickly vs. *quicklier)
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **PP (Prepositional Phrase)**
│           │   │   ├── **Structure:**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 1:** PP → (Spec?) P'
│           │   │   │   │   ├── PP = Maximal prepositional projection
│           │   │   │   ├── Specifier = Rare/debated in English (some particles: right, directly)
│           │   │   │   ├── P' = Bar-level
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 2:** P' → (Adjunct?) P'
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Very limited adjunct attachment
│           │   │   │   │   └── Unusual to modify within PP
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   ├── **Rule 3:** P' → P (Complement)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── P = Preposition head
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Complement = NP object (obligatory for most prepositions)
│           │   │   │   │   └── Some prepositions take clausal complements
│           │   │   │   │
│           │   │   │   └── **Rule 4:** P → {preposition: in, on, at, to, from, etc.}
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Simple: [PP [P in] [NP the room]] → "in the room"
│           │   │   │   ├── Complex object: [PP [P in] [DP [D the] [NP [AP big] [N house]]]] → "in the big house"
│           │   │   │   ├── Stranding (object movement): "Which room did you walk [PP [P in] __]?" → "in which room"
│           │   │   │   └── P-particle: [PP [P right] [P' [P in] [NP the center]]] → "right in the center"
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   └── **Notes:**
│           │   │       ├── PPs are fairly constrained structures
│           │   │       ├── P-stranding in English allows P to separate from object via movement
│           │   │       ├── Some debate about proper P-structure (head-parameter applies)
│           │   │       └── Prepositions typically precede their complements (head-initial)
│           │   │
│           │   └── **CP (Complementizer Phrase)**
│           │       ├── **Structure:**
│           │       │   ├── **Rule 1:** CP → (C-Spec) C'
│           │       │   │   ├── CP = Maximal clausal projection
│           │       │   │   ├── Specifier = Wh-phrase (who, what, where) or empty (∅)
│           │       │   │   └── C' = Bar-level
│           │       │   │
│           │       │   ├── **Rule 2:** C' → (Adjunct?) C'
│           │       │   │   ├── Rare attachment to C'
│           │       │   │   └── Unusual adjunction within clausal structure
│           │       │   │
│           │       │   ├── **Rule 3:** C' → C TP
│           │       │   │   ├── C = Complementizer head (that, if, whether, ∅)
│           │       │   │   ├── TP = Tense Phrase (the embedded clause)
│           │       │   │   └── Order: C then TP (head-initial)
│           │       │   │
│           │       │   └── **Rule 4:** C → {comp: that, for, if, whether, ∅}
│           │       │
│           │       ├── **Examples:**
│           │       │   ├── Declarative: [CP ∅ [C' that [TP he left]]] → "that he left"
│           │       │   ├── Interrogative: [CP [what] [C' ∅ [TP he saw t]]] → "what he saw"
│           │       │   ├── Yes/no: [CP ∅ [C' if [TP he comes]]] → "if he comes"
│           │       │   └── For-infinitive: [CP ∅ [C' for [TP him to leave]]] → "for him to leave"
│           │       │
│           │       └── **Notes:**
│           │           ├── CP is the largest unit in embedded clauses
│           │           ├── C and Spec positions are tightly linked (related filter)
│           │           ├── Wh-movement targets Spec of CP
│           │           └── Some languages have CP recursion (multiple complementizers)
│           │
│           ├── **Language-Specific Variations & English-Specific Features**
│           │   ├── **Head-Direction Parameter:**
│           │   │   ├── English is predominantly HEAD-INITIAL:
│           │   │   │   ├── Verb precedes objects: S + V + O (SVO)
│           │   │   │   ├── Preposition precedes noun: P + N (in the room)
│           │   │   │   ├── Complementizer precedes TP: C + TP (that he left)
│           │   │   │   └── Degree precedes adjective: Deg + A (very tall)
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   └── English is HEAD-FINAL for nouns (modifiers follow):
│           │   │       ├── Noun precedes adjective modifier: N + A (book red) is ungrammatical
│           │   │       ├── Noun precedes PP: N + PP (book in the library)
│           │   │       ├── Noun precedes relative clause: N + Rel-clause (book that I read)
│           │   │       └── Pre-nominal adjectives are exceptions (special position)
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **Adjective Placement (Mixed Parameter):**
│           │   │   ├── Pre-nominal adjectives (most adjectives):
│           │   │   │   ├── "beautiful house", "tall person", "red car"
│           │   │   │   ├── Follow Adjective Order Convention
│           │   │   │   └── Are actually modifier-adjuncts (not complements)
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   └── Post-nominal adjectives (less common; when adjective has complement):
│           │   │       ├── "person proud of her work", "book difficult to understand"
│           │   │       ├── Usually require a complement (PP or infinitive)
│           │   │       └── Signal that adjective is predicate-like
│           │   │
│           │   └── **Auxiliary Verb Order:**
│           │       ├── Auxiliaries in T position raise to C in main questions:
│           │       │   ├── Base: [TP he [T has] [VP left]]
│           │       │   ├── Question: [CP ∅ [C' has [TP he [T t] [VP left]]]] → "Has he left?"
│           │       │   └── T-to-C movement creates question inversion
│           │       │
│           │       └── Auxiliaries in T position stay in embedded clauses:
│           │           ├── Base: [TP he [T will] [VP leave]]
│           │           ├── Embedded: [CP ∅ [C' that [TP he [T will] [VP leave]]]] → "that he will leave"
│           │           └── No T-to-C movement in subordinate clauses
│           │
│           └── **Summary: Applying X-bar to Sentence Analysis**
│               ├── **Steps for analyzing phrase structure:**
│               │   ├── 1. Identify the head of each phrase (noun, verb, adjective, preposition, etc.)
│               │   ├── 2. Identify complements (items required by head's subcategorization)
│               │   ├── 3. Identify modifiers/adjuncts (optional items; can stack)
│               │   ├── 4. Identify specifiers (leftmost, scope-bearing items)
│               │   └── 5. Build tree using X-bar template: [XP Spec [X' (Adjuncts) X Complements]]
│               │
│               └── **Complete Sentence Example:**
│                   └── "The tall student carefully read the difficult book in the library yesterday."
│                       ├── [CP ∅ [C' ∅ [TP
│                       ├── [DP the [N' tall [N student]]] (subject)
│                       ├── [T' read [VP
│                       ├── [DP the [N' difficult [N book]]] (direct object)
│                       ├── [PP in [the library]] (locative adjunct)
│                       ├── [AdvP carefully] (manner adjunct)
│                       ├── [AdvP yesterday] (temporal adjunct)
│                       └── ]]]]] (all adjuncts modify VP level)
│
├── **III. Word Order**
│   │
│   ├── **Basic Pattern [7 Traditional Types]**
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **1. S + V (Intransitive Verb: No object required)**
│   │   │   ├── Simple Intransitive (pure action)
│   │   │   └── Intransitive with Adverbial (optional: S + V + A)
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **2. S + V + DO (Monotransitive: Direct Object)**
│   │   │   ├── Direct Object as NP (noun phrase)
│   │   │   ├── Direct Object as Clause
│   │   │   └── With Optional Adverbial (S + V + DO + A)
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **3. S + V + IO + DO (Ditransitive: Indirect + Direct Object)**
│   │   │   ├── IO before DO (without 'to/for')
│   │   │   ├── Alternative: S + V + DO + to/for + IO
│   │   │   └── With Adverbial
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **4. S + V + SC (Linking Verb + Subject Complement)**
│   │   │   ├── Noun Complement (renames subject)
│   │   │   ├── Adjective Complement (describes subject)
│   │   │   └── Prepositional Phrase Complement
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **5. S + V + DO + OC (Object Complement)**
│   │   │   ├── Noun Object Complement
│   │   │   ├── Adjective Object Complement
│   │   │   └── Infinitive/Participle as OC
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **6. S + V + A (Intransitive Verb + Obligatory Adverbial)**
│   │   │   ├── Adverbial of Place
│   │   │   ├── Adverbial of Time/Duration
│   │   │   └── Adverbial of Manner
│   │   │
│   │   └── **7. S + V + DO + A (Transitive Verb + Direct Object + Obligatory Adverbial)**
│   │       ├── Adverbial of Place (common with verbs like put, place)
│   │       ├── Adverbial of Manner
│   │       └── Adverbial of Time
│   │
│   └── **Variations**
│       │
│       ├── **Inversion (Reversing S-V order for emphasis/questions/conditionals)**
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **Definition & Function**
│       │   │   ├── **What is Inversion?**
│       │   │   │   └── Movement of auxiliary/verb before subject position for emphasis, questions,
│       │   │   │       conditionals, or stylistic effect. Changes canonical SVO order to VSO or other patterns.
│       │   │   ├── **Core Function:**
│       │   │   │   ├── Mark interrogative mood (questions)
│       │   │   │   ├── Create emphasis/dramatic effect
│       │   │   │   ├── Express conditionals without "if"
│       │   │   │   ├── Achieve formal/archaic tone
│       │   │   │   └── Highlight initial adverbials
│       │   │   └── **Grammatical Requirements:**
│       │   │       ├── Requires auxiliary verb or "be" to move
│       │   │       ├── Main verb follows subject (in most types)
│       │   │       ├── Must maintain subject-verb agreement
│       │   │       └── Subject (noun phrase) moves after verb
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **5 Major Types of Inversion**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **1. QUESTION INVERSION (Aux/V + S + V)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Aux + Subject + Main Verb
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Form yes/no and wh-questions
│       │   │   │   ├── **Yes/No Questions:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "She is coming."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Question: "**Is** she coming?" (Aux moves before subject)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "She will go."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Question: "**Will** she go?" (Modal moves before subject)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "She went."
│       │   │   │   │   └── Question: "**Did** she go?" (Do-support + inversion)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Wh-Questions:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Where** is she?" (Wh-word + Aux + S + V)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**What** will they do?" (Wh-word + Aux + S + V)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**When** did you arrive?" (Wh-word + Aux + S + V)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "**Who** left?" (Subject wh-word; no inversion)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Tag Questions:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She is coming, **isn't she**?" (Aux inversion in tag)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "They left, **didn't they**?" (Aux inversion + negation)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Auxiliary inverts and negates
│       │   │   │   └── **Do-Support (for simple tense without aux):**
│       │   │   │       ├── "Does she like it?" (Do inserted when no aux present)
│       │   │   │       ├── "Did they go?" (Did inserted for past tense)
│       │   │   │       └── Non-inverted: "She likes it." → Inverted: "Does she like it?"
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **2. NEGATIVE ADVERBIAL FRONTING (Neg-Adv + Aux + S + V)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Negative Adverbial + Auxiliary + Subject + Verb
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Emphasize negative or restrictive meaning via fronting
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Never** have I seen such beauty." (Never + Aux have + S I + V seen)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Rarely** does he complain." (Rarely + Aux does + S he + V complain)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Seldom** had they met." (Seldom + Aux had + S they + V met)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Not only** did she win, but she broke the record." (Not only + Aux did)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Barely** have they arrived." (Barely + Aux have + S they + V arrived)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**In no way** can I agree." (Negative PP + Aux + S)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "**Only then** did he realize his mistake." (Only-adv + Aux + S + V)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Negative Adverbials Triggering Inversion:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Never, hardly, scarcely, barely, seldom, rarely, no longer, nowhere, at no time
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Only, only then, only after, not only, not once
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Negative PPs: under no circumstances, in no way, with no intention
│       │   │   │   │   └── Note: NOT all negatives trigger inversion (e.g., "I don't know" is normal; inversion optional)
│       │   │   │   └── **Stylistic Function:**
│       │   │   │       ├── Creates formal/emphatic register
│       │   │   │       ├── Emphasizes rarity or extreme negation
│       │   │   │       └── Literary/archaic tone: "Never had I seen such folly."
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **3. CONDITIONAL INVERSION (Had/Were/Should + S + V without 'if')**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Auxiliary alone (no "if") with inversion
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Express conditionals in formal/literary register; omit "if"
│       │   │   │   ├── **Past Counterfactual (Had):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── With "if": "If he had known, he would have come."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Inversion: "**Had** he known, he would have come." (formal alternative)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Had** I studied harder, I would have passed."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Had** we realized the danger, we would have fled."
│       │   │   │   │   └── Pattern: Had + S + V.past.part. (structures past unfactual condition)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Present Counterfactual (Were):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── With "if": "If I were you, I would leave."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Inversion: "**Were** I you, I would leave." (formal)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Were** she here, she would help."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Were** they willing, they could assist."
│       │   │   │   │   └── Pattern: Were + S + Complement (structures present counterfactual)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Future or Modal Conditional (Should/Could):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Should** you need help, call me." (if you should need = formal conditional)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Should** you arrive early, wait outside."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Could** you find the book, send it to me."
│       │   │   │   │   └── Pattern: Should/Could + S + V (future possibility without 'if')
│       │   │   │   └── **Register & Style:**
│       │   │   │       ├── Highly formal and literary
│       │   │   │       ├── Common in written formal English; rare in speech
│       │   │   │       ├── No change in meaning vs. "if" version (purely stylistic)
│       │   │   │       └── Creates elevated/archaic tone: "Were he a man of honor..."
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **4. LOCATIVE INVERSION (Adverbial/Locative + V + S)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Locative/Directional Adverbial + Verb + Subject
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Introduce new information; highlight location; create narrative effect
│       │   │   │   ├── **Constraints:** Works best with intransitive/unaccusative verbs (come, go, arrive, emerge, appear, hang, stand)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Here** comes the bus." (Locative adv + Verb comes + S the bus)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**There** goes the opportunity." (There + goes + S the opportunity)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Up** the hill **walked** the soldiers." (Directional + walked + S soldiers)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**In** the corner **sat** an old man." (PP locative + sat + S man)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Hanging** from the ceiling **were** crystal chandeliers." (Participial + were + S chandeliers)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Outside** the gate **stands** a monument." (Outside + stands + S monument)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "**Deep** in the forest **lay** the treasure." (Locative + lay + S treasure)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Word Order Comparison:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Normal: "The bus is coming here."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Inverted: "**Here** comes the bus." (information-structure focused)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Constraints on Subjects:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✓ Works with indefinite subjects: "Here comes **a** man." / "**There** are books."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✓ Works with definite in narrative/poetic: "**There** goes **the** train."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✗ Awkward with heavy preposing: "*Here comes slowly the bus."
│       │   │   │   │   └── Must preserve subject-verb agreement: "Here **comes** a man" vs. "**come** boys"
│       │   │   │   └── **Semantic Function:**
│       │   │   │       ├── Introduces location as background
│       │   │   │       ├── Treats subject as "news" (focus)
│       │   │   │       ├── Creates narrative vividness: "Around the corner **appeared** the stranger."
│       │   │   │       └── Opposite of normal: normal = subject-focus; locative inversion = event-focus
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **5. EXCLAMATORY INVERSION (Wh-word + Adj + V + S)**
│       │   │       ├── **Structure:** Wh-word (How/What) + Adjective + Verb + Subject
│       │   │       ├── **Function:** Express strong emotion or evaluation exclamatively
│       │   │       ├── **How-Exclamatives:**
│       │   │       │   ├── "**How** beautiful is this view!" (How + Adj beautiful + V is + S view)
│       │   │       │   ├── "**How** terrible are these conditions!" (How + Adj terrible + V are + S conditions)
│       │   │       │   ├── "**How** quickly does the time pass!" (How + Adv quickly + does + time pass)
│       │   │       │   ├── Contrasts with statement: "This view is beautiful." → Exclamation: "How beautiful this view is!"
│       │   │       │   └── Note: Can omit inversion in informal speech: "How beautiful this view is!" (no inversion, still exclamatory)
│       │   │       ├── **What-Exclamatives:**
│       │   │       │   ├── "**What** a beautiful day it is!" (What + NP a beautiful day + V is + S it)
│       │   │       │   ├── "**What** nonsense they speak!" (What + NP nonsense + V speak + S they)
│       │   │       │   ├── "**What** amazing discoveries we have made!" (What + NP discoveries + V made)
│       │   │       │   └── Often with "a" article (indefinite): "**What** a fool I am!"
│       │   │       ├── **Comparison with Questions:**
│       │   │   │       ├── Question: "How beautiful is it?" (seeks information)
│       │   │   │       ├── Exclamation: "How beautiful it is!" (expresses emotion; period, not question mark)
│       │   │   │       ├── Key difference: INTONATION and PUNCTUATION (question mark vs. exclamation point)
│       │   │       └── **Emotional Function:**
│       │   │           ├── Expresses surprise, delight, horror, amazement
│       │   │           ├── Emphasizes degree (how much/what kind)
│       │   │           └── Creates formal/literary tone
│       │   │
│       │   └── **Constraints & Limitations on All Inversion Types**
│       │       ├── **Subject Positioning:**
│       │       │   ├── Subject MUST follow verb/auxiliary (core requirement)
│       │       │   ├── Subject cannot be a simple pronoun in some contexts (except questions)
│       │       │   ├── ✓ "Where is she?" (pronoun OK in question)
│       │       │   ├── ✓ "Here comes John." (name OK in locative)
│       │       │   ├── ✗ "*Here comes he." (pronoun awkward in locative)
│       │       │   └── ✓ "Here he comes." (pronoun + verb in different structure; not true inversion)
│       │       ├── **Verb Type Restrictions:**
│       │       │   ├── Questions: All verb types can invert (transitives, intransitives, be)
│       │       │   ├── Locative: Only unaccusative/intransitive/be work well
│       │       │   ├── Conditional: Only auxiliaries (had, were, should, could) invert
│       │       │   ├── Negative adv fronting: Requires auxiliary (or do-support)
│       │       │   └── No inversion with pure main verb (no auxiliary): *"Came she quickly."
│       │       ├── **Agreement Maintenance:**
│       │       │   ├── Subject-verb agreement still applies after inversion
│       │       │   ├── ✓ "**Were** they coming?" (plural subject = were)
│       │       │   ├── ✗ "*Was they coming?" (agreement violation)
│       │       │   ├── ✓ "Here **come** the boys." (plural subj = come)
│       │       │   └── ✗ "*Here **comes** the boys." (agreement error)
│       │       └── **Stylistic Register:**
│       │           ├── Questions: Neutral (all registers)
│       │           ├── Negative adv fronting: Formal/emphatic
│       │           ├── Conditional inversion: Formal/literary/archaic
│       │           ├── Locative inversion: Narrative/poetic/formal
│       │           └── Exclamatory: Formal/emotional
│       │
│       ├── **Passive Voice (Object promotes to Subject; Agent optional)**
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **Definition & Function**
│       │   │   ├── **What is Passive Voice?**
│       │   │   │   └── Grammatical construction in which the object of an active sentence becomes the subject,
│       │   │   │       and the subject becomes an optional agent (or disappears entirely). The verb undergoes
│       │   │   │       morphosyntactic changes to mark passive aspect (be + past participle).
│       │   │   ├── **Core Function:**
│       │   │   │   ├── Shift focus from agent (doer) to patient (receiver)
│       │   │   │   ├── De-emphasize or omit agent (information structuring)
│       │   │   │   ├── Express universal truths or general statements
│       │   │   │   ├── Avoid assigning responsibility (politeness/evasion)
│       │   │   │   └── Create formal/scientific register
│       │   │   └── **Formation Requirements:**
│       │   │       ├── Transitive verb (requires object to promote)
│       │   │       ├── Auxiliary "be" (marks passive aspect)
│       │   │       ├── Past participle of main verb (required form)
│       │   │       ├── Optional agent phrase with "by" (indicates doer)
│       │   │       └── Word order change: SVO → S(patient) be VP.past.part. (by Agent)
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **Active vs. Passive Comparison**
│       │   │   ├── **Active:** "**The cat** ate **the mouse**." (Subject = agent; Object = patient)
│       │   │   ├── **Passive:** "**The mouse** was eaten **by the cat**." (Subject = patient; Agent optional)
│       │   │   ├── **Passive without agent:** "**The mouse** was eaten." (Agent omitted; emphasis on result)
│       │   │   ├── **Information Structure Shift:**
│       │   │   │   ├── Active = Agent is topic (subject position)
│       │   │   │   ├── Passive = Patient is topic (subject position)
│       │   │   │   └── Same semantic content; different pragmatic focus
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **4 Major Types of Passive**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **1. SHORT PASSIVE (Patient only; no agent phrase)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Patient-S + be + V.past.part
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Focus on action/result; suppress or unknown agent
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "The house **was destroyed**." (Agent unknown/irrelevant)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She **was arrested**." (Agent not mentioned; emphatic)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "The bridge **was completed** last year." (Focus on completion, not builder)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Mistakes **were made**." (Agent deliberately omitted/evasive)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "They **are loved**." (Universal; love as passive state)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Uses:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Agent is obvious/irrelevant: "The letter **was mailed**." (who mails is understood)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Agent is unknown: "The ancient temple **was built** centuries ago."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Evasion/politeness: "**Mistakes were made**." (responsibility evaded)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Scientific register: "The specimen **was analyzed** under controlled conditions."
│       │   │   │   │   └── General truths: "This type of flower **is found** in tropical regions."
│       │   │   │   └── **Note:** Can feel evasive or vague (often critiqued in writing)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **2. LONG PASSIVE (With agent phrase by...)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Patient-S + be + V.past.part + by Agent-NP
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Preserve agent information but shift focus to patient
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "The book **was written by Jane Austen**." (Agent explicit but object of preposition)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "The law **was passed by Congress**." (Agent present but deprioritized)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "The cake **was baked by my mother**." (Agent mentioned but patien is subject)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "The report **was submitted by the department**." (Agent identified but not emphasized)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "The novel **was praised by critics**." (Agent multiple/general; de-emphasized)
│       │   │   │   ├── **By-Agent Restrictions:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Agent is demoted to oblique (by-phrase) position
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Creates formal/written register
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Agent often indefinite/general (people, experts, critics)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Heavy agents are often retained (famous authors, known organizations)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Lightweight agents often omitted (it, someone, one): "**was done** (by someone)"
│       │   │   │   └── **Pragmatics:**
│       │   │   │       ├── Agent included when important/surprising/noteworthy: "**written by a child**"
│       │   │   │       ├── Agent omitted when obvious: "The car **was washed**." (I washed it; obvious)
│       │   │   │       ├── Agent foregrounded by theme/rheme: Beginners "**by the experts**" if news
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **3. GET-PASSIVE (Informal; auxiliary "get" + past participle)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Patient-S + get + V.past.part (+ by Agent)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Function:** Express affective involvement; emphasize process; colloquial register
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She **got fired**." (informal; emotional involvement)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "He **got caught** in the rain." (affected; process-oriented)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "They **got involved** in the project." (participatory involvement)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "The car **got damaged** in the accident." (affected; emphasizes impact)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "The food **got spoiled**." (colloquial; affective)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "We **got embarrassed** by the incident." (emotional response)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Semantic Differences:**
