│       │   │   │   │   ├── Get-passive emphasizes result/affectedness more than be-passive
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Compare: "The window was broken" (factual) vs. "The window got broken" (emphasizes damage)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Get implies some participation/causality: "got arrested" (implies some involvement)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Be is more neutral: "was arrested" (factual statement)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Register:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Conversational/informal (not academic/formal)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── American English more common than British
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Pragmatically engaged (speaker has affective relation to event)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Less appropriate for scientific/legal writing
│       │   │   │   └── **Constraints:**
│       │   │   │       ├── Cannot use with eventless passive ("He got been tall.") *not possible
│       │   │   │       ├── Works best with resultative/affective verbs
│       │   │   │       ├── Rarely takes by-agent (sounds awkward): *"got broken by John"
│       │   │   │       └── Usually short passive form (agent omitted)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **4. IMPERSONAL PASSIVE (It is said that... / There is V.past.part that...)**
│       │   │       ├── **Structure:** It/There + be + V.past.part + that-clause (or similar)
│       │   │       ├── **Function:** Express reported speech/belief; avoid naming source; generic statements
│       │   │       ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │       │   ├── "**It is said that** he is a genius." (Impersonal report; source unnamed)
│       │   │       │   ├── "**It is believed that** the theory is correct." (Passive construction avoiding source)
│       │   │       │   ├── "**It is known that** she left early." (Universal/general knowledge)
│       │   │       │   ├── "**It is reported that** sales have increased." (Report without source attribution)
│       │   │       │   ├── "**There is word that** he is returning." (Colloquial; vague source)
│       │   │       │   └── "**It is understood that** we will meet tomorrow." (Assumption/shared knowledge)
│       │   │       ├── **Semantic Properties:**
│       │   │       │   ├── Expresses attributed proposition without explicit source
│       │   │       │   ├── "It is said" = someone said (but who? unspecified)
│       │   │       │   ├── Functions like reported speech but through passive structure
│       │   │       │   ├── Can express uncertainty/hearsay: "It is claimed that..." (suggests possible falsity)
│       │   │       │   └── Formal/written register
│       │   │       ├── **Common Verbs in Impersonal Passive:**
│       │   │       │   ├── say, believe, think, know, report, claim, suggest, suppose, assume, understand
│       │   │       │   ├── hope, regret, fear, consider, discover
│       │   │       │   └── Note: Many copular/mental state verbs can occur here
│       │   │       └── **Comparison with Personal Passive:**
│       │   │           ├── Personal: "**She** is believed **to be** a genius." (object infinitive passive)
│       │   │           ├── Impersonal: "**It** is believed **that she is** a genius." (expletive it + that-clause)
│       │   │           └── Same meaning; different structural patterns
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **Passive with Tense & Aspect Combinations**
│       │   │   ├── **Simple Passive (Bare be + past participle):**
│       │   │   │   ├── Present: "The book **is read**." (timeless/habitual)
│       │   │   │   ├── Past: "The book **was read**." (single past event)
│       │   │   │   └── Future: "The book **will be read**." (scheduled future)
│       │   │   ├── **Continuous Passive (be + being + past participle):**
│       │   │   │   ├── Present: "The book **is being read**." (ongoing now)
│       │   │   │   ├── Past: "The book **was being read** when I arrived." (ongoing in past)
│       │   │   │   └── Cannot form future continuous passive: *"will be being read" (awkward)
│       │   │   ├── **Perfect Passive (have + been + past participle):**
│       │   │   │   ├── Present: "The book **has been read**." (recent past with present relevance)
│       │   │   │   ├── Past: "The book **had been read**." (prior to another past event)
│       │   │   │   └── Future: "The book **will have been read**." (complete before future time)
│       │   │   └── **Modal Passive (Modal + be + past participle):**
│       │   │       ├── Ability: "This can **be done**." (possibility)
│       │   │       ├── Permission: "This may **be done**." (allowance)
│       │   │       ├── Obligation: "This must **be done**." (necessity)
│       │   │       ├── Possibility: "This might **be done**." (may or may not happen)
│       │   │       └── **Complex modals:** "The project will have **to be** completed on time." (must + passive)
│       │   │
│       │   └── **Active vs. Passive: When to Use Which**
│       │       ├── **Prefer Active When:**
│       │       │   ├── Agent is known and important
│       │       │   ├── Clarity/directness desired (more readable)
│       │       │   ├── Creating emphasis on doer: "**I** wrote the report."
│       │       │   └── Conciseness matters (active often shorter)
│       │       ├── **Prefer Passive When:**
│       │       │   ├── Agent is unknown, irrelevant, or multiple (team effort)
│       │       │   ├── Objectivity/scientific tone desired
│       │       │   ├── Focus should be on patient/result: "**This discovery** will revolutionize medicine."
│       │       │   ├── Politeness/evasion (avoiding blame): "**Mistakes were made**."
│       │       │   └── Patient is already discourse topic (cohesion)
│       │       └── **Common Mistake:** Overusing passive → unclear/wordy prose
│       │           ├── ✘ "The decision was made by the committee to implement the program."
│       │           ├── ✔ "The committee decided to implement the program."
│       │           └── Passive = 14 words; Active = 9 words (more concise)
│       │
│       └── **Fronting / Extraposition (Shifting elements for emphasis/focus/balance)**
│           │
│           ├── **FRONTING (Moving non-subject element to sentence-initial position)**
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **Definition & Function**
│           │   │   ├── **What is Fronting?**
│           │   │   │   └── Moving a non-subject constituent (object, adjective, adverbial, or predicate)
│           │   │   │       from its canonical position to sentence-initial position for emphasis,
│           │   │   │       contrast, or discourse cohesion.
│           │   │   ├── **Core Function:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Create emphasis/contrast on fronted element
│           │   │   │   ├── Establish discourse topic or theme
│           │   │   │   ├── Achieve stylistic/poetic effect
│           │   │   │   ├── Focus on information structure (theme-rheme)
│           │   │   │   └── Create narrative/literary tone
│           │   │   └── **Key Difference from Inversion:**
│           │   │       ├── Fronting: Element moves to initial; verb/word order otherwise unchanged
│           │   │       ├── Inversion: Element moves + verb moves before subject (S-V inversion)
│           │   │       ├── "**That book**, I love." (fronting; no verb movement)
│           │   │       ├── Vs. "**Do** I love that book!" (inversion; verb moves before subject)
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **4 Major Types of Fronting**
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   ├── **1. OBJECT FRONTING (NP/DP object moves to front)**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Object + Subject + Verb (remaining)
│           │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "I love **that book**."
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Fronted: "**That book**, I love." (emphatic; contrastive)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "She ate **the cake**."
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Fronted: "**The cake**, she ate." (focus on object; implies others didn't)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "They trust **her**."
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Fronted: "**Her**, they trust." (emphatic contrast)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "No one understood **the answer**."
│           │   │   │   │   └── Fronted: "**The answer**, no one understood." (mysterious/emphasized)
│           │   │   │   ├── **Stylistic Effect:**
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Contrastive: "I like apples, but **oranges**, I prefer." (oranges contrasted with apples)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Emphatic: "**This job**, I cannot do." (strong negation)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Narrative: "**The treasure**, they finally found." (suspense/climax)
│           │   │   │   │   └── Unusual/marked tone (not standard word order)
│           │   │   │   └── **Pronunciation:** Often with pause/intonation break after fronted object
│           │   │   │       └── "**The cake**, | I ate." (| = intonational break)
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   ├── **2. ADVERBIAL FRONTING (Adverbial/adjunct moves to front)**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Adverbial + Subject + Verb
│           │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Temporal fronting:
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "I saw her **yesterday**."
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── Fronted: "**Yesterday**, I saw her." (focuses on time; discourse frame)
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Tomorrow**, we begin the project." (tomorrow = established frame)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Locative fronting:
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "We met **in the park**."
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── Fronted: "**In the park**, we met." (where = topic)
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── "**Under the bridge**, the fugitive hid." (location-focused narrative)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Manner fronting:
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "He spoke **carefully**."
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── Fronted: "**Carefully**, he spoke." (deliberate manner emphasized)
│           │   │   │   │   └── Causal/Reason fronting:
│           │   │   │   │       ├── "**Because of illness**, she left early." (reason established upfront)
│           │   │   │   │       ├── "**Due to budget cuts**, the program was cancelled." (cause = foregrounded)
│           │   │   │   ├── **Frequency & Naturalness:**
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Temporal fronting: Very common/natural (sets discourse time frame)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Locative fronting: Common in narrative/literary contexts
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Manner fronting: Less common; more emphatic
│           │   │   │   │   └── **Note:** Adverbial fronting more acceptable than object fronting in English
│           │   │   │   └── **Discourse Function:**
│           │   │   │       ├── Establishes spatio-temporal context
│           │   │   │       ├── Prepares hearer for main clause information
│           │   │   │       └── Creates cohesion with previous discourse
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   ├── **3. COMPLEMENT FRONTING (Predicate adjective/noun moves to front)**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** Complement (AP/NP) + Subject + Verb
│           │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Adjective complement fronting:
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "She seems **happy**."
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── Fronted: "**Happy**, she seems." (archaic/poetic; literary tone)
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "He was **intelligent**."
│           │   │   │   │   │   ├── Fronted: "**Intelligent**, he was." (old-fashioned; emphasis)
│           │   │   │   ├── Noun complement fronting:
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "He became **a doctor**."
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Fronted: "**A doctor**, he became." (less common; marked)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "She is **the expert**."
│           │   │   │   │   └── Fronted: "**The expert**, she is." (contrastive; rare)
│           │   │   │   ├── **Register & Style:**
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Highly literary/archaic
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Creates Shakespearean/classical tone
│           │   │   │   │   ├── Rarely used in modern English (except intentional effect)
│           │   │   │   │   └── Example: "Wise, indeed, was the choice." (literary fronting)
│           │   │   │   └── **Constraints:**
│           │   │   │       ├── Typically requires comma after fronted complement
│           │   │   │       ├── Cannot use with all verbs (mainly copular be/seem/appear/become)
│           │   │   │       └── Uncommon in speech (primarily written/literary)
│           │   │   │
│           │   │   └── **4. PREDICATE FRONTING (Bare verb/VP moves to front; rare)**
│           │   │       ├── **Structure:** Verb + Subject + Verb (repetition of V)
│           │   │       ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │       │   ├── "**Run**, he did." (bare infinitive fronted; do-support for tense)
│           │   │       │   ├── "**Sing**, they would." (infinitive fronted; would for tense)
│           │   │       │   ├── "**Leave**, we must." (fronted infinitive; modal must for tense)
│           │   │       │   └── "**Go**, he should." (infinitive fronted; should for tense)
│           │   │       ├── **Note:** DO-SUPPORT or MODAL required (English needs tense marking)
│       │   │   ├── **Register & Frequency:**
│       │   │   │   ├── Very rare in modern English
│       │   │   │   ├── Primarily archaic/poetic (biblical/Shakespearean tone)
│       │   │   │   ├── Creates formal/emphatic register
│       │   │   │   └── Example: "**Forgive**, we must, those who trespass." (religious/archaic)
│       │   │
│       │   └── **Constraints & Effects of Fronting**
│       │       ├── **Topicalization Effects:**
│       │       │   ├── Fronted element becomes sentence topic
│       │       │   ├── Creates contrastive interpretation: "**That book**, I like." (implies others, I don't like)
│       │       │   ├── Establishes discourse theme
│       │       │   └── Can focus attention on fronted element
│       │       ├── **Prosody & Punctuation:**
│       │       │   ├── Usually marked by comma or intonational pause: "**In the park**, we met." (comma in writing)
│       │       │   ├── Intonational break in speech (pause after fronted element)
│       │       │   ├── Stress on fronted element (contrastive stress)
│       │       │   └── Distinguishes from normal word order: "In the park **we met**." (no fronting)
│       │       └── **Extraction Constraints:**
│       │           ├── Some elements cannot be fronted (wh-islands, complex NPs)
│       │           ├── ✓ Simple objects can front: "**That book**, I like."
│       │           ├── ✗ Complex fronting problematic: "*A [book that is blue], I like." (less acceptable)
│       │           └── Depends on syntactic complexity and semantic coherence
│       │
│       └── **EXTRAPOSITION (Postponing heavy/complex material; using dummy 'it')**
│           │
│           ├── **Definition & Function**
│           │   ├── **What is Extraposition?**
│           │   │   └── Moving a heavy or complex constituent (usually a clause or infinitive) from
│           │   │       its canonical position (subject) to the end of the sentence, with a dummy
│           │   │       expletive "it" replacing it in subject position. Balances sentence weight.
│           │   ├── **Core Function:**
│           │   │   ├── Avoid heavy/awkward subjects
│           │   │   ├── Balance sentence information structure (weight principle)
│           │   │   ├── Improve readability/clarity
│           │   │   ├── Place emphasis on extraposed element (sentence-final position)
│           │   │   └── Create more natural-sounding sentences
│           │   └── **Mechanism:**
│           │       ├── Canonical: [Heavy Subject] + Copula + Complement
│           │       ├── Extraposed: It + Copula + Complement + [Heavy Subject]
│           │       ├── Dummy "it" fills subject position syntactically
│           │       └── Weight principle: Light element (it) before heavy element (clause)
│           │
│           ├── **4 Major Types of Extraposition**
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **1. INFINITIVE EXTRAPOSITION (to-clause postponed)**
│           │   │   ├── **Structure:** It + Copula/Predicate + AP + [to-clause]
│           │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**To learn languages** is difficult."
│           │   │   │   ├── Extraposed: "**It** is difficult **to learn languages**." (it-extraposition; easier to read)
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**To finish this project** seems impossible."
│           │   │   │   ├── Extraposed: "**It** seems impossible **to finish this project**."
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**To understand quantum physics** requires years of study."
│           │   │   │   ├── Extraposed: "**It** requires years of study **to understand quantum physics**."
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**To succeed in this career** takes determination."
│           │   │   │   └── Extraposed: "**It** takes determination **to succeed in this career**."
│           │   │   ├── **Predicates Allowing Extraposition:**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Easy/difficult/hard/impossible:** "It is easy to learn."
│           │   │   │   ├── **Important/necessary/crucial:** "It is important to attend."
│           │   │   │   ├── **Verbs of seeming:** seem, appear, happen, turn out, prove
│           │   │   │   ├── **Verbs requiring effort:** take, require, demand, involve
│           │   │   │   └── **Other:** possible, likely, unlikely, unfortunate
│           │   │   └── **Semantic Shift:**
│           │   │       ├── Extraposition often preserves meaning
│           │   │       ├── BUT: Some predicates change meaning: "**To smoke**is not forbidden." vs. "**It** is not forbidden to smoke."
│           │   │       └── Information structure changes (weight vs. emphasis)
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **2. THAT-CLAUSE EXTRAPOSITION (that-clause postponed)**
│           │   │   ├── **Structure:** It + Copula/Adjective + AP + [that-clause]
│           │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**That he left** is clear."
│           │   │   │   ├── Extraposed: "**It** is clear **that he left**." (more natural; weight-balanced)
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**That she won** is surprising."
│           │   │   │   ├── Extraposed: "**It** is surprising **that she won**."
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**That violence increases** is obvious."
│           │   │   │   ├── Extraposed: "**It** is obvious **that violence increases**."
│           │   │   │   └── Canonical: "**That he was guilty** was undeniable."
│           │   │   │       Extraposed: "**It** was undeniable **that he was guilty**."
│           │   │   ├── **Common Predicates:**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Clear/evident/obvious:** "It is clear that..."
│           │   │   │   ├── **True/false/certain:** "It is true that..."
│           │   │   │   ├── **Good/bad/unfortunate:** "It is unfortunate that..."
│           │   │   │   ├── **Likely/possible/probable:** "It is likely that..."
│           │   │   │   └── **Verbs:** seem, appear, happen, matter, turn out, prove
│           │   │   └── **Frequency:**
│           │   │       ├── Very common in English (especially spoken)
│           │   │       ├── Canonical form ("That...-clause as subject") feels formal/literary
│           │   │       ├── Extraposed form (it-form) feels more natural/conversational
│           │   │       └── Most English speakers prefer extraposed version
│           │   │
│           │   ├── **3. GERUND EXTRAPOSITION (Gerund/NP postponed)**
│           │   │   ├── **Structure:** It + Copula/Verb + AP + [gerund/NP]
│           │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**Smoking in restaurants** is prohibited."
│           │   │   │   ├── Extraposed: "**It** is prohibited **smoking in restaurants**." (less common; gerund rarely extraposed)
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**Leaving early** would be disrespectful."
│           │   │   │   ├── Extraposed: "**It** would be disrespectful **leaving early**." (archaic/rare)
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**Her constant complaining** is annoying."
│           │   │   │   ├── Extraposed: "**It** is annoying **her constant complaining**." (extremely rare)
│           │   │   ├── **Note:** Gerund extraposition is RARE in modern English
│           │   │   │   ├── Most speakers prefer: "It's no use crying over spilled milk." (idiom)
│           │   │   │   ├── Avoid: *"It is no use—crying over spilled milk." (not natural)
│           │   │   │   └── Prefers infinitive alternative: "It's no use to cry..."
│           │   │   └── **Idiomatic Uses:**
│           │   │       ├── "It's no use...ing" (set phrase)
│           │   │       ├── "It's a waste of time...ing" (set phrase)
│           │   │       └── Limited to fixed expressions
│           │   │
│           │   └── **4. RELATIVE CLAUSE EXTRAPOSITION / CLEFT SENTENCES**
│           │       ├── **Cleft Structures (It was John who...)**
│           │       │   ├── **Structure:** It + Copula + NP + Relative-clause
│           │       │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**John** won the prize."
│           │   │   │   ├── Cleft: "**It** was **John** who won the prize." (focus on John)
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**Yesterday** I saw her."
│           │   │   │   ├── Cleft: "**It** was **yesterday** that I saw her." (focus on time)
│           │   │   │   ├── Canonical: "**She broke the window** with a stone."
│           │   │   │   ├── Cleft: "**It** was **with a stone** that she broke the window." (focus on instrument)
│           │   │   │   └── Canonical: "**The students** failed the exam."
│           │   │   │       Cleft: "**It** was **the students** who failed the exam." (focus on agent)
│           │   │   ├── **Inverse Cleft (Pseudocleft: What/Whatever-clause)**
│           │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** [Wh-clause] + Copula + [Focused constituent]
│           │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│           │   │   │   │   ├── "**What I want** is **peace**." (focused element at end)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── "**All he needed** was **love**." (focused element at end)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── "**Where they met** was **in Paris**." (location focused)
│           │   │   │   │   ├── "**How she solved it** was **by using logic**." (manner focused)
│           │   │   │   │   └── "**What really matters** is **family**." (complement focused)
│           │   │   ├── **Function of Clefts:**
│           │   │   │   ├── Focus/emphasize specific constituent
│           │   │   │   ├── Highlight contrast: "It was **John**, not Mary, who won."
│           │   │   │   ├── Create emphatic/marked interpretation
│           │   │   │   ├── Improve information structure (separate known from new information)
│           │   │   │   └── Allow flexibility in focus assignment
│           │   │   └── **Comparison with Simple Sentences:**
│           │   │       ├── Simple: "John won the prize." (all-focus; new information)
│           │   │       ├── Cleft: "It was **John** who won the prize." (John is focus; action is background)
│           │   │       ├── Simple: no particular focus; neutral statement
│           │   │       └── Cleft: highlights the focused element through special structure
│           │
│           └── **Pragmatic Effects & Constraints**
│               ├── **Weight Principle:**
│               │   ├── Light subjects (pronouns, simple NPs) → Heavy complements: natural order
│               │   ├── Heavy subjects (complex clauses) → Light dummy "it": extraposition preferred
│               │   ├── Example: "**It** is clear that he will return." (heavy clause postponed)
│               │   └── Psychology: Human parsing prefers light-to-heavy ordering
│               ├── **Information Structure:**
│               │   ├── Extraposed element receives more prominence (sentence-final stress)
│               │   ├── Dummy "it" is prosodically weak (anaphoric; not stressed)
│               │   ├── Creates theme-rheme structure: theme (it) + rheme (postponed clause)
│               │   └── Improves discourse coherence
│               ├── **Constraints:**
│               │   ├── NOT all clauses can be extraposed: *"It seems that I will sleep" (base infinitive form preferred)
│               │   ├── Some adjectives require extraposition: "**It** is difficult to learn Chinese." (better than "To learn Chinese is difficult.")
│               │   ├── Extraposition optional for most predicates (both forms possible)
│               │   └── Register: Extraposition is more colloquial/modern; canonical form = more formal
│               └── **Stylistic Variation:**
│                   ├── Academic writing: Often uses extraposition (natural-sounding)
│                   ├── Poetry: May use canonical form (weight/rhythm effects)
│                   ├── Formal writing: Varies by preference (both acceptable)
│                   └── Speech: Strongly prefers extraposition (weight principle)
│
├── **IV. GOVERNMENT & CASE**
│   │
│   ├── **A. Governing Heads (Elements that dictate form/case of dependents)**
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **1. Verb (Assigns case to objects/complements; selects complement types)**
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **(a) Overview: Verbs as Governors**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Verbs govern their complements by assigning case and selecting complement types
│   │   │   │   ├── **Two Types of Government:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Case Assignment:** Verbs assign objective case to NP objects
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Subcategorization:** Verbs select what complement types they take
│   │   │   │   ├── **Argument Structure:**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Subject (External Argument):** Not governed by V, but by T (Tense)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Object (Internal Argument):** Directly governed by V
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Complements:** Required elements selected by the verb
│   │   │   │   └── **Why This Matters:**
│   │   │   │       ├── Determines correct pronoun case after verbs
│   │   │   │       ├── Explains why some verbs take infinitives, others take gerunds
│   │   │   │       └── Tested on exams: verb complement errors are common
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **(b) Case Assignment by Verbs**
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(i) Direct Object → Objective (Accusative) Case**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Rule:** Transitive verbs assign objective case to direct objects
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I saw **him**." (him = objective)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She loves **me**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "They invited **us**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "We called **her**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "**Whom** did you see?" (formal)
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Incorrect:**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── ✘ "I saw **he**." → him
│   │   │   │   │       ├── ✘ "She loves **I**." → me
│   │   │   │   │       └── ✘ "They invited **we**." → us
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(ii) Indirect Object → Objective Case**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Rule:** Ditransitive verbs assign objective to both objects
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Give **me** the book." (me = indirect object)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She told **him** a story."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Send **her** a letter."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "Show **us** the way."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **With Preposition 'to':**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── ✔ "Give the book to **me**."
│   │   │   │   │       ├── ✔ "Send a letter to **her**."
│   │   │   │   │       └── **Note:** 'to' also assigns objective case
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(iii) ECM: Exceptional Case Marking**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Verb assigns objective to subject of infinitive complement
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Pattern:** [V] + [NP-objective] + [to VP]
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I believe **him** to be honest." (him = subject of 'to be')
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "We expect **her** to win."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "They consider **me** to be qualified."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "She proved **it** to be false."
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **ECM Verbs:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Belief:** believe, consider, think, know, find, imagine, suppose
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Perception:** see, hear, watch, feel, notice, observe
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── **Expectation:** expect, want, need, prefer, require
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **Declaration:** declare, prove, show, report, certify
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Test:** Replace infinitive with that-clause:
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "I believe him to be smart." ≈ "I believe that **he** is smart."
│   │   │   │   │       └── Objective in infinitive = Nominative in finite clause
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(iv) Bare Infinitive Constructions**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Pattern:** [V] + [NP-objective] + [bare infinitive (no 'to')]
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Causative Verbs:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Let **him** go."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Make **her** understand."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Have **them** wait."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "Help **me** carry this." (optional 'to')
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Perception Verbs:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I saw **him** leave."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "We heard **her** sing."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "They watched **us** play."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "I felt **it** move."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Incorrect:**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── ✘ "Let **he** go." → him
│   │   │   │   │       ├── ✘ "Make **she** understand." → her
│   │   │   │   │       └── ✘ "I saw **they** leave." → them
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   └── **(v) No Case Assignment (Intransitive/Linking Verbs)**
│   │   │   │       ├── **Intransitive Verbs:** No object to case-mark
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "He **sleeps**." (no object)
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "She **arrived**." (no object)
│   │   │   │       │   └── "They **laughed**." (no object)
│   │   │   │       └── **Linking Verbs:** Subject complement (not object)
│   │   │   │           ├── "It is **I**." (formal - nominative complement)
│   │   │   │           ├── "She became **a doctor**." (NP complement)
│   │   │   │           └── **Note:** 'Be' doesn't assign case; complement = nominative (formal)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **(c) Transitivity: Verb Classes by Object Requirements**
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(i) Intransitive Verbs (No Object)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Cannot take a direct object
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Pure Intransitives:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── sleep, arrive, die, fall, go, come, appear, exist
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "She **arrived**." / "He **died**." / "They **exist**."
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Unergative (Agent-based):** laugh, cry, dance, run, talk
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "She **laughed**." / "He **cried**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Unaccusative (Theme-based):** arrive, fall, appear, break
│   │   │   │   │       └── "The glass **broke**." (glass = theme, no agent)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(ii) Transitive Verbs (One Object)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Requires a direct object
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── see, hit, love, want, find, make, take, know, like, meet
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I **saw him**." / "She **loves music**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "They **want help**." / "We **found it**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Object required:**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── ✔ "She hit **the ball**."
│   │   │   │   │       └── ✘ "She hit." (incomplete without object)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(iii) Ditransitive Verbs (Two Objects)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Takes indirect + direct object
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Pattern:** [V] + [IO] + [DO] OR [V] + [DO] + [to/for IO]
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── give, send, tell, show, offer, teach, bring, lend, write
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "Give **me** **the book**." = "Give **the book** to **me**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She told **him** **a story**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "They sent **her** **a letter**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Dative Alternation:**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "Give him a book." (double object)
│   │   │   │   │       └── "Give a book to him." (prepositional)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(iv) Complex Transitive Verbs (Object + Complement)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Object followed by object complement
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **With Adjective Complement:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "They made **her happy**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I consider **him intelligent**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "She painted **the door red**."
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **With Noun Complement:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "They elected **him president**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "We call **her a genius**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "They named **the baby John**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Verbs:** make, consider, find, elect, name, call, think, declare
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   └── **(v) Ambitransitive Verbs (Can Be Transitive or Intransitive)**
│   │   │   │       ├── **Definition:** Can be used with or without object
│   │   │   │       ├── **Examples:**
│   │   │   │       │   ├── **eat:** "She **eats**." / "She **eats cake**."
│   │   │   │       │   ├── **read:** "He **reads**." / "He **reads books**."
│   │   │   │       │   ├── **sing:** "They **sing**." / "They **sing songs**."
│   │   │   │       │   ├── **write:** "I **write**." / "I **write letters**."
│   │   │   │       │   └── **play:** "Children **play**." / "Children **play games**."
│   │   │   │       └── **Ergative Verbs (Object becomes subject):**
│   │   │   │           ├── "She **broke the glass**." (transitive)
│   │   │   │           ├── "The glass **broke**." (intransitive - same verb)
│   │   │   │           └── Examples: break, open, close, melt, freeze, boil, sink
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **(d) Complement Selection (Subcategorization)**
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(i) Definition**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Subcategorization:** The property of verbs specifying complement types
│   │   │   │   │   ├── Verbs "select" or "require" specific complement structures
│   │   │   │   │   └── This is lexically determined (specific to each verb)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(ii) Verb + NP (Noun Phrase Complement)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Transitive:** "want **money**" / "see **him**"
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Ditransitive:** "give **her** **a book**"
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Case:** NP receives objective case
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(iii) Verb + To-Infinitive**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Control Verbs (Subject Control):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Subject of main verb = understood subject of infinitive
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I want **to go**." (I go)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She decided **to leave**." (she leaves)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Verbs: want, decide, try, hope, expect, promise, refuse, agree
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Control Verbs (Object Control):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Object of main verb = understood subject of infinitive
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I persuaded **him** **to go**." (he goes)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She asked **me** **to help**." (I help)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Verbs: persuade, ask, tell, order, force, allow, encourage
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Raising Verbs:**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── Subject "raised" from infinitive clause
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "She seems **to understand**."
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "He appears **to be happy**."
│   │   │   │   │       └── Verbs: seem, appear, tend, happen, turn out
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(iv) Verb + -ing (Gerund/Participle Complement)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Gerund Object:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I enjoy **reading**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She avoided **meeting** him."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "They finished **working**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Verbs: enjoy, avoid, finish, mind, suggest, consider, deny
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Perception + -ing (Progressive aspect):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I saw him **running**." (in progress)
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── Compare: "I saw him **run**." (complete action)
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Verbs: see, hear, watch, feel, notice
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Subject of Gerund (Formal vs. Informal):**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── Formal: "I appreciate **his helping**." (possessive)
│   │   │   │   │       └── Informal: "I appreciate **him helping**." (objective)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(v) Verb + Bare Infinitive**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Causative:** make, let, have
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "Make her **go**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "Let him **stay**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "Have them **wait**."
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Perception:** see, hear, watch, feel, notice
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I saw him **leave**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── "We heard her **sing**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Help (optional 'to'):**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "Help me **carry** this." (bare)
│   │   │   │   │       └── "Help me **to carry** this." (to-infinitive)
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(vi) Verb + That-Clause (Finite Complement)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **Declarative Verbs:**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I believe **that it's true**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She knows **that he left**."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── Verbs: believe, know, think, say, report, claim, assume
│   │   │   │   │   ├── **'That' Deletion (Optional):**
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I think **(that)** he's right."
│   │   │   │   │   │   ├── "She said **(that)** she'd come."
│   │   │   │   │   │   └── **No deletion** after noun: "The fact **that** you're here..."
│   │   │   │   │   └── **Subjunctive (Mandative):**
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "I demand **that he go**." (go, not goes)
│   │   │   │   │       ├── "She suggests **that he be** present."
│   │   │   │   │       └── Verbs: demand, suggest, recommend, insist, require
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(vii) Verb + Wh-Clause (Embedded Question)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "I wonder **what happened**."
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "She knows **where he lives**."
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "Tell me **who called**."
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "I don't know **if/whether he's coming**."
│   │   │   │   │   └── Verbs: wonder, know, ask, tell, discover, decide
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   ├── **(viii) Verb + Adjective/NP Complement (Resultative)**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "She drove me **crazy**." (adjective complement)
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "They painted the door **red**."
│   │   │   │   │   ├── "We consider him **a genius**." (NP complement)
│   │   │   │   │   └── Verbs: make, drive, render, consider, find, think
│   │   │   │   │
│   │   │   │   └── **(ix) Verb + Prepositional Phrase Complement**
│   │   │   │       ├── **Obligatory PP (Part of verb meaning):**
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "rely **on** you" / "consist **of** parts" / "belong **to** her"
│   │   │   │       │   ├── "depend **on** / refer **to** / result **in**"
│   │   │   │       │   └── ✘ "It consists." (incomplete - needs "of")
│   │   │   │       └── **Optional PP (Adjunct):**
│   │   │   │           ├── "He ran **(to the store)**." (ran is complete alone)
│   │   │   │           └── "She sang **(in the shower)**."
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   └── **(e) Exam-Focused Summary**
│   │   │       ├── **Key Rules:**
│   │   │       │   ├── All verb objects take **objective case** (him, her, me, them, us, whom)
│   │   │       │   ├── After causatives/perception verbs + bare infinitive: objective case
│   │   │       │   ├── ECM verbs assign objective to infinitive subject
│   │   │       │   └── Subject of finite verb = nominative (not governed by V)
│   │   │       ├── **Common Errors:**
│   │   │       │   ├── ✘ "Let **he** go." → Let **him** go
│   │   │       │   ├── ✘ "I saw **they** leave." → I saw **them** leave
│   │   │       │   ├── ✘ "We believe **he** to be..." → We believe **him** to be
│   │   │       │   └── ✘ "I want **that** you go." → I want you to go
│   │   │       └── **Gerund vs. Infinitive (Meaning Change):**
│   │   │           ├── "I stopped **smoking**." (quit the habit)
│   │   │           ├── "I stopped **to smoke**." (paused in order to smoke)
│   │   │           ├── "I remember **doing** it." (recall past action)
│   │   │           └── "I remember **to do** it." (remember future obligation)
│   │   │
│   │   ├── **2. Preposition (Always governs objective/accusative case for its object)**
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   ├── **a. General Rule**
│   │   │   │   ├── **Objective Case for Pronouns**
│   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ between you and me
│   │   │   │   │   └── ✘ between you and I
│   │   │   │   └── **NP as Object (No case visible on nouns)**
│   │   │   │       └── In the house (house = objective)
│   │   │   │
│   │   │   └── **b. Subtypes by Preposition Type**
│   │   │       ├── **Simple Prepositions (at, by, for, from, in, of, on, to, with)**
│   │   │       │   └── Govern NP: at home, by car
│   │   │       ├── **Compound Prepositions (because of, instead of)**
│   │   │       │   └── Govern NP: instead of him
│   │   │       ├── **Participial Prepositions (considering, during, regarding)**
│   │   │       │   └── Govern NP: during the meeting
│   │   │       └── **Prepositions Governing Clauses (Rare)**
│   │   │           └── Before he arrived (he = nominative; clause as object of preposition)
│   │   │
│   │   └── **3. Adjective (Governs complements, often prepositional)**
│   │       │
│   │       ├── **a. Governs Prepositional Complements**
│   │       │   ├── **Obligatory PP**
│   │       │   │   ├── Afraid of snakes, dependent on others
│   │       │   │   └── Subtypes: Directional (adjacent to), Causal (angry at)
│   │       │   └── **Optional PP**
│   │       │       └── Happy about the news (happy alone is fine)
│   │       │
│   │       └── **b. Fixed Adjective + Preposition Patterns (Idiomatic Combinations)**
│   │           ├── With "of": afraid of, aware of, capable of, fond of, full of, jealous of, proud of, sure of, tired of
│   │           ├── With "in": interested in, rich in, successful in
│   │           ├── With "at": angry at, bad at, good at, surprised at
│   │           ├── With "to": accustomed to, addicted to, kind to, similar to
│   │           ├── With "with": angry with, bored with, familiar with, pleased with, satisfied with
│   │           ├── With "about": anxious about, excited about, worried about
│   │           ├── With "for": famous for, ready for, responsible for, sorry for
│   │           ├── With "from": different from, safe from
│   │           └── With "on": dependent on, keen on
│   │
│   └── **B. Case (Morphological marking on words, mainly pronouns in English, indicating syntactic role)**
│       ├── **1. Types of Case (Modern English) (English has 3 main cases: visible on pronouns; nouns show only possessive)**
│       │
│       ├── **(a) Overview & Definition**
│       │   ├── **Definition:** Case is the morphological form of a noun/pronoun indicating its syntactic role
│       │   ├── **Modern English Case System:**
│       │   │   ├── **3 Cases:** Subjective (Nominative), Objective (Accusative), Possessive (Genitive)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Visible on Pronouns:** I/me/my, he/him/his, she/her/her, etc.
│       │   │   │   ├── **Nouns:** Only show possessive ('s/s'); no subject/object distinction
│       │   │   │   └── **Historical Note:** Old English had 4+ cases; Modern English is greatly simplified
│       │   │   ├── **Why Case Matters:**
│       │   │   │   ├── Determines correct pronoun form in formal English
│       │   │   │   ├── Common source of errors (e.g., "between you and I" = wrong)
│       │   │   │   └── Tested frequently on standardized exams (SAT, GRE, TOEFL)
│       │   │   └── **Case vs. Position:**
│       │   │       ├── **Old English:** Case determined function (flexible word order)
│       │   │       └── **Modern English:** Word order determines function (case mostly vestigial)
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(b) Complete Pronoun Declension Tables**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) Personal Pronouns**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   │        │ Subjective │ Objective │ Possessive (Dep.) │ Possessive (Indep.) │
│       │   │   │   │        │------------|-----------|-------------------|---------------------|
│       │   │   │   │ 1st Sg │ I          │ me        │ my                │ mine                │
│       │   │   │   │ 2nd Sg │ you        │ you       │ your              │ yours               │
│       │   │   │   │ 3rd Sg │ he         │ him       │ his               │ his                 │
│       │   │   │   │        │ she        │ her       │ her               │ hers                │
│       │   │   │   │        │ it         │ it        │ its               │ its (rare)          │
│       │   │   │   │ 1st Pl │ we         │ us        │ our               │ ours                │
│       │   │   │   │ 2nd Pl │ you        │ you       │ your              │ yours               │
│       │   │   │   │ 3rd Pl │ they       │ them      │ their             │ theirs              │
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **Notes:**
│       │   │   │       ├── "You" is identical in subjective/objective (older: thou/thee)
│       │   │   │       ├── "It" is identical in subjective/objective
│       │   │   │       ├── "His" is identical in dependent/independent possessive
│       │   │   │       └── "They/them/their" now also used as singular gender-neutral
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Interrogative/Relative Pronouns**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   │           │ Subjective │ Objective │ Possessive │
│       │   │   │   │           │------------|-----------|------------|
│       │   │   │   │ For people│ who        │ whom      │ whose      │
│       │   │   │   │ For things│ which      │ which     │ whose/of which │
│       │   │   │   │ Either    │ that       │ that      │ (no form)  │
│       │   │   │   │           │ whoever    │ whomever  │ whosever   │
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **Notes:**
│       │   │   │       ├── "Whom" declining in informal usage (often replaced by "who")
│       │   │   │       ├── "Whose" can be used for things: "The house whose roof..."
│       │   │   │       └── "That" has no case distinction (same form for all)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(iii) Reflexive/Emphatic Pronouns (No case variation)**
│       │   │       ├── myself, yourself, himself, herself, itself
│       │   │       ├── ourselves, yourselves, themselves
│       │   │       ├── **Function:** Object referring back to subject OR for emphasis
│       │   │       └── **Note:** Always same form regardless of function
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(c) Subjective (Nominative) Case - Detailed**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) Definition & Core Concept**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** The case used for subjects of finite verbs
│       │   │   │   ├── **Forms:** I, he, she, we, they, who, whoever
│       │   │   │   ├── **Nouns:** No distinct form (same as objective)
│       │   │   │   └── **Technical:** Assigned by finite T(ense) in generative grammar
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Functions (When to Use Subjective Case)**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **A. Subject of Finite Clause**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**He** runs every morning."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**She** and **I** are going." (compound subject)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**They** believe it's true."
│       │   │   │   │   └── "The man **who** called left." (subject of relative clause)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **B. Predicate Nominative (After Linking Verbs)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── **Formal:** "It is **I**." / "It was **he** who won."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── **Formal:** "This is **she** speaking."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── **Formal:** "The winner was **he**."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ⚠ **Informal (widely accepted):** "It's **me**." / "It was **him**."
│       │   │   │   │   └── **Rule:** After "be" verbs, subject complement = same case as subject
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **C. Subject of Infinitive (Rare, formal)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**I** to do that? Never!" (elliptical)
│       │   │   │   │   └── *Note: More commonly objective: "I want **him** to go."*
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **D. In Comparisons (Formal)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She is smarter than **I** [am]."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "He runs as fast as **she** [does]."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── **Logic:** Implied verb makes pronoun a subject
│       │   │   │   │   └── ⚠ **Informal:** "...smarter than **me**" (widely used)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **E. After "as" and "than" (When Subject of Implied Clause)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "No one is as tall as **he** [is]."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She likes him more than **I** [like him]." (I = subject)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Compare: "She likes him more than [she likes] **me**." (me = object)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **F. In Inversions and Questions**
│       │   │   │       ├── "**Who** called?" (subject of question)
│       │   │   │       ├── "Never have **I** seen such beauty." (inverted - I still subject)
│       │   │   │       └── "**Whoever** did this will pay." (subject of embedded clause)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) Forms by Person and Number**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Singular:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── 1st person: **I** (unique subjective form)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── 2nd person: **you** (same as objective)
│       │   │   │   │   └── 3rd person: **he, she, it, who** (he/she distinct from objective)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **Plural:**
│       │   │   │       ├── 1st person: **we** (distinct from objective "us")
│       │   │   │       ├── 2nd person: **you** (same as objective)
│       │   │   │       └── 3rd person: **they, who** (they distinct from "them")
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iv) Formal vs. Informal Usage**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Formal (Prescriptive Grammar):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "It is **I**."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "This is **she** speaking."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "He is taller than **I**."
│       │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "**Who** did you say is calling?"
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **Informal (Widely Accepted):**
│       │   │   │       ├── ✔ "It's **me**." (standard in speech)
│       │   │   │       ├── ✔ "This is **her**." (common)
│       │   │   │       ├── ✔ "He is taller than **me**."
│       │   │   │       └── ⚠ "**Who** are you looking for?" (informal; formal = whom)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(v) Common Errors to Avoid**
│       │   │       ├── ✘ "**Me** and him went to the store." → ✔ "**He** and **I** went..."
│       │   │       ├── ✘ "**Her** and **me** are friends." → ✔ "**She** and **I** are friends."
│       │   │       ├── ✘ "**Us** students need more time." → ✔ "**We** students need..."
│       │   │       └── **Test:** Remove the other person → "**I** went" (not "**me** went")
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(d) Objective (Accusative) Case - Detailed**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) Definition & Core Concept**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** The case used for objects (direct, indirect, prepositional)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Forms:** me, him, her, us, them, whom, whomever
│       │   │   │   ├── **Nouns:** No distinct form (same as subjective)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Technical:** Assigned by verbs and prepositions
│       │   │   │   └── **Note:** Also called "accusative" (historical term for direct object case)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Functions (When to Use Objective Case)**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **A. Direct Object (Receives action of verb)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "I saw **him**."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She loves **me**."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "They invited **us** and **them**." (compound)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "**Whom** did you see?" (formal)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **B. Indirect Object (Recipient of action)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Give **me** the book."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She told **him** a story."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "They sent **her** a letter."
│       │   │   │   │   └── "Show **us** the way."
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **C. Object of Preposition (CRITICAL - Tested Often)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Give it to **me**." (NOT: to I)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Between you and **me**..." (NOT: you and I)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "For **him** and **her**." (NOT: he and she)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "With **us**." / "About **them**." / "To **whom**."
│       │   │   │   │   └── **Rule:** EVERY preposition takes objective case
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **D. Subject of Infinitive (After certain verbs)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "I want **him** to leave."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She asked **me** to help."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "They expected **us** to win."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Let **him** go." (bare infinitive)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "Make **her** understand."
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **E. Object of Verbal (Gerund, Infinitive, Participle)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Seeing **her** made me happy." (object of gerund)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "To help **them** is my goal." (object of infinitive)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "Having seen **him**, I left." (object of participle)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **F. In Comparisons (Informal, or when object of implied verb)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She likes him more than [she likes] **me**." (me = object)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Informal: "He's taller than **me**." (widely accepted)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Compare: "...more than **I** [like him]." (I = subject)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **G. In Exclamations**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Him** again!"
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Poor **me**!"
│       │   │   │   │   └── "Lucky **her**!"
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **H. After "but" and "except" (When meaning "excluding")**
│       │   │   │       ├── "Everyone but **me** was invited."
│       │   │   │       ├── "No one except **him** knew."
│       │   │   │       └── **Note:** "But" and "except" act as prepositions here
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) Forms by Person and Number**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Singular:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── 1st person: **me** (distinct from "I")
│       │   │   │   │   ├── 2nd person: **you** (same as subjective)
│       │   │   │   │   └── 3rd person: **him, her, it, whom** (him/her distinct)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **Plural:**
│       │   │   │       ├── 1st person: **us** (distinct from "we")
│       │   │   │       ├── 2nd person: **you** (same as subjective)
│       │   │   │       └── 3rd person: **them, whom** (them distinct from "they")
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iv) Who vs. Whom (Detailed Guide)**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Basic Rule:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── **Who** = Subject (like he/she/they)
│       │   │   │   │   └── **Whom** = Object (like him/her/them)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Test Method (Substitute he/him):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Rephrase the question/clause as a statement
│       │   │   │   │   ├── If "he" fits → use **who**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── If "him" fits → use **whom**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── Example: "**Who/Whom** did you see?"
│       │   │   │   │   │   └── "You saw **him**." → **whom**
│       │   │   │   │   └── Example: "**Who/Whom** is calling?"
│       │   │   │   │       └── "**He** is calling." → **who**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples - Who (Subject):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Who** called?" (He called.)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "The person **who** won..." (She won.)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Who** do you think is right?" (He is right.)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "**Whoever** comes first wins." (He comes first.)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples - Whom (Object):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Whom** did you see?" (You saw him.)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "The person **whom** I met..." (I met her.)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "To **whom** are you speaking?" (Speaking to him.)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Whom** should I ask?" (Ask him.)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "**Whomever** you choose is fine." (You choose her.)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Tricky Cases:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Who** do you think called?" → "You think **he** called." (who)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**Whom** shall I say is calling?" → "**He** is calling." (who!)
│       │   │   │   │   │   └── ⚠ Trap! "is calling" needs subject, so **who**
│       │   │   │   │   └── "Give it to **whoever/whomever** needs it."
│       │   │   │   │       └── "**He** needs it." → **whoever** (subject of clause)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **Modern Usage Note:**
│       │   │   │       ├── "Whom" declining in informal speech
│       │   │   │       ├── "Who did you see?" now widely accepted
│       │   │   │       ├── **But:** Still required after prepositions in formal writing
│       │   │   │       └── "To **whom** it may concern" (never "To who")
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(v) Common Errors to Avoid**
│       │   │       ├── ✘ "Between you and **I**." → ✔ "Between you and **me**."
│       │   │       ├── ✘ "Give it to **she** and **I**." → ✔ "Give it to **her** and **me**."
│       │   │       ├── ✘ "For **we** students..." → ✔ "For **us** students..."
│       │   │       ├── ✘ "Let **he** and **I** go." → ✔ "Let **him** and **me** go."
│       │   │       └── **Test:** Remove the other person → "Give it to **me**" (not "to I")
│       │   │
│       │   └── **(e) Possessive (Genitive) Case - Detailed**
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(i) Definition & Core Concept**
│       │       │   ├── **Definition:** The case showing possession, origin, or close relationship
│       │       │   ├── **Unique Feature:** Only case visible on NOUNS in Modern English
│       │       │   ├── **Two Types:**
│       │       │   │   ├── **Determiner/Dependent:** Used before nouns (my, his, John's)
│       │       │   │   └── **Independent:** Stands alone (mine, his, John's)
│       │       │   └── **Formation:**
│       │       │       ├── **Pronouns:** Distinct forms (my/mine, her/hers, etc.)
│       │       │       └── **Nouns:** Add 's or ' (John's, students')
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(ii) Two Forms of Possessive Pronouns**
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **A. Dependent (Determiner) Possessives - Before Nouns**
│       │       │   │   ├── **Forms:** my, your, his, her, its, our, their, whose
│       │       │   │   ├── **Function:** Act as determiners modifying following noun
│       │       │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │       │   │   │   ├── "**My** book is here."
│       │       │   │   │   ├── "**Her** car is new."
│       │       │   │   │   ├── "**Their** house is beautiful."
│       │       │   │   │   └── "**Whose** bag is this?"
│       │       │   │   └── **Note:** Cannot stand alone: ✘ "This book is **my**."
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   └── **B. Independent Possessives - Stand Alone**
│       │       │       ├── **Forms:** mine, yours, his, hers, ours, theirs, whose
│       │       │       ├── **Function:** Replace entire noun phrase (possessive + noun)
│       │       │       ├── **Examples:**
│       │       │       │   ├── "This book is **mine**." (= my book)
│       │       │       │   ├── "**Yours** is better than **hers**."
│       │       │       │   ├── "The choice is **theirs** to make."
│       │       │       │   └── "A friend of **mine**..." (double genitive)
│       │       │       ├── **Note:** "His" and "whose" are same in both forms
│       │       │       └── **⚠ No apostrophe:** ✘ "your's" → ✔ "yours"
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(iii) Possessive with Nouns ('s / ')**
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **A. Singular Nouns → Add 's**
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **boy's** book" (one boy)
│       │       │   │   ├── "**John's** car"
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **dog's** tail"
│       │       │   │   └── "A **week's** notice"
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **B. Plural Nouns Ending in -s → Add ' only**
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **boys'** books" (multiple boys)
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **students'** essays"
│       │       │   │   ├── "My **parents'** house"
│       │       │   │   └── "The **ladies'** room"
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **C. Plural Nouns NOT Ending in -s → Add 's**
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **children's** toys"
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **men's** room"
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **women's** rights"
│       │       │   │   └── "The **people's** choice"
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **D. Names Ending in -s (Variable)**
│       │       │   │   ├── **Standard:** "**James's** book" / "**Charles's** reign"
│       │       │   │   ├── **Alternative:** "**James'** book" (acceptable)
│       │       │   │   ├── **Classical names:** "**Jesus'** teachings" / "**Socrates'** philosophy"
│       │       │   │   └── **Pronounced rule:** Add 's if you pronounce extra syllable
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   └── **E. Compound Nouns / Phrases → 's on final element**
│       │       │       ├── "My **mother-in-law's** advice"
│       │       │       ├── "The **Queen of England's** crown"
│       │       │       ├── "**Someone else's** problem"
│       │       │       └── "The **Attorney General's** office"
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(iv) Semantic Functions of Possessive (Types of Possession)**
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **A. Ownership/Possession Proper**
│       │       │   │   ├── "**John's** car" (John owns the car)
│       │       │   │   ├── "**Her** money"
│       │       │   │   └── "The **company's** assets"
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **B. Subjective Genitive (Possessor = Agent/Subject of implied action)**
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **boy's** arrival" (= The boy arrived)
│       │       │   │   ├── "**Shakespeare's** plays" (= Shakespeare wrote the plays)
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **government's** decision" (= The government decided)
│       │       │   │   └── "**Her** refusal" (= She refused)
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **C. Objective Genitive (Possessor = Object of implied action)**
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **city's** destruction" (= [Someone] destroyed the city)
│       │       │   │   ├── "**Caesar's** assassination" (= [Someone] assassinated Caesar)
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **prisoner's** release" (= [They] released the prisoner)
│       │       │   │   └── "**Her** admiration" (= [Someone] admires her) - ambiguous!
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **D. Descriptive Genitive (Classification/Type)**
│       │       │   │   ├── "A **summer's** day" (type of day)
│       │       │   │   ├── "A **bachelor's** degree"
│       │       │   │   ├── "**Women's** clothing"
│       │       │   │   └── "A **moment's** notice"
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **E. Partitive Genitive (Part-of relationship)**
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **car's** engine" (engine is part of car)
│       │       │   │   ├── "The **book's** cover"
│       │       │   │   └── "The **tree's** branches"
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **F. Measure/Extent Genitive**
│       │       │   │   ├── "A **day's** work"
│       │       │   │   ├── "Two **weeks'** vacation"
│       │       │   │   ├── "Five **minutes'** walk"
│       │       │   │   └── "A **dollar's** worth"
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   └── **G. Double Genitive (Of + Possessive)**
│       │       │       ├── "A friend **of mine**" (= one of my friends)
│       │       │       ├── "That idea **of hers**"
│       │       │       ├── "A book **of John's**"
│       │       │       ├── **Meaning difference:**
│       │       │       │   ├── "A picture of the queen" (shows the queen)
│       │       │       │   └── "A picture of the queen's" (belongs to the queen)
│       │       │       └── **Note:** Combines "of" phrase with possessive
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(v) 's vs. Of-Phrase (When to use which)**
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **'s Preferred For:**
│       │       │   │   ├── **People/Animals:** "John's book" / "the dog's tail"
│       │       │   │   ├── **Time expressions:** "today's news" / "a week's rest"
│       │       │   │   ├── **Personified entities:** "Fortune's wheel" / "Nature's beauty"
│       │       │   │   ├── **Geographical names:** "London's streets" / "Japan's economy"
│       │       │   │   └── **Organizations:** "the company's policy" / "the government's role"
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   ├── **Of-Phrase Preferred For:**
│       │       │   │   ├── **Inanimate objects:** "the leg of the table" (not: the table's leg)
│       │       │   │   ├── **Long/complex possessors:** "the efforts of all the students"
│       │       │   │   ├── **Abstract nouns:** "the power of love" / "the height of folly"
│       │       │   │   └── **After "of":** "a friend of John" (then + 's = "a friend of John's")
│       │       │   │
│       │       │   └── **Both Acceptable (Variation):**
│       │       │       ├── "The earth's surface" / "the surface of the earth"
│       │       │       ├── "The plan's success" / "the success of the plan"
│       │       │       └── **General trend:** 's gaining ground even for inanimates
│       │       │
│       │       └── **(vi) Common Errors with Possessives**
│       │           ├── ✘ "**it's**" for possession → ✔ "**its**" (it's = it is)
│       │           ├── ✘ "**your's**" / "**their's**" → ✔ "**yours**" / "**theirs**"
│       │           ├── ✘ "**who's** book" → ✔ "**whose** book" (who's = who is)
│       │           ├── ✘ "The **boy's** are here." → ✔ "The **boys** are here."
│       │           ├── ✘ "**Mens'** room" → ✔ "**Men's** room"
│       │           └── **Apostrophe Rule:** Possessives NEVER use apostrophe + s on pronouns
│       │
│       ├── **2. Case Assigners/Assignment (How case is assigned in syntax)**
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(a) Overview & Theoretical Background**
│       │   │   ├── **Definition:** Case assignment is the syntactic process by which words receive their morphological form
│       │   │   ├── **Case Assigner:** A syntactic head that assigns case to a noun/pronoun in its domain
│       │   │   ├── **Core Principle:** Every overt NP must be assigned (abstract) case
│       │   │   │   └── This is called the "Case Filter" in generative grammar
│       │   │   ├── **English Case Assigners:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Finite Tense (T/INFL)** → Assigns Subjective (Nominative) to subject
│       │   │   │   ├── **Verbs** → Assign Objective (Accusative) to objects
│       │   │   │   └── **Prepositions** → Assign Objective (Accusative) to their objects
│       │   │   └── **Two Types of Case:**
│       │   │       ├── **Structural Case:** Assigned based on position (subject, object)
│       │   │       │   └── Subjective and Objective are structural
│       │   │       └── **Inherent Case:** Assigned based on theta-role/meaning
│       │   │           └── Genitive (Possessive) is considered inherent
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(b) Finite Tense (T) → Subjective Case**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) The Rule**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Finite T assigns Nominative (Subjective) case to its Specifier (Subject)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Only finite clauses have nominative subjects**
│       │   │   │   └── **Non-finite clauses lack nominative case assignment**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) What Makes T "Finite"**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Tense marking:** Present or Past tense on the verb
│       │   │   │   ├── **Agreement:** Subject-verb agreement features (I am, he is, they are)
│       │   │   │   └── **Finite verbs:** "is, was, runs, ran, have, had"
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) Examples - Finite Clauses (Subjective Case)**
│       │   │   │   ├── "**He** runs every day." (He = subject of finite 'runs')
│       │   │   │   ├── "**She** is happy." (She = subject of finite 'is')
│       │   │   │   ├── "**They** have arrived." (They = subject of finite 'have')
│       │   │   │   ├── "**I** believe **he** is right." (Both I and he = subjects of their finite verbs)
│       │   │   │   ├── "The man **who** lives here..." (who = subject of finite 'lives')
│       │   │   │   └── "It is **I** who am responsible." (I = subject complement, formal)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iv) Examples - Non-Finite Clauses (No Nominative)**
│       │   │   │   ├── "I want **him** to leave." (infinitive - him is objective, not nominative)
│       │   │   │   ├── "**His** leaving surprised us." (gerund - no nominative subject)
│       │   │   │   ├── "For **him** to win would be amazing." (infinitive with 'for' - objective)
│       │   │   │   └── **Note:** Non-finite T cannot assign nominative case
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(v) Subject-Verb Agreement as Case Marker**
│       │   │   │   ├── Agreement features signal the nominative relationship
│       │   │   │   ├── "**I am** / **He is** / **They are**" (agreement with nominative subject)
│       │   │   │   ├── "**She runs** / **They run**" (3rd person singular -s)
│       │   │   │   └── No such agreement in non-finite clauses: "I want him to run" (no 'runs')
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(vi) Special Contexts for Nominative**
│       │   │       ├── **Predicate Nominatives (Formal):**
│       │   │       │   ├── "It is **I**." (linking verb 'be' - subject complement)
│       │   │       │   ├── "The winner was **she**." (formal)
│       │   │       │   └── *Rule: Subject complement takes same case as subject*
│       │   │       ├── **Comparative Clauses (Formal):**
│       │   │       │   ├── "She is taller than **I** [am]." (I = subject of implied verb)
│       │   │       │   └── "He runs as fast as **she** [does]."
│       │   │       └── **Inverted Structures:**
│       │   │           ├── "Never have **I** seen such beauty." (I still nominative)
│       │   │           └── "Rarely does **she** complain."
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(c) Verbs → Objective Case**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) The Rule**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Verbs assign Accusative (Objective) case to their complements**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Applies to:** Direct objects, some indirect objects
│       │   │   │   └── **Both finite and non-finite verbs assign objective case**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Types of Verbal Case Assignment**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **A. Transitive Verbs → Direct Object**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "I saw **him**." (him = direct object of 'saw')
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She loves **me**." (me = direct object of 'loves')
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "They invited **us**." (us = direct object of 'invited')
│       │   │   │   │   └── "**Whom** did you see?" (whom = object of 'see', formal)
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **B. Ditransitive Verbs → Indirect + Direct Object**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Give **me** the book." (me = indirect object)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She told **him** a story." (him = indirect object)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "They sent **her** a letter." (her = indirect object)
│       │   │   │   │   └── **Both** objects receive objective case: "Give **it** to **me**."
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **C. Infinitive Verbs (Non-finite)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "To see **him** would be great." (him = object of infinitive)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "I want to meet **her**." (her = object of infinitive 'meet')
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "To help **them** is my goal."
│       │   │   │   │   └── **Note:** Infinitives assign objective case like finite verbs
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **D. Gerunds (-ing verbals)**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Seeing **her** made me happy." (her = object of gerund)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "I enjoy helping **them**." (them = object of gerund)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "Without meeting **him**, I can't decide."
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   └── **E. Participles**
│       │   │   │       ├── "Having seen **him**, I left." (him = object of participle)
│       │   │   │       ├── "The teacher, helping **us**, smiled."
│       │   │   │       └── "While reading **it**, I noticed..."
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) ECM: Exceptional Case Marking**
│       │   │   │   │
│       │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** When a verb assigns objective case to the subject of an embedded infinitive clause
│       │   │   │   ├── **Pattern:** [Main V] + [NP-objective] + [to VP]
│       │   │   │   ├── **The NP is semantically the subject of the infinitive, but case-marked by main verb**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "I believe **him** to be honest." (him = subject of 'to be', but objective)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She considers **them** to be qualified."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "We expect **her** to win."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "They proved **it** to be false."
│       │   │   │   │   └── "I know **him** to be a liar."
│       │   │   │   ├── **ECM Verbs (Common):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── **Belief/Mental:** believe, consider, think, know, find, imagine
│       │   │   │   │   ├── **Perception:** see, hear, watch, feel, notice
│       │   │   │   │   ├── **Expectation:** expect, want, need, require, prefer
│       │   │   │   │   ├── **Reporting:** report, declare, prove, show
│       │   │   │   │   └── **Causative:** make, let, have, help (some take bare infinitives)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Contrast with Control Verbs:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ECM: "I believe **him** to be smart." (him = overt subject)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Control: "I persuaded **him** to leave." (him = object, PRO subject)
│       │   │   │   └── **Test:** Replace with finite clause:
│       │   │   │       ├── "I believe him to be smart." ≈ "I believe that **he** is smart."
│       │   │   │       └── Nominative in finite = ECM subject in infinitive
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iv) Bare Infinitives (Causative/Perception Verbs)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Pattern:** [V] + [NP-objective] + [bare infinitive (no 'to')]
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Let **him** go." (him = objective, subject of 'go')
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Make **her** understand." (her = objective)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "I saw **them** leave." (them = objective)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "We heard **him** sing." (him = objective)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "Help **me** carry this." (me = objective)
│       │   │   │   └── **Verbs taking bare infinitives:**
│       │   │   │       ├── **Causative:** make, let, have (sometimes)
│       │   │   │       ├── **Perception:** see, hear, watch, feel, notice
│       │   │   │       └── **Others:** help (optional 'to')
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(v) Object Complements**
│       │   │       ├── **Definition:** Element describing the object, not receiving separate case
│       │   │       ├── **Pattern:** [V] + [Object-objective] + [Complement]
│       │   │       ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │       │   ├── "They elected **him** president." (him = objective object)
│       │   │       │   ├── "I consider **her** intelligent." (her = objective)
│       │   │       │   ├── "We painted **it** blue." (it = objective)
│       │   │       │   └── "She made **me** happy." (me = objective)
│       │   │       └── **Complement:** Does not receive case (adjective/noun describing object)
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(d) Prepositions → Objective Case**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) The Rule**
│       │   │   │   ├── **ALL prepositions assign Objective (Accusative) case to their objects**
│       │   │   │   ├── **NO EXCEPTIONS in Modern English**
│       │   │   │   └── **This is the most common source of case errors (hypercorrection)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Simple Prepositions**
│       │   │   │   ├── "Give it to **me**." (NOT: to I)
│       │   │   │   ├── "Come with **us**." (NOT: with we)
│       │   │   │   ├── "She sat beside **him**."
│       │   │   │   ├── "They voted for **her**."
│       │   │   │   ├── "I spoke about **them**."
│       │   │   │   ├── "The gift is from **me**."
│       │   │   │   └── "To **whom** it may concern." (formal)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) Compound/Coordinated Objects (CRITICAL ERROR ZONE)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Rule:** ALL parts of compound object take objective case
│       │   │   │   ├── **Correct:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Between you and **me**..." (both objective)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "For **him** and **her**."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Give it to **her** and **me**."
│       │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "The secret is between **us**."
│       │   │   │   ├── **Incorrect (Hypercorrection):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Between you and **I**." (I should be me)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✘ "For **he** and **she**." (should be him and her)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Give it to **she** and **I**." (should be her and me)
│       │   │   │   │   └── ✘ "Just between **we** friends." (should be us)
│       │   │   │   └── **Test:** Remove the other person - would you say "for I"? No. → "for me"
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iv) Phrasal/Complex Prepositions**
│       │   │   │   ├── "According to **him**..." (objective)
│       │   │   │   ├── "In front of **me**..." (objective)
│       │   │   │   ├── "Because of **them**..." (objective)
│       │   │   │   ├── "On behalf of **her**..." (objective)
│       │   │   │   ├── "As for **us**..." (objective)
│       │   │   │   └── "Except for **him**..." (objective)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(v) Preposition-Like Words**
│       │   │   │   ├── **"But" (meaning "except"):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Everyone but **me** was invited." (objective)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "No one but **him** knew." (objective)
│       │   │   │   ├── **"Except":**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Everyone except **her** attended." (objective)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "All except **us** have left."
│       │   │   │   └── **"Like" (as preposition):**
│       │   │   │       ├── "Someone like **me**..." (objective)
