│       │   │   │       └── "People like **us**..."
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(vi) Stranded Prepositions**
│       │   │       ├── **Definition:** Preposition separated from its object (at end of clause)
│       │   │       ├── **Still assigns objective case:**
│       │   │       │   ├── "**Whom** are you talking to?" (whom = object of 'to')
│       │   │       │   ├── "**Who** is this for?" (informal - who for whom)
│       │   │       │   ├── "That's the person I was talking about." (whom implied)
│       │   │       └── **Formal vs. Informal:**
│       │   │           ├── Formal: "To **whom** are you speaking?"
│       │   │           └── Informal: "**Who** are you speaking to?"
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(e) Possessive Case Assignment**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) Not Assigned by T, V, or P**
│       │   │   │   ├── Possessive (Genitive) is NOT structural case
│       │   │   │   ├── It is "inherent" case, tied to semantic role
│       │   │   │   └── Assigned in NP structure, not by external heads
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Determiner Position**
│       │   │   │   ├── Possessive pronouns (my, his, her...) are determiners
│       │   │   │   ├── They occupy the specifier of NP/DP
│       │   │   │   └── "**My** book" = [DP my [NP book]]
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) Genitive 's Construction**
│       │   │   │   ├── "'s" attaches to the possessor phrase
│       │   │   │   ├── "John's book" = [DP [John's] [NP book]]
│       │   │   │   └── **Clitic nature:** Attaches to end of phrase:
│       │   │   │       └── "The Queen of England**'s** crown" (not: Queen's)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(iv) Gerund Subjects (Formal)**
│       │   │       ├── **Formal:** Possessive before gerund
│       │   │       │   ├── "**His** leaving surprised us."
│       │   │       │   └── "I appreciate **your** helping."
│       │   │       └── **Informal:** Objective also used
│       │   │           ├── "**Him** leaving surprised us."
│       │   │           └── "I appreciate **you** helping."
│       │   │
│       │   └── **(f) Default Case**
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(i) Definition**
│       │       │   ├── **Default case:** The form used when no case assigner is present
│       │       │   └── **In English:** Objective is the default case
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(ii) Evidence for Objective as Default**
│       │       │   ├── **Isolation/Exclamation:** "**Me**? Never!" (not: I? Never!)
│       │       │   ├── "**Him** again!" / "Poor **me**!" / "Lucky **her**!"
│       │       │   ├── **One-word answers:** "Who's there?" → "**Me**." (not: I)
│       │       │   ├── **Tag questions:** "It was fun, wasn't it?" → "Yes, for **me**."
│       │       │   └── **Coordination without verb:** "**Him** and **me**, we're leaving."
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(iii) After "Be" (Informal Default)**
│       │       │   ├── "It's **me**." (default objective in informal)
│       │       │   ├── "That's **him**." (widely accepted)
│       │       │   ├── **Formal:** "It is **I**." (nominative, prescriptive)
│       │       │   └── **Modern usage:** Objective after 'be' is standard in speech
│       │       │
│       │       └── **(iv) Exam Implication**
│       │           ├── Formal exams expect nominative in prescriptive contexts
│       │           ├── "It is **I**." = correct for formal tests
│       │           └── But, "It's **me**." = correct for descriptive/spoken English
│       │
│       ├── **3. Special Case Constructions (Non-standard case usage)**
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(a) Expletive/Dummy Constructions**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) It-Cleft Constructions**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** It + be + focused NP + relative clause
│       │   │   │   ├── **Case Rule:** Focused NP takes nominative if it's the semantic subject
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples (Formal - Nominative):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "It was **he** who won the prize." (he = subject of 'won')
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "It is **she** who deserves credit." (she = subject of 'deserves')
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "It was **I** who called." (I = subject of 'called')
│       │   │   │   │   └── "It is **they** who should apologize."
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples (Informal - Objective accepted):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "It was **him** who won." (widely used in speech)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "It's **me** who should go." (common informal)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "It was **her** that called."
│       │   │   │   ├── **With Objective Focus (Object of relative clause):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "It was **him** that I saw." (him = object of 'saw')
│       │   │   │   │   └── "It was **her** whom they chose." (her = object of 'chose')
│       │   │   │   └── **Exam Note:** Formal tests expect nominative for subject focus
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Dummy/Expletive "It" (Weather, Time, Distance)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** "It" with no referent; placeholder subject
│       │   │   │   ├── **No case assignment to logical subject** (none exists)
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**It** rains." (no semantic subject)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**It** is snowing."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**It** is 5 o'clock." (time)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**It** is 10 miles to town." (distance)
│       │   │   │   │   └── "**It** seems that he left." (anticipatory - clause is subject)
│       │   │   │   └── **Note:** "It" is grammatically nominative but semantically empty
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) Extraposition (Anticipatory "It")**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** "It" replaces a clausal subject moved to end
│       │   │   │   ├── **Pattern:** It + be + Adj/NP + that-clause/to-infinitive
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**It** is obvious [that he lied]." (Real subject: that-clause)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**It** is important [to study]." (Real subject: infinitive)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "**It** surprised me [that she left]."
│       │   │   │   │   └── "**It** was wrong [for him to leave]."
│       │   │   │   └── **Case:** "It" is nominative; real subject has no morphological case
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(iv) Existential "There" Construction**
│       │   │       ├── **Structure:** There + be + NP (+ location/time)
│       │   │       ├── **Case Rule:** Post-verbal NP is the logical subject (nominative)
│       │   │       ├── **Examples:**
│       │   │       │   ├── "**There** is a book on the table." (a book = logical subject)
│       │   │       │   ├── "**There** are many students here."
│       │   │       │   ├── "**There** seems to be a problem."
│       │   │       │   └── "**There** came a knock at the door." (literary)
│       │   │       ├── **Agreement:** Verb agrees with logical subject (not "there")
│       │   │       │   ├── ✔ "There **is** a cat." (singular)
│       │   │       │   ├── ✔ "There **are** cats." (plural)
│       │   │       │   └── ✘ "There **is** many problems." (should be 'are')
│       │   │       └── **Note:** "There" is a dummy subject, not locative
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(b) Comparative Structures (Than/As Clauses)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) The Debate: Nominative vs. Objective**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Two analyses of "than/as":**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── **As conjunction:** Introduces a clause → pronoun takes case based on function
│       │   │   │   │   └── **As preposition:** Takes an object → pronoun is objective
│       │   │   │   └── **Result:** Both analyses exist, leading to variation
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Formal Usage (Conjunction Analysis)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Rule:** Pronoun takes the case it would have in the full clause
│       │   │   │   ├── **Subject position → Nominative:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "He is taller than **I** [am tall]." (I = subject of 'am')
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She runs as fast as **he** [runs]." (he = subject of 'runs')
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "No one works harder than **she** [does]."
│       │   │   │   │   └── "You are as smart as **he** [is]."
│       │   │   │   └── **Object position → Objective:**
│       │   │   │       ├── "She likes him more than [she likes] **me**." (me = object)
│       │   │   │       └── "They helped her as much as [they helped] **him**."
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) Informal Usage (Preposition Analysis)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Rule:** "Than/as" treated as prepositions → always objective
│       │   │   │   ├── **Examples (Widely Accepted in Speech):**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "He is taller than **me**."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She runs as fast as **him**."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "No one is smarter than **her**."
│       │   │   │   │   └── "You're older than **us**."
│       │   │   │   └── **Note:** This is standard in informal English
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iv) Ambiguity (Why Case Matters)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Case can change meaning:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She likes him more than **I** [like him]." (I = subject)
│       │   │   │   │   │   └── Meaning: She likes him more than I like him.
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "She likes him more than [she likes] **me**." (me = object)
│       │   │   │   │   │   └── Meaning: She likes him more than she likes me.
│       │   │   │   │   └── **Different cases = Different meanings!**
│       │   │   │   └── **Avoiding ambiguity:** Complete the clause when meaning is unclear
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(v) Exam Guidance**
│       │   │       ├── **Formal tests:** Expect nominative when pronoun is implied subject
│       │   │       ├── ✔ "He is taller than **I**." (SAT, GRE style)
│       │   │       ├── ✘ "He is taller than **me**." (marked wrong on formal tests)
│       │   │       └── **Strategy:** Mentally complete the clause to determine case
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(c) Coordination (Case in Conjoined Elements)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) The Rule**
│       │   │   │   ├── **All conjoined pronouns must have the SAME case**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Case determined by function of entire coordinate structure**
│       │   │   │   └── **Test:** Remove one conjunct - remaining pronoun reveals correct case
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Subject Position (Nominative)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Correct:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**He** and **I** went to the store." (Both nominative)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**She** and **they** are coming." (Both nominative)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Neither **he** nor **she** was invited."
│       │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "Both **you** and **I** know the truth."
│       │   │   │   └── **Incorrect:**
│       │   │   │       ├── ✘ "**Him** and **me** went to the store." → He and I
│       │   │   │       ├── ✘ "**Her** and **me** are friends." → She and I
│       │   │   │       └── ✘ "**Me** and him did it." → He and I
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) Object Position (Objective)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **After Verbs:**
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "They invited **him** and **me**." (Both objective)
│       │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She saw **her** and **them**."
│       │   │   │   │   └── ✘ "They invited **he** and **I**." → him and me
│       │   │   │   └── **After Prepositions (CRITICAL):**
│       │   │   │       ├── ✔ "Between **you** and **me**..." (Both objective)
│       │   │   │       ├── ✔ "For **him** and **her**..."
│       │   │   │       ├── ✔ "Give it to **her** and **me**."
│       │   │   │       ├── ✘ "Between **you** and **I**." → me (MOST COMMON ERROR)
│       │   │   │       └── ✘ "Give it to **she** and **I**." → her and me
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iv) The "Remove One Conjunct" Test**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Method:** Remove one pronoun, check if sentence is grammatical
│       │   │   │   ├── **Example:** "Give it to she and I."
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Give it to **she**." → ✘ Wrong (should be 'her')
│       │   │   │   │   ├── "Give it to **I**." → ✘ Wrong (should be 'me')
│       │   │   │   │   └── ✔ Correct: "Give it to **her** and **me**."
│       │   │   │   └── **Example:** "Him and me went."
│       │   │   │       ├── "**Him** went." → ✘ Wrong (should be 'He')
│       │   │   │       └── ✔ Correct: "**He** and **I** went."
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(v) Order of Pronouns (Politeness)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Convention:** Put "I/me" last
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "He and **I**" / "Him and **me**" (polite)
│       │   │   │   ├── ⚠ "**I** and he" / "**Me** and him" (less polite, but grammatical)
│       │   │   │   └── **Note:** Order doesn't affect case; case affects correctness
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(vi) Common Hypercorrection**
│       │   │       ├── **Hypercorrection:** Overusing nominative to sound "correct"
│       │   │       ├── **Why it happens:** People learn "me is wrong" → overcorrect to "I"
│       │   │       ├── **Common errors:**
│       │   │       │   ├── ✘ "Between you and **I**" (I sounds formal but is wrong)
│       │   │       │   ├── ✘ "They gave it to **we** students." (should be 'us')
│       │   │       │   └── ✘ "For **she** and **I**" (should be 'her and me')
│       │   │       └── **Rule:** Position determines case, not formality
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(d) Appositives (Case Agreement)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) The Rule**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Appositive takes same case as the noun it renames**
│       │   │   │   └── **Both elements have the same grammatical function**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Subject Appositive (Nominative)**
│       │   │   │   ├── "**We** students need more time." (we = subject, students = appositive)
│       │   │   │   ├── "The winners, **he** and **I**, were announced."
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "**Us** students need more time." → We students
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) Object Appositive (Objective)**
│       │   │   │   ├── "They chose **us** students for the project." (us = object)
│       │   │   │   ├── "The award was given to **us** finalists."
│       │   │   │   ├── "She invited **him** and **me**, the new members."
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "They chose **we** students." → us students
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(iv) Test Method**
│       │   │       ├── **Remove the appositive noun, check pronoun case:**
│       │   │       ├── "**We/Us** students need time." → "**We** need time." → We students
│       │   │       └── "For **we/us** students..." → "For **us**..." → us students
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(e) Reflexive Pronouns (Special Case Usage)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) Standard Use (Object = Subject)**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Reflexive:** Object refers back to subject of same clause
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I hurt **myself**." (I = subject, myself = object referring to I)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She taught **herself** to code."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "They blamed **themselves**."
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "**Myself** went to the store." (reflexive cannot be subject)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Emphatic Use (Intensifier)**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I **myself** saw it." (emphasizes 'I')
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "The president **himself** attended."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She did it **herself**." (without help)
│       │   │   │   └── **Note:** Can be removed without changing grammaticality
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) Common Errors with Reflexives**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Give it to John and **myself**." → me (no reflexive antecedent)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "**Myself** and John will go." → I (reflexive cannot be subject)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Between **yourself** and I..." → you and me
│       │   │   │   └── **Rule:** Use reflexive ONLY if it refers back to subject in same clause
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(iv) Why These Errors Occur**
│       │   │       ├── **Hypercorrection:** "Myself" sounds formal/polite
│       │   │       ├── **Avoidance:** Unsure of me/I, so use "myself"
│       │   │       └── **Reality:** "Myself" without antecedent is non-standard
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(f) Elliptical Constructions**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(i) Definition**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Ellipsis:** Omission of words recoverable from context
│       │   │   │   └── **Case is determined by the IMPLIED full structure**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(ii) Comparative Ellipsis**
│       │   │   │   ├── "She is smarter than **I** [am]." (I = subject of implied 'am')
│       │   │   │   ├── "He runs faster than **she** [runs]."
│       │   │   │   └── **Formal:** Complete mentally to determine case
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **(iii) "As well as" / "Not only...but also"**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She as well as **I** knows the truth." (I = subject)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Not only he but also **she** was invited."
│       │   │   │   └── ✔ "They invited her as well as **me**." (me = object)
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   └── **(iv) Short Answers**
│       │   │       ├── **Q:** "Who did it?" **A:** "**I** [did it]." (formal, subject)
│       │   │       ├── **Q:** "Who did it?" **A:** "**Me**." (informal, default case)
│       │   │       ├── **Q:** "Who's there?" **A:** "**Me**." / "It's **me**." (informal)
│       │   │       └── **Exam note:** Formal expects nominative; spoken allows objective
│       │   │
│       │   └── **(g) Predicate Nominatives (After Linking Verbs)**
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(i) The Formal Rule**
│       │       │   ├── **After "be" verbs, subject complement = same case as subject**
│       │       │   ├── **Since subject is nominative, complement should be nominative**
│       │       │   └── **This applies to: be, become, remain, seem, appear (linking verbs)**
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(ii) Formal Usage (Nominative)**
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "It is **I**." (not: It is me)
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "This is **she** speaking." (telephone)
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "The winner was **he**."
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "If I were **he**, I would leave."
│       │       │   └── ✔ "It was **they** who complained."
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **(iii) Informal Usage (Objective)**
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "It's **me**." (universally accepted in speech)
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "This is **her**." (common)
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "That's **him** over there."
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "It was **us** who won."
│       │       │   └── **Note:** Objective after "be" is now standard in spoken English
│       │       │
│       │       └── **(iv) Exam Strategy**
│       │           ├── **Formal tests (SAT, GRE):** Expect nominative
│       │           ├── **Descriptive/Spoken English:** Objective is correct
│       │           └── **Rule of thumb:** On standardized tests, use formal (nominative)
│       │
│       ├── **4. Common Exam Traps (Frequent errors in tests)**
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(a) Preposition + Pronoun (ALWAYS Objective)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **The Rule:** Every preposition takes objective case
│       │   │   ├── **Correct Examples:**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "For you and **me**."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Between **him** and **her**."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Give it to **us**."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "To **whom** it may concern."
│       │   │   │   └── ✔ "According to **them**..."
│       │   │   ├── **Incorrect (Hypercorrection):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "For you and **I**." → For you and **me**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Between you and **I**." → Between you and **me**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Give it to **she** and **I**." → to **her** and **me**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "For **we** students." → For **us** students
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "To **who** it may concern." → To **whom**
│       │   │   ├── **Why This Error Occurs:**
│       │   │   │   ├── Speakers learn "me is wrong" in subject position
│       │   │   │   ├── They overcorrect to "I" everywhere
│       │   │   │   └── "Between you and I" sounds formal but is WRONG
│       │   │   └── **Quick Test:** Remove the other pronoun
│       │   │       ├── "For you and I" → "For **I**" → ✘ Wrong! → "For **me**"
│       │   │       └── "Between you and me" → "Between **me**" → ✔ Correct
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(b) Coordination Errors (Case Mismatch in Conjoined Pronouns)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **The Rule:** All conjoined pronouns must share the SAME case
│       │   │   ├── **Subject Position (Nominative):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**She** and **I** are friends."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**He** and **they** left early."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "**Her** and **me** are friends." → She and I
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "**Him** and **her** went shopping." → He and she
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "**Me** and him did it." → He and I
│       │   │   ├── **Object Position (Objective):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "They invited **him** and **me**."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She saw **her** and **them**."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "They invited **he** and **I**." → him and me
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "Give it to **she** and **I**." → her and me
│       │   │   └── **Quick Test:** Remove one conjunct
│       │   │       ├── "**Him** and me went." → "**Him** went." → ✘ → "**He** went."
│       │   │       └── Correct: "**He** and **I** went."
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(c) Verb Complement Errors (Objective After Transitives)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **The Rule:** Verbs assign objective case to their objects
│       │   │   ├── **Correct Examples:**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Let **him** go."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Make **her** understand."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I want **them** to leave."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "We believe **him** to be honest."
│       │   │   │   └── ✔ "She asked **me** to help."
│       │   │   ├── **Incorrect:**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Let **he** go." → Let **him** go
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Make **she** understand." → Make **her** understand
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "I want **they** to leave." → I want **them**
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "We believe **he** to be honest." → We believe **him**
│       │   │   └── **ECM Note:** In "I believe him to be smart," him is subject of infinitive but takes objective case (assigned by "believe")
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(d) Comparative Clause Errors (Than/As + Pronoun)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Formal Rule:** Complete the implied clause mentally
│       │   │   ├── **Subject of Implied Verb (Nominative):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She is taller than **I** [am]."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "He runs as fast as **she** [runs]."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "No one works harder than **he** [works]."
│       │   │   │   └── ⚠ "...taller than **me**" = acceptable informal but wrong on formal tests
│       │   │   ├── **Object of Implied Verb (Objective):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She likes him more than [she likes] **me**."
│       │   │   │   └── ✔ "They helped her as much as [they helped] **him**."
│       │   │   ├── **Ambiguity Alert:**
│       │   │   │   ├── "She likes him more than **I**." = I like him less
│       │   │   │   └── "She likes him more than **me**." = She likes me less
│       │   │   └── **Exam Strategy:** Complete the clause → determine pronoun's function → choose case
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(e) Who vs. Whom Confusion**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **The Rule:**
│       │   │   │   ├── **Who** = Subject (like he/she/they)
│       │   │   │   └── **Whom** = Object (like him/her/them)
│       │   │   ├── **Correct - Who (Subject):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**Who** called?" (He called.)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "The person **who** won..." (She won.)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**Who** do you think is right?" (He is right.)
│       │   │   │   └── ✔ "**Whoever** wants it can have it."
│       │   │   ├── **Correct - Whom (Object):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**Whom** did you see?" (You saw him.)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "To **whom** are you speaking?" (Speaking to him.)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "The person **whom** I met..." (I met her.)
│       │   │   │   └── ✔ "**Whomever** you choose is fine." (You choose her.)
│       │   │   ├── **Tricky Traps:**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "**Whom** shall I say is calling?" → **Who** (He is calling)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Trap: "is calling" needs a subject → who
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Give it to **whomever** needs it." → **whoever** (He needs it)
│       │   │   │   │   └── Trap: "needs" requires a subject → whoever
│       │   │   │   └── ✔ "Give it to **whomever** you choose." (You choose him)
│       │   │   └── **Quick Test:** Substitute he/him in a rephrased statement
│       │   │       ├── **He** fits → **who**
│       │   │       └── **Him** fits → **whom**
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(f) Reflexive Pronoun Misuse**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **The Rule:** Use reflexive ONLY when it refers back to subject in same clause
│       │   │   ├── **Correct (Reflexive refers to subject):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I hurt **myself**." (myself → I)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She taught **herself**." (herself → she)
│       │   │   │   └── ✔ "They blamed **themselves**." (themselves → they)
│       │   │   ├── **Incorrect (No reflexive antecedent in clause):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Give it to John and **myself**." → me (no antecedent for 'myself')
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "**Myself** and John will go." → I (reflexive can't be subject)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Between **yourself** and I..." → you and me
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "Please contact **myself** if..." → me
│       │   │   └── **Why Error Occurs:** "Myself" sounds polite/formal; used to avoid me/I uncertainty
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(g) Possessive Pronoun Confusion (Its vs. It's, Whose vs. Who's)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **The Rule:** Possessive pronouns NEVER use apostrophes
│       │   │   ├── **Its vs. It's:**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "The dog wagged **its** tail." (possessive)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**It's** raining." (it is)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "The dog wagged **it's** tail." → its
│       │   │   │   └── **Test:** Expand to "it is" - if it works, use it's; if not, use its
│       │   │   ├── **Whose vs. Who's:**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**Whose** book is this?" (possessive)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**Who's** coming?" (who is)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "**Who's** book is this?" → Whose
│       │   │   │   └── **Test:** Expand to "who is" - if it works, use who's
│       │   │   ├── **Your vs. You're:**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Is this **your** car?" (possessive)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**You're** welcome." (you are)
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "**Your** welcome." → You're
│       │   │   └── **Their vs. They're vs. There:**
│       │   │       ├── ✔ "**Their** house is big." (possessive)
│       │   │       ├── ✔ "**They're** leaving." (they are)
│       │   │       └── ✔ "Put it **there**." (location)
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(h) Appositive Case Errors**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **The Rule:** Appositive takes same case as the noun it renames
│       │   │   ├── **Subject Appositive (Nominative):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "**We** students need more time."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "The winners, **he** and **I**, celebrated."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✘ "**Us** students need more time." → We students
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "**Them** teachers are strict." → Those/The teachers
│       │   │   ├── **Object Appositive (Objective):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "They chose **us** students."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She invited **him** and **me**, the newcomers."
│       │   │   │   └── ✘ "They chose **we** students." → us students
│       │   │   └── **Quick Test:** Remove the noun, check pronoun alone
│       │   │       ├── "**We/Us** students need..." → "**We** need..." → We students
│       │   │       └── "For **we/us** students..." → "For **us**..." → us students
│       │   │
│       │   ├── **(i) Predicate Nominative Errors (After Linking Verbs)**
│       │   │   │
│       │   │   ├── **Formal Rule:** Complement of "be" = same case as subject (nominative)
│       │   │   ├── **Formal (Expected on Tests):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "It is **I**."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "This is **she** speaking."
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "The winner was **he**."
│       │   │   │   └── ✔ "If I were **he**, I would leave."
│       │   │   ├── **Informal (Acceptable in Speech):**
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "It's **me**." (universally accepted spoken)
│       │   │   │   ├── ✔ "That's **him**."
│       │   │   │   └── ✔ "It was **us** who complained."
│       │   │   └── **Exam Strategy:**
│       │   │       ├── Formal tests (SAT, GRE): Use nominative
│       │   │       ├── Descriptive/spoken: Objective is fine
│       │   │       └── When in doubt on standardized tests: Choose nominative
│       │   │
│       │   └── **(j) Embedded Question Word Order**
│       │       │
│       │       ├── **The Rule:** Embedded questions use STATEMENT word order (S + V)
│       │       ├── **Correct (Statement Order):**
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "I wonder what **he wants**."
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "Tell me where **she lives**."
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "I don't know who **he is**."
│       │       │   ├── ✔ "Ask him if **he can come**."
│       │       │   └── ✔ "Can you tell me what time **it is**?"
│       │       ├── **Incorrect (Inverted/Question Order):**
│       │       │   ├── ✘ "I wonder what **does he want**." → what he wants
│       │       │   ├── ✘ "Tell me where **does she live**." → where she lives
│       │       │   ├── ✘ "I don't know who **is he**." → who he is
│       │       │   ├── ✘ "Ask him **can he** come." → if he can come
│       │       │   └── ✘ "Can you tell me what time **is it**?" → it is
│       │       ├── **Why Error Occurs:**
│       │       │   ├── Direct questions use inversion: "What does he want?"
│       │       │   ├── Speakers transfer this to embedded questions
│       │       │   └── But embedded questions are STATEMENTS about questions
│       │       └── **Quick Fix:** If the clause is inside another sentence, use S + V order
│       │
│       └── **5. Competitive Exam Summary (Key points for tests like SAT, GRE, ESL)**
│           │
│           ├── **a. Government = control (Head controls dependent's form/case)**
│           ├── **b. Case = form (Morphological shape indicating role)**
│           ├── **c. Verbs & Prepositions govern objective case (Him not he after V/P)**
│           ├── **d. Subject position → subjective case (He runs, not him runs)**
│           └── **e. Preposition + "I" = almost always wrong (Use me; e.g., to I → to me)**
│    
└── **V. Subordination & Coordination**
    │
    ├── **A. Coordination (Joining equal grammatical units: clauses, phrases, words)**
    │   │
    │   ├── **1. Definition & Core Concept**
    │   │   ├── **Definition:** Coordination links two or more grammatically equivalent elements
    │   │   │   └── Elements must be of the SAME syntactic category (clause + clause, NP + NP, etc.)
    │   │   ├── **Key Properties:**
    │   │   │   ├── **Symmetry:** Coordinated elements are structurally equal (no embedding)
    │   │   │   ├── **Independence:** Each conjunct can stand alone syntactically
    │   │   │   ├── **Reversibility:** Often reversible without grammatical change
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "John and Mary" ≈ "Mary and John"
    │   │   │   │   └── ⚠ Meaning may shift: "He came and saw" ≠ "He saw and came" (temporal order)
    │   │   │   └── **No Hierarchy:** Unlike subordination, no element is "main" or "dependent"
    │   │   └── **Contrast with Subordination:**
    │   │       ├── **Coordination:** [A] + [B] → Both A and B are equal
    │   │       └── **Subordination:** [A [B]] → B is embedded inside A (dependent)
    │   │
    │   ├── **2. Types of Coordination (By Connector Style)**
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(a) Syndetic Coordination (With explicit coordinator)**
    │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Uses a coordinating conjunction to join elements
    │   │   │   ├── **Simple Syndetic (Standard coordination)**
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Two elements:** "John **and** Mary"
    │   │   │   │   ├── **With list (serial/Oxford comma):** "apples, oranges**, and** bananas"
    │   │   │   │   └── **Position:** Coordinator appears before LAST element only
    │   │   │   └── **Polysyndetic (Multiple coordinators)**
    │   │   │       ├── **Definition:** Coordinator repeated before each element
    │   │   │       ├── **Examples:**
    │   │   │       │   ├── "John **and** Mary **and** Tom **and** Sue"
    │   │   │       │   ├── "He tried **and** tried **and** tried again."
    │   │   │       │   └── "Neither here **nor** there **nor** anywhere."
    │   │   │       ├── **Effect:** Emphasis, rhythm, dramatic buildup
    │   │   │       └── **Literary Use:** Common in formal/rhetorical prose
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(b) Asyndetic Coordination (Without coordinator)**
    │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Elements joined by comma, semicolon, or intonation (no conjunction)
    │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── **List:** "John, Mary, Tom" (implied 'and')
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Clause:** "He came, he saw, he conquered." (Veni, vidi, vici)
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Dramatic:** "I asked, I begged, I pleaded."
    │   │   │   │   └── **Descriptive:** "a tall, dark, handsome man"
    │   │   │   ├── **Effect:** Speed, urgency, dramatic impact, poetic rhythm
    │   │   │   ├── **Punctuation:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Commas:** For words/phrases in a list
    │   │   │   │   └── **Semicolons:** For clauses (esp. if clauses contain commas)
    │   │   │   └── **Note:** Final element often has implied coordinator
    │   │   │
    │   │   └── **(c) Correlative Coordination (Paired/Double coordinators)**
    │   │       ├── **Definition:** Two-part coordinators; first part signals second is coming
    │   │       ├── **Common Correlatives:**
    │   │       │   │
    │   │       │   ├── **both ... and** (Additive, emphatic inclusion)
    │   │       │   │   ├── "**Both** John **and** Mary came."
    │   │       │   │   ├── "She is **both** intelligent **and** hardworking."
    │   │       │   │   └── ⚠ Cannot use with negatives: ✘ "Both he and she didn't come"
    │   │       │   │
    │   │       │   ├── **either ... or** (Alternative/choice)
    │   │       │   │   ├── "**Either** you leave **or** I will."
    │   │       │   │   ├── "You can choose **either** tea **or** coffee."
    │   │       │   │   └── **Verb Agreement:** Verb agrees with NEARER subject
    │   │       │   │       └── "Either the cats or the dog **is** here." / "Either the dog or the cats **are** here."
    │   │       │   │
    │   │       │   ├── **neither ... nor** (Negative alternative)
    │   │       │   │   ├── "**Neither** John **nor** Mary attended."
    │   │       │   │   ├── "He **neither** smokes **nor** drinks."
    │   │       │   │   ├── **Verb Agreement:** Verb agrees with NEARER subject
    │   │       │   │   └── ⚠ Do NOT add extra negative: ✘ "He neither doesn't smoke nor drink"
    │   │       │   │
    │   │       │   ├── **not only ... but (also)** (Additive with emphasis/surprise)
    │   │       │   │   ├── "She is **not only** smart **but also** kind."
    │   │       │   │   ├── "He **not only** won **but** broke the record."
    │   │       │   │   ├── **Inversion (formal):** "Not only did he win, but he also broke the record."
    │   │       │   │   └── **Note:** "also" is optional but preferred
    │   │       │   │
    │   │       │   ├── **whether ... or** (Alternative in embedded questions/conditions)
    │   │       │   │   ├── "I don't know **whether** to stay **or** go."
    │   │       │   │   ├── "**Whether** you agree **or** disagree, it will happen."
    │   │       │   │   └── **Note:** Often used in noun clauses
    │   │       │   │
    │   │       │   ├── **not ... but** (Contrast/correction)
    │   │       │   │   ├── "It's **not** stupidity **but** laziness."
    │   │       │   │   └── "He came **not** to destroy **but** to build."
    │   │       │   │
    │   │       │   ├── **as ... as** (Comparison - equality)
    │   │       │   │   ├── "She is **as** tall **as** her brother."
    │   │       │   │   └── "Run **as** fast **as** you can."
    │   │       │   │
    │   │       │   └── **no sooner ... than / hardly ... when** (Temporal sequence)
    │   │       │       ├── "**No sooner** had he arrived **than** it started raining."
    │   │       │       ├── "**Hardly** had she spoken **when** he interrupted."
    │   │       │       └── **Inversion required:** "No sooner had I + past participle..."
    │   │       │
    │   │       └── **Parallelism Rule (CRITICAL):**
    │   │           ├── Elements after BOTH parts must be grammatically identical
    │   │           ├── ✔ "Both **smart** and **kind**" (Adj + Adj)
    │   │           ├── ✔ "Either **stay** or **leave**" (V + V)
    │   │           ├── ✘ "Both **smart** and **a kind person**" (Adj + NP = incorrect)
    │   │           └── ✘ "Not only **did he win** but also **his record**" (Clause + NP = incorrect)
    │   │
    │   ├── **3. Coordinating Conjunctions (Connectors)**
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(a) FANBOYS Mnemonic (7 Pure Coordinating Conjunctions)**
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **F - For** (Reason/Cause - Formal/Literary)
    │   │   │   │   ├── "He stayed home, **for** he was tired."
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Note:** More formal than "because"; rarely used in modern speech
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Punctuation:** MUST have comma before "for"
    │   │   │   │   └── ⚠ Do NOT confuse with preposition "for" (purpose)
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **A - And** (Addition/Sequence)
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Addition:** "John **and** Mary are friends."
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Sequence:** "He woke up **and** brushed his teeth."
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Result (informal):** "Study hard **and** you'll pass." (= If you study hard...)
    │   │   │   │   └── **Note:** Most common coordinator; neutral in meaning
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **N - Nor** (Negative addition)
    │   │   │   │   ├── "She doesn't smoke, **nor** does she drink."
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Inversion required:** Subject-auxiliary inversion after "nor"
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Alternative:** "She doesn't smoke **or** drink." (simpler form)
    │   │   │   │   └── ⚠ Requires negative in first clause (or "neither")
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **B - But** (Contrast/Concession)
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Contrast:** "He is poor **but** happy."
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Unexpected:** "She studied hard **but** failed."
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Exception:** "Everyone **but** John attended." (= except)
    │   │   │   │   └── **Note:** Signals opposition or unexpected outcome
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **O - Or** (Alternative/Choice)
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Exclusive:** "Is it right **or** wrong?" (one or the other, not both)
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Inclusive:** "Do you have tea **or** coffee?" (either is fine)
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Threat/Warning:** "Finish now, **or** you'll be late."
    │   │   │   │   └── **Correction:** "It's not red **or** rather, it's orange."
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **Y - Yet** (Contrast with surprise/concession)
    │   │   │   │   ├── "She is young **yet** wise."
    │   │   │   │   ├── "He worked hard, **yet** he failed."
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Difference from 'but':** 'Yet' implies more surprise/unexpectedness
    │   │   │   │   └── **Punctuation:** Comma before "yet" when joining clauses
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   └── **S - So** (Result/Consequence)
    │   │   │       ├── "It rained, **so** we stayed home."
    │   │   │       ├── "He was tired, **so** he went to bed."
    │   │   │       ├── **Punctuation:** Comma before "so" when joining clauses
    │   │   │       └── ⚠ "So" as intensifier is different: "She is **so** happy." (adverb)
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(b) Conjunctive Adverbs (Near-coordinators)**
    │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Adverbs that show logical connection between clauses
    │   │   │   ├── **Key Difference:** NOT true coordinators; require semicolon or period
    │   │   │   ├── **Common Conjunctive Adverbs:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Addition:** moreover, furthermore, besides, also, in addition
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Contrast:** however, nevertheless, nonetheless, on the other hand, instead
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Result:** therefore, thus, consequently, hence, accordingly, as a result
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Time:** then, meanwhile, subsequently, afterward, finally
    │   │   │   │   └── **Clarification:** namely, that is, in other words, for example
    │   │   │   └── **Punctuation Pattern:**
    │   │   │       ├── ✔ "He was tired**;** **however,** he continued."
    │   │   │       ├── ✔ "He was tired**. However,** he continued."
    │   │   │       └── ✘ "He was tired**, however,** he continued." (COMMA SPLICE ERROR)
    │   │   │
    │   │   └── **(c) Coordinator Properties Chart**
    │   │       │
    │   │       ├── **And:** Addition, Sequence, Result (informal)
    │   │       ├── **But:** Contrast, Unexpected outcome, Exception (= except)
    │   │       ├── **Or:** Choice, Alternative, Warning, Correction
    │   │       ├── **Nor:** Negative alternative (requires inversion)
    │   │       ├── **For:** Reason/Cause (formal, archaic)
    │   │       ├── **Yet:** Contrast with surprise (stronger than 'but')
    │   │       └── **So:** Result, Consequence
    │   │
    │   ├── **4. Semantic Functions (Meaning Relations)**
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(a) Addition/Conjunction**
    │   │   │   ├── **Coordinator:** and, both...and, not only...but also, as well as
    │   │   │   ├── **Meaning:** Combines elements additively
    │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "She sings **and** dances." (parallel activities)
    │   │   │   │   ├── "**Both** the cat **and** the dog are pets."
    │   │   │   │   └── "He is **not only** smart **but also** hardworking."
    │   │   │   └── **Subtypes:**
    │   │   │       ├── **Simple Addition:** "A and B"
    │   │   │       ├── **Emphatic Addition:** "both A and B"
    │   │   │       └── **Surprising Addition:** "not only A but also B"
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(b) Contrast/Adversative**
    │   │   │   ├── **Coordinator:** but, yet, however, nevertheless
    │   │   │   ├── **Meaning:** Signals opposition, contradiction, or unexpected continuation
    │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "He tried hard **but** failed." (unexpected result)
    │   │   │   │   ├── "She is young **yet** mature." (contradiction)
    │   │   │   │   └── "The task was difficult; **however,** she succeeded."
    │   │   │   └── **Subtypes:**
    │   │   │       ├── **Direct Opposition:** "but" (plain contrast)
    │   │   │       ├── **Concessive:** "yet" (despite expectation)
    │   │   │       └── **Correction:** "but rather" / "instead"
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(c) Alternative/Disjunction**
    │   │   │   ├── **Coordinator:** or, either...or, whether...or
    │   │   │   ├── **Meaning:** Presents choices or alternatives
    │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "Tea **or** coffee?" (choice)
    │   │   │   │   ├── "**Either** you stay **or** you leave." (exclusive choice)
    │   │   │   │   └── "**Whether** you agree **or** not, it's happening."
    │   │   │   └── **Subtypes:**
    │   │   │       ├── **Exclusive Or:** "A or B, but not both" (Is it true or false?)
    │   │   │       ├── **Inclusive Or:** "A or B, or both" (Do you want tea or coffee?)
    │   │   │       └── **Corrective Or:** "or rather" (He's tired, or rather, exhausted.)
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(d) Negative Alternative**
    │   │   │   ├── **Coordinator:** nor, neither...nor
    │   │   │   ├── **Meaning:** Negates both/all alternatives
    │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "He **neither** smokes **nor** drinks."
    │   │   │   │   ├── "She doesn't sing, **nor** does she dance."
    │   │   │   │   └── "**Neither** the cat **nor** the dog was found."
    │   │   │   └── **Grammar Note:** Inversion after "nor" when joining clauses
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(e) Result/Consequence**
    │   │   │   ├── **Coordinator:** so, therefore, consequently, thus
    │   │   │   ├── **Meaning:** Shows cause-effect relationship
    │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "It rained, **so** we stayed home."
    │   │   │   │   ├── "He was late; **therefore,** he missed the bus."
    │   │   │   │   └── "The road was icy, **consequently** many accidents occurred."
    │   │   │   └── **Note:** "So" is true coordinator; others are conjunctive adverbs
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(f) Cause/Reason (Explanation)**
    │   │   │   ├── **Coordinator:** for (formal/archaic)
    │   │   │   ├── **Meaning:** Explains why something happened
    │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "He stayed home, **for** he was ill."
    │   │   │   │   └── "She wept, **for** her heart was broken."
    │   │   │   └── **Note:** Rarely used in modern English; replaced by "because"
    │   │   │
    │   │   └── **(g) Sequence/Temporal**
    │   │       ├── **Coordinator:** and (with temporal meaning), then
    │   │       ├── **Meaning:** Shows order of events
    │   │       ├── **Examples:**
    │   │       │   ├── "He woke up **and** had breakfast." (= then)
    │   │       │   ├── "She entered **and** everyone stood up."
    │   │       │   └── "I'll finish this **and then** call you."
    │   │       └── **Note:** Order matters; not fully reversible
    │   │
    │   ├── **5. Syntactic Forms (What Can Be Coordinated)**
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(a) Clause Coordination (Compound Sentences)**
    │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Two or more independent clauses joined
    │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** [Independent Clause] + coordinator + [Independent Clause]
    │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "**I came**, **I saw**, **I conquered**." (asyndetic)
    │   │   │   │   ├── "**She likes coffee**, but **he prefers tea**." (syndetic)
    │   │   │   │   └── "**Either you apologize**, or **I'm leaving**." (correlative)
    │   │   │   ├── **Punctuation Rules:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── **With FANBOYS:** Comma before coordinator: "A, and B"
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Short clauses:** Comma optional: "I tried and I failed"
    │   │   │   │   └── **Conjunctive adverbs:** Semicolon required: "A; however, B"
    │   │   │   └── **Ellipsis (Gapping):**
    │   │   │       ├── Second clause may omit repeated elements
    │   │   │       ├── "John loves Mary, and Bill [loves] Susan."
    │   │   │       └── "She can sing and [she can] dance."
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(b) Phrase Coordination**
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **(i) Noun Phrase Coordination**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "**The cat** and **the dog** are friends."
    │   │   │   │   ├── "She bought **apples, oranges, and bananas**."
    │   │   │   │   └── "**Neither John nor Mary** knew the answer."
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **(ii) Verb Phrase Coordination**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "She can **sing** and **dance**."
    │   │   │   │   ├── "He **ran quickly** but **fell suddenly**."
    │   │   │   │   └── Shared Subject: "John [VP **ate dinner**] and [VP **watched TV**]."
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **(iii) Adjective Phrase Coordination**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "She is **tall** and **beautiful**."
    │   │   │   │   ├── "The room was **dark** but **warm**."
    │   │   │   │   └── "He is **neither happy nor sad**."
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **(iv) Adverb Phrase Coordination**
    │   │   │   │   ├── "She spoke **quickly** yet **clearly**."
    │   │   │   │   ├── "He ran **fast** and **furiously**."
    │   │   │   │   └── "Do it **carefully** but **quickly**."
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   └── **(v) Prepositional Phrase Coordination**
    │   │   │       ├── "The book is **on the table** or **under the chair**."
    │   │   │       ├── "She travels **by bus** and **by train**."
    │   │   │       └── "He works **in the office** and **at home**."
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(c) Word Coordination**
    │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Individual words of same category joined
    │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Nouns:** "cats **and** dogs" / "bread, butter**, and** jam"
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Verbs:** "run **and** jump" / "eat, drink**, and** be merry"
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Adjectives:** "hot **and** cold" / "tall**, dark, and** handsome"
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Adverbs:** "slowly **but** surely" / "here **and** there"
    │   │   │   │   └── **Prepositions:** "in **and** out" / "up **or** down"
    │   │   │   └── **Rule:** Words MUST be of the same grammatical category
    │   │   │
    │   │   └── **(d) Sentence Coordination**
    │   │       ├── **Definition:** Entire sentences linked (often by conjunctive adverbs)
    │   │       ├── **Examples:**
    │   │       │   ├── "The exam was hard. **However**, everyone passed."
    │   │       │   ├── "She prepared well. **Therefore**, she succeeded."
    │   │       │   └── "Rain is forecast. **Nevertheless**, we'll proceed."
    │   │       └── **Punctuation:** Period or semicolon between sentences
    │   │
    │   ├── **6. Grammatical Rules & Constraints**
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(a) Parallelism (CRITICAL EXAM TOPIC)**
    │   │   │   ├── **Rule:** Coordinated elements MUST be grammatically parallel
    │   │   │   ├── **Correct Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She likes **swimming** and **dancing**." (Gerund + Gerund)
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "He is **smart**, **kind**, and **honest**." (Adj + Adj + Adj)
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I came **to learn** and **to grow**." (Infinitive + Infinitive)
    │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "**Running daily** and **eating well** keep you healthy." (Gerund phrases)
    │   │   │   ├── **Incorrect Examples:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✘ "She likes **swimming** and **to run**." (Gerund + Infinitive)
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✘ "He is **smart**, **kind**, and **has honesty**." (Adj + Adj + Clause)
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✘ "I want **to eat** and **sleeping**." (Infinitive + Gerund)
    │   │   │   │   └── ✘ "Not only **he sings** but also **dance**." (Clause + Verb)
    │   │   │   └── **Fix Strategy:**
    │   │   │       ├── Identify the grammatical form of each element
    │   │   │       ├── Convert all elements to the same form
    │   │   │       └── Example: "swimming and to run" → "swimming and running" OR "to swim and to run"
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(b) Subject-Verb Agreement with Compound Subjects**
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **With "and":**
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Rule:** Compound subject takes PLURAL verb
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "John **and** Mary **are** coming."
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "The cat **and** the dog **play** together."
    │   │   │   │   ├── **EXCEPTION - Single entity:**
    │   │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Bread and butter **is** my breakfast." (= one item)
    │   │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "The rise and fall of Rome **was** dramatic." (= one concept)
    │   │   │   │   └── **EXCEPTION - Each/Every:**
    │   │   │   │       └── ✔ "**Every** cat and dog **was** rescued." (singular focus)
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **With "or" / "nor" / "either...or" / "neither...nor":**
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Rule:** Verb agrees with NEARER (closest) subject
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Either the cats or the **dog is** here." (dog = nearest, singular)
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Either the dog or the **cats are** here." (cats = nearest, plural)
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "Neither he nor **they are** guilty." (they = nearest, plural)
    │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "Neither they nor **he is** guilty." (he = nearest, singular)
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   └── **With "as well as" / "along with" / "together with":**
    │   │   │       ├── **Rule:** Verb agrees with FIRST subject only
    │   │   │       ├── ✔ "**John**, as well as his brothers, **is** coming." (John = singular)
    │   │   │       ├── ✔ "**The students**, along with the teacher, **are** leaving." (students = plural)
    │   │   │       └── **Note:** These are NOT true coordinators; they don't form compound subjects
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(c) Punctuation Rules for Coordination**
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **Comma Before Coordinator (Clauses):**
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "She sang**,** and he danced." (comma before 'and' with clauses)
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I tried**,** but I failed."
    │   │   │   │   └── **Optional for short clauses:** "I tried and I failed." (acceptable)
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **Serial/Oxford Comma (Lists):**
    │   │   │   │   ├── **With Oxford comma:** "A, B**,** and C"
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Without Oxford comma:** "A, B and C"
    │   │   │   │   ├── **Prevents ambiguity:**
    │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I love my parents, Batman and Superman." (= parents are Batman/Superman??)
    │   │   │   │   │   └── "I love my parents, Batman, and Superman." (= three separate entities ✔)
    │   │   │   │   └── **Recommendation:** Use Oxford comma consistently
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   ├── **No Comma with Phrases/Words:**
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "cats and dogs" (no comma needed)
    │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "tall and handsome" (no comma needed)
    │   │   │   │   └── ✘ "cats, and dogs" (incorrect for simple coordination)
    │   │   │   │
    │   │   │   └── **Semicolon with Conjunctive Adverbs:**
    │   │   │       ├── ✔ "He tried**;** however**,** he failed."
    │   │   │       ├── ✔ "She is smart**;** therefore**,** she succeeded."
    │   │   │       └── ✘ "He tried, however, he failed." (COMMA SPLICE - ERROR)
    │   │   │
    │   │   └── **(d) Position of Correlatives**
    │   │       ├── **Rule:** First correlative must come immediately before the first element
    │   │       ├── **Correct Placement:**
    │   │       │   ├── ✔ "She is **both smart** and **kind**." (both → adj, and → adj)
    │   │       │   ├── ✔ "He **not only sings** but also **dances**." (not only → verb, but also → verb)
    │   │       │   └── ✔ "**Either you leave** or **I will**." (either → clause, or → clause)
    │   │       └── **Incorrect Placement:**
    │   │           ├── ✘ "She **both** is **smart** and **kind**." (both before verb, not adjective)
    │   │           └── ✘ "He **not only** can sing but also **dance**." (misaligned elements)
    │   │
    │   ├── **7. Common Exam Traps & Errors**
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(a) Faulty Parallelism**
    │   │   │   ├── ✘ "She enjoys swimming, hiking, and **to bike**." → ✔ "...and biking."
    │   │   │   ├── ✘ "The job requires patience, skill, and **being persistent**." → ✔ "...and persistence."
    │   │   │   └── ✘ "I like **to read** and **writing**." → ✔ "I like reading and writing."
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(b) Misplaced Correlatives**
    │   │   │   ├── ✘ "She not only is **smart** but also **kind**." → ✔ "She is not only smart but also kind."
    │   │   │   ├── ✘ "He both plays **guitar** and **piano**." → ✔ "He plays both guitar and piano."
    │   │   │   └── ✘ "Either you must **go** or **stay**." → ✔ "You must either go or stay."
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(c) Subject-Verb Agreement Errors**
    │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Neither the students nor the teacher **were** present." (nearest = teacher = singular)
    │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "Neither the students nor the teacher **was** present."
    │   │   │   ├── ✘ "Bread and butter **are** served." (single breakfast item)
    │   │   │   │   └── ✔ "Bread and butter **is** served."
    │   │   │   └── ✘ "John, as well as his friends, **are** coming." (main subject = John = singular)
    │   │   │       └── ✔ "John, as well as his friends, **is** coming."
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(d) Comma Splice with Conjunctive Adverbs**
    │   │   │   ├── ✘ "He was tired, however, he continued." (comma splice)
    │   │   │   ├── ✔ "He was tired**;** however, he continued." (semicolon)
    │   │   │   └── ✔ "He was tired**. However,** he continued." (period)
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(e) Double Negative with "neither...nor"**
    │   │   │   ├── ✘ "He neither **doesn't** smoke nor drink." (double negative)
    │   │   │   ├── ✔ "He neither smokes nor drinks."
    │   │   │   └── ✘ "Neither John nor Mary **wasn't** there." → ✔ "...was there."
    │   │   │
    │   │   ├── **(f) Confusing "and/or" with "as well as"**
    │   │   │   ├── **"and" creates compound subject:** "John and Mary **are** here." (plural)
    │   │   │   └── **"as well as" does NOT:** "John, as well as Mary, **is** here." (singular)
    │   │   │
    │   │   └── **(g) Missing Elements in Coordination**
    │   │       ├── ✘ "She gave him the book and ." (incomplete)
    │   │       ├── ✘ "Neither John nor ." (incomplete)
    │   │       └── **Rule:** Both elements must be complete
    │   │
    │   └── **8. Competitive Exam Summary**
    │       │
    │       ├── **Key Concepts:**
    │       │   ├── **Coordination = Equal elements** joined by coordinator
    │       │   ├── **FANBOYS** = For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So
    │       │   ├── **Correlatives** = both...and, either...or, neither...nor, not only...but also
    │       │   └── **Conjunctive Adverbs** = however, therefore, moreover (NOT true coordinators)
    │       │
    │       ├── **Essential Rules:**
    │       │   ├── **(1) Parallelism:** Coordinated elements must be same grammatical form
    │       │   ├── **(2) Agreement with "or/nor":** Verb agrees with NEARER subject
    │       │   ├── **(3) Agreement with "and":** Compound subject → plural verb
    │       │   ├── **(4) "As well as":** Does NOT create compound subject
    │       │   └── **(5) Correlative Position:** First part must be immediately before first element
    │       │
    │       ├── **Punctuation Summary:**
    │       │   ├── **Clauses + FANBOYS:** Comma before coordinator
    │       │   ├── **Phrases/Words:** No comma needed
    │       │   ├── **Conjunctive Adverbs:** Semicolon or period required
    │       │   └── **Oxford Comma:** Recommended for clarity in lists
    │       │
    │       └── **Quick Tests:**
    │           ├── **Parallelism Test:** Can each element stand alone in the same position?
    │           ├── **Agreement Test:** Which subject is nearest to the verb (for or/nor)?
    │           └── **Correlative Test:** Is the first correlative directly before its element?
    │
    └── **B. Subordination (Joining unequal units: one dependent clause embedded in main)**
        │
        ├── **1. Definition & Core Concept**
        │   ├── **Definition:** Subordination embeds one clause (dependent) within another (main/matrix)
        │   │   └── The embedded clause CANNOT stand alone as a complete sentence
        │   ├── **Key Properties:**
        │   │   ├── **Asymmetry:** One clause is "main" (independent), one is "subordinate" (dependent)
        │   │   ├── **Embedding:** Subordinate clause is contained WITHIN the main clause structure
        │   │   ├── **Dependency:** Subordinate clause depends on main clause for complete meaning
        │   │   │   ├── ✔ Main alone: "He left." (complete)
        │   │   │   ├── ✘ Subordinate alone: "Because he was tired." (incomplete - fragment)
        │   │   │   └── ✔ Together: "He left because he was tired." (complete)
        │   │   └── **Hierarchy:** Main clause is structurally superior; subordinate is embedded
        │   ├── **Terminology:**
        │   │   ├── **Main Clause** = Independent Clause = Matrix Clause = Superordinate Clause
        │   │   ├── **Subordinate Clause** = Dependent Clause = Embedded Clause
        │   │   └── **Subordinator** = Word that introduces subordinate clause
        │   └── **Contrast with Coordination:**
        │       ├── **Subordination:** [Main [Subordinate]] → Hierarchy (one inside other)
        │       │   └── "I know **[that he left]**." (subordinate = object of 'know')
        │       ├── **Coordination:** [A] + [B] → Equality (side by side)
        │       │   └── "[I came] and [I saw]." (both independent)
        │       └── **Key Difference:** Subordinate clauses have grammatical FUNCTIONS (subject, object, modifier)
        │
        ├── **2. Types of Subordinate Clauses (By Syntactic Function)**
        │   │
        │   ├── **(a) Nominal (Noun) Clauses** - Function as nouns (S, O, C, or Appositive)
        │   │   │
        │   │   ├── **(i) That-Clauses (Declarative Content Clauses)**
        │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** that + S + V (+ complements)
        │   │   │   ├── **Meaning:** Reports/states a proposition (fact, belief, thought)
        │   │   │   ├── **Functions:**
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Subject:** "**That he lied** is obvious."
        │   │   │   │   │   └── Often extraposed: "It is obvious **that he lied**."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Direct Object:** "I believe **that she is honest**."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Subject Complement:** "The truth is **that he left**."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Appositive:** "The fact **that he lied** shocked us."
        │   │   │   │   └── **Adjective Complement:** "I'm happy **that you came**."
        │   │   │   ├── **That-Deletion (Optional omission):**
        │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I think **(that)** he is right." (object position - can omit)
        │   │   │   │   ├── ✔ "I know **(that)** she left." (informal)
        │   │   │   │   ├── ✘ "**That** he lied is obvious." (subject - CANNOT omit)
        │   │   │   │   └── ⚠ Formal writing: Keep "that" for clarity
        │   │   │   └── **Verbs Taking That-Clauses:**
        │   │   │       ├── **Thinking:** think, believe, know, realize, understand, suppose, imagine
        │   │   │       ├── **Saying:** say, tell, mention, report, claim, announce, state
        │   │   │       ├── **Feeling:** hope, wish, fear, regret, prefer
        │   │   │       └── **Perception:** see, notice, observe, feel, sense
        │   │   │
        │   │   ├── **(ii) Wh-Clauses (Interrogative/Exclamative Content Clauses)**
        │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** wh-word + S + V (+ complements) [NOTE: NOT inverted]
        │   │   │   ├── **Wh-words:** what, who, whom, whose, which, where, when, why, how
        │   │   │   ├── **Types:**
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Embedded Questions (Indirect Questions):**
        │   │   │   │   │   ├── "I wonder **what he wants**." (NOT: what does he want)
        │   │   │   │   │   ├── "Do you know **where she lives**?"
        │   │   │   │   │   ├── "Tell me **why you left**."
        │   │   │   │   │   └── **Word Order:** Subject before verb (unlike direct questions)
        │   │   │   │   └── **Free Relatives (Nominal Relatives):**
        │   │   │   │       ├── "**What you said** was wrong." (= The thing that you said)
        │   │   │   │       ├── "I'll eat **whatever you cook**." (= anything that)
        │   │   │   │       └── "Go **wherever you want**." (= to any place that)
        │   │   │   ├── **Functions:**
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Subject:** "**Who did it** remains unknown."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Direct Object:** "I know **what you mean**."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Subject Complement:** "The question is **where to go**."
        │   │   │   │   └── **Prepositional Object:** "I'm curious about **what happened**."
        │   │   │   └── **⚠ Common Error - Inversion in Embedded Questions:**
        │   │   │       ├── ✘ "I wonder what **does he** want." (inverted - WRONG)
        │   │   │       ├── ✔ "I wonder what **he wants**." (normal order - CORRECT)
        │   │   │       ├── ✘ "Tell me where **is she**." (inverted - WRONG)
        │   │   │       └── ✔ "Tell me where **she is**." (normal order - CORRECT)
        │   │   │
        │   │   ├── **(iii) If/Whether-Clauses (Yes-No Embedded Questions)**
        │   │   │   ├── **Structure:** if/whether + S + V (+ complements)
        │   │   │   ├── **Meaning:** Embedded yes/no question (expresses uncertainty)
        │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "I wonder **if/whether he is coming**."
        │   │   │   │   ├── "Ask him **whether he agrees**."
        │   │   │   │   └── "I don't know **if she saw me**."
        │   │   │   ├── **If vs. Whether:**
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Interchangeable (most contexts):** "I wonder if/whether he'll come."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **"Whether" preferred:**
        │   │   │   │   │   ├── After prepositions: "I'm uncertain about **whether** to go."
        │   │   │   │   │   ├── With "or not" immediately after: "**Whether or not** he comes..."
        │   │   │   │   │   ├── As subject: "**Whether** he agrees is unclear."
        │   │   │   │   │   └── In formal writing
        │   │   │   │   └── **"If" NOT used:**
        │   │   │   │       ├── ✘ "I'm unsure about **if** to go." → ✔ "...about **whether** to go."
        │   │   │   │       └── ✘ "**If or not** he comes..." → ✔ "**Whether or not**..."
        │   │   │   └── **"Whether...or" (Alternative):**
        │   │   │       ├── "I don't know **whether to stay or leave**."
        │   │   │       └── "Tell me **whether you agree or disagree**."
        │   │   │
        │   │   └── **(iv) Infinitive Noun Clauses (Non-finite Nominal Clauses)**
        │   │       ├── **Structure:** (for + NP) + to + V
        │   │       ├── **Examples:**
        │   │       │   ├── **Subject:** "**To err** is human." / "**For him to leave** would be wrong."
        │   │       │   ├── **Object:** "I want **to leave**." / "She decided **to stay**."
        │   │       │   ├── **Complement:** "Her goal is **to win**."
        │   │       │   └── **Extraposed:** "It is important **to study**."
        │   │       └── **Wh-infinitives:**
        │   │           ├── "I don't know **what to do**."
        │   │           ├── "Tell me **where to go**."
        │   │           └── "She showed me **how to cook**."
        │   │
        │   ├── **(b) Adjectival (Relative) Clauses** - Modify nouns/pronouns
        │   │   │
        │   │   ├── **(i) Restrictive (Defining) Relative Clauses**
        │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Essential information that IDENTIFIES which noun is meant
        │   │   │   ├── **Punctuation:** NO commas (integral to NP)
        │   │   │   ├── **Meaning:** Limits/specifies the reference of the noun
        │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The book **that I read** was excellent." (Which book? The one I read.)
        │   │   │   │   ├── "Students **who study hard** succeed." (Which students? Those who study hard.)
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The person **whom you met** is my boss." (Which person? The one you met.)
        │   │   │   │   └── "The house **where I grew up** was demolished." (Which house? That one.)
        │   │   │   ├── **Relative Pronouns for Restrictive:**
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Who:** Subject (people): "The man **who called**..."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Whom:** Object (people, formal): "The man **whom I saw**..."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Which:** Subject/Object (things): "The book **which I read**..."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **That:** Subject/Object (people/things): "The man/book **that I saw**..."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Whose:** Possessive (people/things): "The man **whose car** broke..."
        │   │   │   │   └── **Zero/Ø:** Object position (omittable): "The book **Ø I read**..."
        │   │   │   └── **"That" vs. "Which/Who" in Restrictive:**
        │   │   │       ├── "That" is preferred in restrictive clauses (especially AmE)
        │   │   │       ├── ✔ "The book **that** I need is missing."
        │   │   │       └── ✔ "The person **that** called just left."
        │   │   │
        │   │   ├── **(ii) Non-Restrictive (Non-Defining) Relative Clauses**
        │   │   │   ├── **Definition:** Additional information; NOT needed to identify noun
        │   │   │   ├── **Punctuation:** COMMAS required (parenthetical)
        │   │   │   ├── **Meaning:** Adds extra info about an already-identified noun
        │   │   │   ├── **Examples:**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "My brother**,** **who lives in Paris,** is visiting." (I have one brother.)
        │   │   │   │   ├── "Paris**,** **which is beautiful,** attracts tourists." (Paris already identified)
        │   │   │   │   ├── "Dr. Smith**,** **whom I admire,** gave a lecture." (Specific person)
        │   │   │   │   └── "The Eiffel Tower**,** **where we met,** was crowded." (Unique landmark)
        │   │   │   ├── **Relative Pronouns for Non-Restrictive:**
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Who:** Subject (people): "John, **who is tall,**..."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Whom:** Object (people): "John, **whom I met,**..."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Which:** Subject/Object (things): "Paris, **which I love,**..."
        │   │   │   │   ├── **Whose:** Possessive: "John, **whose car** broke,..."
        │   │   │   │   ├── ⚠ **CANNOT use "that"**: ✘ "Paris, **that** is beautiful,..."
        │   │   │   │   └── ⚠ **CANNOT omit pronoun**: ✘ "John, **Ø** I met,..."
        │   │   │   └── **Sentential Relatives (Refers to whole clause):**
        │   │   │       ├── "He passed the exam, **which surprised everyone**."
        │   │   │       │   └── "which" = the fact that he passed (not the exam)
        │   │   │       └── "She arrived late, **which annoyed me**."
        │   │   │
        │   │   ├── **(iii) Relative Pronouns - Complete Guide**
        │   │   │   │
        │   │   │   ├── **Who (Subject - People)**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The woman **who called** is my aunt."
        │   │   │   │   ├── "Students **who work hard** succeed."
        │   │   │   │   └── Can use "that" in restrictive: "The woman **that** called..."
        │   │   │   │
        │   │   │   ├── **Whom (Object - People, Formal)**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The man **whom I met** was kind."
        │   │   │   │   ├── "She is someone **whom I admire**."
        │   │   │   │   ├── After preposition: "The person **to whom** I spoke..."
        │   │   │   │   └── Informal: "who" often replaces "whom" (but not after prepositions)
        │   │   │   │
        │   │   │   ├── **Which (Things - Subject/Object)**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The book **which fell** is mine." (subject)
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The book **which I read** was good." (object)
        │   │   │   │   └── Non-restrictive only: "Paris, **which** I love,..."
        │   │   │   │
        │   │   │   ├── **That (People/Things - Restrictive Only)**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The man **that** called left." (people)
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The book **that** I read was good." (things)
        │   │   │   │   ├── ⚠ CANNOT use in non-restrictive: ✘ "Paris, **that** I love,..."
        │   │   │   │   └── Often preferred over "which" in restrictive clauses
        │   │   │   │
        │   │   │   ├── **Whose (Possessive - People/Things)**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The man **whose car** broke down..."
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The house **whose roof** collapsed..."
        │   │   │   │   └── Alternative for things: "The house **the roof of which**..." (formal)
        │   │   │   │
        │   │   │   └── **Zero Relative / Contact Clause (Omission)**
        │   │   │       ├── **When to Omit:** Object position in restrictive clauses
        │   │   │       ├── ✔ "The book **Ø I read** was good." (object - can omit)
        │   │   │       ├── ✔ "The man **Ø she married** is rich." (object - can omit)
        │   │   │       ├── ✘ "The man **Ø called** left." (subject - CANNOT omit)
        │   │   │       └── ✘ "Paris, **Ø I love**, is beautiful." (non-restrictive - CANNOT omit)
        │   │   │
        │   │   ├── **(iv) Relative Adverbs**
        │   │   │   ├── **Where (Place):**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The city **where I was born**..." (= in which)
        │   │   │   │   └── "This is the house **where Shakespeare lived**."
        │   │   │   ├── **When (Time):**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The day **when we met**..." (= on which)
        │   │   │   │   └── "I remember the time **when dinosaurs roamed**." (figurative)
        │   │   │   ├── **Why (Reason):**
        │   │   │   │   ├── "The reason **why he left**..." (= for which)
        │   │   │   │   └── "That's **why** I'm here." (often without antecedent)
        │   │   │   └── **Note:** In informal English, often replaced by "that" or omitted:
        │   │   │       ├── "The day **(when/that/Ø)** we met..."
        │   │   │       └── "The reason **(why/that/Ø)** he left..."
        │   │   │
        │   │   └── **(v) Reduced Relative Clauses (Participle Phrases)**
        │   │       ├── **Present Participle (-ing) - Active meaning:**
        │   │       │   ├── "The man **who is standing** there..." → "The man **standing** there..."
        │   │       │   ├── "Students **who are studying** hard..." → "Students **studying** hard..."
        │   │       │   └── Reduces: who/which + be + -ing
        │   │       ├── **Past Participle (-ed) - Passive meaning:**
        │   │       │   ├── "The book **which was written** by her..." → "The book **written** by her..."
        │   │       │   ├── "The car **that was stolen**..." → "The car **stolen**..."
        │   │       │   └── Reduces: which/that + be + past participle
        │   │       └── **Rules for Reduction:**
        │   │           ├── Only when relative pronoun is SUBJECT of clause
        │   │           ├── Delete relative pronoun + auxiliary "be"
        │   │           └── Keep the participle
        │   │
        │   └── **(c) Adverbial Clauses** - Modify verbs, clauses, or sentences
        │       │
        │       ├── **(i) Time (Temporal) Clauses**
        │       │   ├── **Subordinators:**
        │       │   │   ├── **Sequence:** when, before, after, until, till, since, once
        │       │   │   ├── **Duration:** while, as, as long as
        │       │   │   ├── **Point in time:** as soon as, the moment (that), the instant (that)
        │       │   │   └── **Frequency:** whenever, every time (that)
        │       │   ├── **Examples:**
        │       │   │   ├── "I left **when he arrived**."
        │       │   │   ├── "Read this **before you go**."
        │       │   │   ├── "**After she finished**, she left."
        │       │   │   ├── "Wait **until I return**."
        │       │   │   ├── "**While I was sleeping**, the phone rang."
        │       │   │   ├── "**As soon as** I heard, I called."
        │       │   │   └── "I've worked here **since I graduated**."
        │       │   └── **Tense Rule (Future Time):**
        │       │       ├── ⚠ Use PRESENT tense for future in time clauses
        │       │       ├── ✔ "When he **arrives**, I'll leave." (NOT: when he will arrive)
        │       │       ├── ✔ "I'll wait until she **comes**." (NOT: until she will come)
        │       │       └── ✔ "Before you **go**, call me." (NOT: before you will go)
        │       │
        │       ├── **(ii) Reason/Cause Clauses**
        │       │   ├── **Subordinators:** because, since, as, for, now that, seeing that, given that
        │       │   ├── **Examples:**
        │       │   │   ├── "He stayed home **because he was sick**."
        │       │   │   ├── "**Since you asked**, I'll tell you."
        │       │   │   ├── "**As it was raining**, we stayed inside."
        │       │   │   └── "**Now that you're here**, let's begin."
        │       │   ├── **Differences:**
        │       │   │   ├── **Because:** Strongest; gives the main reason; often final position
        │       │   │   ├── **Since/As:** Weaker; often initial; reason is already known
        │       │   │   └── **For:** Very formal/literary; always final; rarely used
        │       │   └── **Position:**
        │       │       ├── "Because" clauses usually final: "I left **because I was tired**."
        │       │       └── "Since/As" clauses often initial: "**Since you know**, tell me."
        │       │
        │       ├── **(iii) Purpose Clauses**
        │       │   ├── **Subordinators:**
        │       │   │   ├── **Positive:** so that, in order that, in order to, so as to
        │       │   │   └── **Negative:** so that...not, lest (formal), for fear (that)
        │       │   ├── **Examples:**
        │       │   │   ├── "I study hard **so that I can pass**."
        │       │   │   ├── "She left early **in order to catch** the train."
        │       │   │   ├── "He whispered **so as not to wake** the baby."
        │       │   │   ├── "Be careful **lest you fall**." (formal/archaic)
        │       │   │   └── "She hurried **for fear that she would be late**."
        │       │   ├── **Modal Verbs in Purpose Clauses:**
        │       │   │   ├── Often use: can, could, may, might, will, would
        │       │   │   └── "I'll leave early so that I **can** be on time."
        │       │   └── **Infinitive Alternative:**
        │       │       ├── "I study hard **to pass**." (= so that I can pass)
        │       │       └── Same subject: infinitive preferred over full clause
        │       │
        │       ├── **(iv) Result Clauses**
        │       │   ├── **Subordinators:** so...that, such...that, so that (result)
        │       │   ├── **Structure:**
        │       │   │   ├── **so + Adj/Adv + that:** "She is **so smart that** she won."
        │       │   │   ├── **such + (a/an) + Noun + that:** "It was **such a good book that** I read it twice."
        │       │   │   └── **so + many/much/few/little + N + that:** "He has **so much money that**..."
        │       │   ├── **Examples:**
        │       │   │   ├── "He ran **so fast that** he won the race."
        │       │   │   ├── "It was **such terrible weather that** we stayed home."
        │       │   │   ├── "She spoke **so quietly that** I couldn't hear."
        │       │   │   └── "There were **so many people that** we couldn't enter."
        │       │   └── **so + adj + a(n) + N + that (formal):**
        │       │       └── "He is **so good a player that**..." (= such a good player that)
        │       │
        │       ├── **(v) Conditional Clauses (If-Clauses)**
        │       │   ├── **Subordinators:** if, unless, provided (that), providing (that), as long as, on condition that, supposing (that), in case
        │       │   ├── **Types:**
        │       │   │   ├── **Type 0 (General Truth/Habit):**
        │       │   │   │   ├── If + present, present
        │       │   │   │   └── "**If** water boils, it **evaporates**."
        │       │   │   ├── **Type 1 (Real/Possible Future):**
        │       │   │   │   ├── If + present, will + infinitive
        │       │   │   │   └── "**If** it rains, I **will stay** home."
        │       │   │   ├── **Type 2 (Unreal Present/Hypothetical):**
        │       │   │   │   ├── If + past, would + infinitive
